"208" 1998 "     Notes on \"Thus Spake Zarathustra\" by Anthony M. Ludovici."

"212" 1998 ""
"213" 1998 "INTRODUCTION BY MRS FORSTER-NIETZSCHE."
"214" 1998 ""
"215" 1998 "HOW ZARATHUSTRA CAME INTO BEING."
"216" 1998 ""
"217" 1998 "\"Zarathustra\" is my brother's most personal work; it is the history of"
"218" 1998 "his most individual experiences, of his friendships, ideals, raptures,"
"219" 1998 "bitterest disappointments and sorrows. Above it all, however, there"
"220" 1998 "soars, transfiguring it, the image of his greatest hopes and remotest"
"221" 1998 "aims. My brother had the figure of Zarathustra in his mind from his very"
"222" 1998 "earliest youth: he once told me that even as a child he had dreamt of"
"223" 1998 "him. At different periods in his life, he would call this haunter of his"
"224" 1998 "dreams by different names; \"but in the end,\" he declares in a note on"
"225" 1998 "the subject, \"I had to do a PERSIAN the honour of identifying him with"
"226" 1998 "this creature of my fancy. Persians were the first to take a broad and"
"227" 1998 "comprehensive view of history. Every series of evolutions, according"
"228" 1998 "to them, was presided over by a prophet; and every prophet had his"
"229" 1998 "'Hazar,'--his dynasty of a thousand years.\""
"230" 1998 ""
"231" 1998 "All Zarathustra's views, as also his personality, were early conceptions"
"232" 1998 "of my brother's mind. Whoever reads his posthumously published writings"
"233" 1998 "for the years 1869-82 with care, will constantly meet with passages"
"234" 1998 "suggestive of Zarathustra's thoughts and doctrines. For instance, the"
"235" 1998 "ideal of the Superman is put forth quite clearly in all his writings"
"236" 1998 "during the years 1873-75; and in \"We Philologists\", the following"
"237" 1998 "remarkable observations occur:--"
"238" 1998 ""
"239" 1998 "\"How can one praise and glorify a nation as a whole?--Even among the"
"240" 1998 "Greeks, it was the INDIVIDUALS that counted.\""
"241" 1998 ""
"242" 1998 "\"The Greeks are interesting and extremely important because they reared"
"243" 1998 "such a vast number of great individuals. How was this possible? The"
"244" 1998 "question is one which ought to be studied."
"245" 1998 ""
"246" 1998 "\"I am interested only in the relations of a people to the rearing of"
"247" 1998 "the individual man, and among the Greeks the conditions were unusually"
"248" 1998 "favourable for the development of the individual; not by any means owing"
"249" 1998 "to the goodness of the people, but because of the struggles of their"
"250" 1998 "evil instincts."
"251" 1998 ""
"252" 1998 "\"WITH THE HELP OF FAVOURABLE MEASURES GREAT INDIVIDUALS MIGHT BE REARED"
"253" 1998 "WHO WOULD BE BOTH DIFFERENT FROM AND HIGHER THAN THOSE WHO HERETOFORE"
"254" 1998 "HAVE OWED THEIR EXISTENCE TO MERE CHANCE. Here we may still be hopeful:"
"255" 1998 "in the rearing of exceptional men.\""
"256" 1998 ""
"257" 1998 "The notion of rearing the Superman is only a new form of an ideal"
"258" 1998 "Nietzsche already had in his youth, that \"THE OBJECT OF MANKIND SHOULD"
"259" 1998 "LIE IN ITS HIGHEST INDIVIDUALS\" (or, as he writes in \"Schopenhauer as"
"260" 1998 "Educator\": \"Mankind ought constantly to be striving to produce great"
"261" 1998 "men--this and nothing else is its duty.\") But the ideals he most revered"
"262" 1998 "in those days are no longer held to be the highest types of men. No,"
"263" 1998 "around this future ideal of a coming humanity--the Superman--the poet"
"264" 1998 "spread the veil of becoming. Who can tell to what glorious heights man"
"265" 1998 "can still ascend? That is why, after having tested the worth of our"
"266" 1998 "noblest ideal--that of the Saviour, in the light of the new valuations,"
"267" 1998 "the poet cries with passionate emphasis in \"Zarathustra\":"
"268" 1998 ""
"269" 1998 "\"Never yet hath there been a Superman. Naked have I seen both of them,"
"270" 1998 "the greatest and the smallest man:--"
"271" 1998 ""
"272" 1998 "All-too-similar are they still to each other. Verily even the greatest"
"273" 1998 "found I--all-too-human!\"--"
"274" 1998 ""
"275" 1998 "The phrase \"the rearing of the Superman,\" has very often been"
"276" 1998 "misunderstood. By the word \"rearing,\" in this case, is meant the act of"
"277" 1998 "modifying by means of new and higher values--values which, as laws and"
"278" 1998 "guides of conduct and opinion, are now to rule over mankind. In general"
"279" 1998 "the doctrine of the Superman can only be understood correctly in"
"280" 1998 "conjunction with other ideas of the author's, such as:--the Order"
"281" 1998 "of Rank, the Will to Power, and the Transvaluation of all Values. He"
"282" 1998 "assumes that Christianity, as a product of the resentment of the botched"
"283" 1998 "and the weak, has put in ban all that is beautiful, strong, proud, and"
"284" 1998 "powerful, in fact all the qualities resulting from strength, and that,"
"285" 1998 "in consequence, all forces which tend to promote or elevate life have"
"286" 1998 "been seriously undermined. Now, however, a new table of valuations"
"287" 1998 "must be placed over mankind--namely, that of the strong, mighty, and"
"288" 1998 "magnificent man, overflowing with life and elevated to his zenith--the"
"289" 1998 "Superman, who is now put before us with overpowering passion as the"
"290" 1998 "aim of our life, hope, and will. And just as the old system of valuing,"
"291" 1998 "which only extolled the qualities favourable to the weak, the suffering,"
"292" 1998 "and the oppressed, has succeeded in producing a weak, suffering, and"
"293" 1998 "\"modern\" race, so this new and reversed system of valuing ought to rear"
"294" 1998 "a healthy, strong, lively, and courageous type, which would be a glory"
"295" 1998 "to life itself. Stated briefly, the leading principle of this new system"
"296" 1998 "of valuing would be: \"All that proceeds from power is good, all that"
"297" 1998 "springs from weakness is bad.\""
"298" 1998 ""
"299" 1998 "This type must not be regarded as a fanciful figure: it is not a"
"300" 1998 "nebulous hope which is to be realised at some indefinitely remote"
"301" 1998 "period, thousands of years hence; nor is it a new species (in the"
"302" 1998 "Darwinian sense) of which we can know nothing, and which it would"
"303" 1998 "therefore be somewhat absurd to strive after. But it is meant to be"
"304" 1998 "a possibility which men of the present could realise with all their"
"305" 1998 "spiritual and physical energies, provided they adopted the new values."
"306" 1998 ""
"307" 1998 "The author of \"Zarathustra\" never lost sight of that egregious example"
"308" 1998 "of a transvaluation of all values through Christianity, whereby the"
"309" 1998 "whole of the deified mode of life and thought of the Greeks, as well as"
"310" 1998 "strong Romedom, was almost annihilated or transvalued in a comparatively"
"311" 1998 "short time. Could not a rejuvenated Graeco-Roman system of valuing (once"
"312" 1998 "it had been refined and made more profound by the schooling which"
"313" 1998 "two thousand years of Christianity had provided) effect another such"
"314" 1998 "revolution within a calculable period of time, until that glorious type"
"315" 1998 "of manhood shall finally appear which is to be our new faith and hope,"
"316" 1998 "and in the creation of which Zarathustra exhorts us to participate?"
"317" 1998 ""
"318" 1998 "In his private notes on the subject the author uses the expression"
"319" 1998 "\"Superman\" (always in the singular, by-the-bye), as signifying \"the most"
"320" 1998 "thoroughly well-constituted type,\" as opposed to \"modern man\"; above"
"321" 1998 "all, however, he designates Zarathustra himself as an example of the"
"322" 1998 "Superman. In \"Ecco Homo\" he is careful to enlighten us concerning the"
"323" 1998 "precursors and prerequisites to the advent of this highest type, in"
"324" 1998 "referring to a certain passage in the \"Gay Science\":--"
"325" 1998 ""
"326" 1998 "\"In order to understand this type, we must first be quite clear in"
"327" 1998 "regard to the leading physiological condition on which it depends: this"
"328" 1998 "condition is what I call GREAT HEALTHINESS. I know not how to express my"
"329" 1998 "meaning more plainly or more personally than I have done already in"
"330" 1998 "one of the last chapters (Aphorism 382) of the fifth book of the 'Gaya"
"331" 1998 "Scienza'.\""
"332" 1998 ""
"333" 1998 "\"We, the new, the nameless, the hard-to-understand,\"--it says"
"334" 1998 "there,--\"we firstlings of a yet untried future--we require for a new end"
"335" 1998 "also a new means, namely, a new healthiness, stronger, sharper, tougher,"
"336" 1998 "bolder and merrier than all healthiness hitherto. He whose soul"
"337" 1998 "longeth to experience the whole range of hitherto recognised values"
"338" 1998 "and desirabilities, and to circumnavigate all the coasts of this ideal"
"339" 1998 "'Mediterranean Sea', who, from the adventures of his most personal"
"340" 1998 "experience, wants to know how it feels to be a conqueror, and discoverer"
"341" 1998 "of the ideal--as likewise how it is with the artist, the saint, the"
"342" 1998 "legislator, the sage, the scholar, the devotee, the prophet, and the"
"343" 1998 "godly non-conformist of the old style:--requires one thing above all"
"344" 1998 "for that purpose, GREAT HEALTHINESS--such healthiness as one not only"
"345" 1998 "possesses, but also constantly acquires and must acquire, because one"
"346" 1998 "unceasingly sacrifices it again, and must sacrifice it!--And now, after"
"347" 1998 "having been long on the way in this fashion, we Argonauts of the ideal,"
"348" 1998 "more courageous perhaps than prudent, and often enough shipwrecked"
"349" 1998 "and brought to grief, nevertheless dangerously healthy, always healthy"
"350" 1998 "again,--it would seem as if, in recompense for it all, that we have a"
"351" 1998 "still undiscovered country before us, the boundaries of which no one"
"352" 1998 "has yet seen, a beyond to all countries and corners of the ideal known"
"353" 1998 "hitherto, a world so over-rich in the beautiful, the strange, the"
"354" 1998 "questionable, the frightful, and the divine, that our curiosity as well"
"355" 1998 "as our thirst for possession thereof, have got out of hand--alas! that"
"356" 1998 "nothing will now any longer satisfy us!--"
"357" 1998 ""
"358" 1998 "\"How could we still be content with THE MAN OF THE PRESENT DAY"
"359" 1998 "after such outlooks, and with such a craving in our conscience and"
"360" 1998 "consciousness? Sad enough; but it is unavoidable that we should look"
"361" 1998 "on the worthiest aims and hopes of the man of the present day with"
"362" 1998 "ill-concealed amusement, and perhaps should no longer look at them."
"363" 1998 "Another ideal runs on before us, a strange, tempting ideal full of"
"364" 1998 "danger, to which we should not like to persuade any one, because we"
"365" 1998 "do not so readily acknowledge any one's RIGHT THERETO: the ideal of"
"366" 1998 "a spirit who plays naively (that is to say involuntarily and from"
"367" 1998 "overflowing abundance and power) with everything that has hitherto"
"368" 1998 "been called holy, good, intangible, or divine; to whom the loftiest"
"369" 1998 "conception which the people have reasonably made their measure of value,"
"370" 1998 "would already practically imply danger, ruin, abasement, or at least"
"371" 1998 "relaxation, blindness, or temporary self-forgetfulness; the ideal of"
"372" 1998 "a humanly superhuman welfare and benevolence, which will often enough"
"373" 1998 "appear INHUMAN, for example, when put alongside of all past seriousness"
"374" 1998 "on earth, and alongside of all past solemnities in bearing, word, tone,"
"375" 1998 "look, morality, and pursuit, as their truest involuntary parody--and"
"376" 1998 "WITH which, nevertheless, perhaps THE GREAT SERIOUSNESS only commences,"
"377" 1998 "when the proper interrogative mark is set up, the fate of the soul"
"378" 1998 "changes, the hour-hand moves, and tragedy begins...\""
"379" 1998 ""
"380" 1998 "Although the figure of Zarathustra and a large number of the leading"
"381" 1998 "thoughts in this work had appeared much earlier in the dreams and"
"382" 1998 "writings of the author, \"Thus Spake Zarathustra\" did not actually come"
"383" 1998 "into being until the month of August 1881 in Sils Maria; and it was the"
"384" 1998 "idea of the Eternal Recurrence of all things which finally induced my"
"385" 1998 "brother to set forth his new views in poetic language. In regard to his"
"386" 1998 "first conception of this idea, his autobiographical sketch, \"Ecce Homo\","
"387" 1998 "written in the autumn of 1888, contains the following passage:--"
"388" 1998 ""
"389" 1998 "\"The fundamental idea of my work--namely, the Eternal Recurrence of"
"390" 1998 "all things--this highest of all possible formulae of a Yea-saying"
"391" 1998 "philosophy, first occurred to me in August 1881. I made a note of the"
"392" 1998 "thought on a sheet of paper, with the postscript: 6,000 feet beyond"
"393" 1998 "men and time! That day I happened to be wandering through the woods"
"394" 1998 "alongside of the lake of Silvaplana, and I halted beside a huge,"
"395" 1998 "pyramidal and towering rock not far from Surlei. It was then that the"
"396" 1998 "thought struck me. Looking back now, I find that exactly two months"
"397" 1998 "previous to this inspiration, I had had an omen of its coming in the"
"398" 1998 "form of a sudden and decisive alteration in my tastes--more particularly"
"399" 1998 "in music. It would even be possible to consider all 'Zarathustra' as a"
"400" 1998 "musical composition. At all events, a very necessary condition in its"
"401" 1998 "production was a renaissance in myself of the art of hearing. In a small"
"402" 1998 "mountain resort (Recoaro) near Vicenza, where I spent the spring of"
"403" 1998 "1881, I and my friend and Maestro, Peter Gast--also one who had been"
"404" 1998 "born again--discovered that the phoenix music that hovered over us, wore"
"405" 1998 "lighter and brighter plumes than it had done theretofore.\""
"406" 1998 ""
"407" 1998 "During the month of August 1881 my brother resolved to reveal the"
"408" 1998 "teaching of the Eternal Recurrence, in dithyrambic and psalmodic form,"
"409" 1998 "through the mouth of Zarathustra. Among the notes of this period, we"
"410" 1998 "found a page on which is written the first definite plan of \"Thus Spake"
"411" 1998 "Zarathustra\":--"
"412" 1998 ""
"413" 1998 "\"MIDDAY AND ETERNITY.\""
"414" 1998 ""
"415" 1998 "\"GUIDE-POSTS TO A NEW WAY OF LIVING.\""
"416" 1998 ""
"417" 1998 "Beneath this is written:--"
"418" 1998 ""
"419" 1998 "\"Zarathustra born on lake Urmi; left his home in his thirtieth year,"
"420" 1998 "went into the province of Aria, and, during ten years of solitude in the"
"421" 1998 "mountains, composed the Zend-Avesta.\""
"422" 1998 ""
"423" 1998 "\"The sun of knowledge stands once more at midday; and the serpent"
"424" 1998 "of eternity lies coiled in its light--: It is YOUR time, ye midday"
"425" 1998 "brethren.\""
"426" 1998 ""
"427" 1998 "In that summer of 1881, my brother, after many years of steadily"
"428" 1998 "declining health, began at last to rally, and it is to this first gush"
"429" 1998 "of the recovery of his once splendid bodily condition that we owe not"
"430" 1998 "only \"The Gay Science\", which in its mood may be regarded as a prelude"
"431" 1998 "to \"Zarathustra\", but also \"Zarathustra\" itself. Just as he was"
"432" 1998 "beginning to recuperate his health, however, an unkind destiny brought"
"433" 1998 "him a number of most painful personal experiences. His friends caused"
"434" 1998 "him many disappointments, which were the more bitter to him, inasmuch as"
"435" 1998 "he regarded friendship as such a sacred institution; and for the first"
"436" 1998 "time in his life he realised the whole horror of that loneliness to"
"437" 1998 "which, perhaps, all greatness is condemned. But to be forsaken is"
"438" 1998 "something very different from deliberately choosing blessed loneliness."
"439" 1998 "How he longed, in those days, for the ideal friend who would thoroughly"
"440" 1998 "understand him, to whom he would be able to say all, and whom he"
"441" 1998 "imagined he had found at various periods in his life from his earliest"
"442" 1998 "youth onwards. Now, however, that the way he had chosen grew ever more"
"443" 1998 "perilous and steep, he found nobody who could follow him: he therefore"
"444" 1998 "created a perfect friend for himself in the ideal form of a majestic"
"445" 1998 "philosopher, and made this creation the preacher of his gospel to the"
"446" 1998 "world."
"447" 1998 ""
"448" 1998 "Whether my brother would ever have written \"Thus Spake Zarathustra\""
"449" 1998 "according to the first plan sketched in the summer of 1881, if he"
"450" 1998 "had not had the disappointments already referred to, is now an idle"
"451" 1998 "question; but perhaps where \"Zarathustra\" is concerned, we may also say"
"452" 1998 "with Master Eckhardt: \"The fleetest beast to bear you to perfection is"
"453" 1998 "suffering.\""
"454" 1998 ""
"455" 1998 "My brother writes as follows about the origin of the first part of"
"456" 1998 "\"Zarathustra\":--\"In the winter of 1882-83, I was living on the charming"
"457" 1998 "little Gulf of Rapallo, not far from Genoa, and between Chiavari and"
"458" 1998 "Cape Porto Fino. My health was not very good; the winter was cold and"
"459" 1998 "exceptionally rainy; and the small inn in which I lived was so close"
"460" 1998 "to the water that at night my sleep would be disturbed if the sea were"
"461" 1998 "high. These circumstances were surely the very reverse of favourable;"
"462" 1998 "and yet in spite of it all, and as if in demonstration of my belief that"
"463" 1998 "everything decisive comes to life in spite of every obstacle, it was"
"464" 1998 "precisely during this winter and in the midst of these unfavourable"
"465" 1998 "circumstances that my 'Zarathustra' originated. In the morning I used to"
"466" 1998 "start out in a southerly direction up the glorious road to Zoagli, which"
"467" 1998 "rises aloft through a forest of pines and gives one a view far out into"
"468" 1998 "the sea. In the afternoon, as often as my health permitted, I walked"
"469" 1998 "round the whole bay from Santa Margherita to beyond Porto Fino. This"
"470" 1998 "spot was all the more interesting to me, inasmuch as it was so dearly"
"471" 1998 "loved by the Emperor Frederick III. In the autumn of 1886 I chanced to"
"472" 1998 "be there again when he was revisiting this small, forgotten world"
"473" 1998 "of happiness for the last time. It was on these two roads that all"
"474" 1998 "'Zarathustra' came to me, above all Zarathustra himself as a type;--I"
"475" 1998 "ought rather to say that it was on these walks that these ideas waylaid"
"476" 1998 "me.\""
"477" 1998 ""
"478" 1998 "The first part of \"Zarathustra\" was written in about ten days--that is"
"479" 1998 "to say, from the beginning to about the middle of February 1883. \"The"
"480" 1998 "last lines were written precisely in the hallowed hour when Richard"
"481" 1998 "Wagner gave up the ghost in Venice.\""
"482" 1998 ""
"483" 1998 "With the exception of the ten days occupied in composing the first part"
"484" 1998 "of this book, my brother often referred to this winter as the hardest"
"485" 1998 "and sickliest he had ever experienced. He did not, however, mean thereby"
"486" 1998 "that his former disorders were troubling him, but that he was suffering"
"487" 1998 "from a severe attack of influenza which he had caught in Santa"
"488" 1998 "Margherita, and which tormented him for several weeks after his arrival"
"489" 1998 "in Genoa. As a matter of fact, however, what he complained of most was"
"490" 1998 "his spiritual condition--that indescribable forsakenness--to which he"
"491" 1998 "gives such heartrending expression in \"Zarathustra\". Even the reception"
"492" 1998 "which the first part met with at the hands of friends and acquaintances"
"493" 1998 "was extremely disheartening: for almost all those to whom he presented"
"494" 1998 "copies of the work misunderstood it. \"I found no one ripe for many of my"
"495" 1998 "thoughts; the case of 'Zarathustra' proves that one can speak with the"
"496" 1998 "utmost clearness, and yet not be heard by any one.\" My brother was very"
"497" 1998 "much discouraged by the feebleness of the response he was given, and as"
"498" 1998 "he was striving just then to give up the practice of taking hydrate"
"499" 1998 "of chloral--a drug he had begun to take while ill with influenza,--the"
"500" 1998 "following spring, spent in Rome, was a somewhat gloomy one for him."
"501" 1998 "He writes about it as follows:--\"I spent a melancholy spring in Rome,"
"502" 1998 "where I only just managed to live,--and this was no easy matter. This"
"503" 1998 "city, which is absolutely unsuited to the poet-author of 'Zarathustra',"
"504" 1998 "and for the choice of which I was not responsible, made me inordinately"
"505" 1998 "miserable. I tried to leave it. I wanted to go to Aquila--the opposite"
"506" 1998 "of Rome in every respect, and actually founded in a spirit of enmity"
"507" 1998 "towards that city (just as I also shall found a city some day), as a"
"508" 1998 "memento of an atheist and genuine enemy of the Church--a person very"
"509" 1998 "closely related to me,--the great Hohenstaufen, the Emperor Frederick"
"510" 1998 "II. But Fate lay behind it all: I had to return again to Rome. In the"
"511" 1998 "end I was obliged to be satisfied with the Piazza Barberini, after I had"
"512" 1998 "exerted myself in vain to find an anti-Christian quarter. I fear that"
"513" 1998 "on one occasion, to avoid bad smells as much as possible, I actually"
"514" 1998 "inquired at the Palazzo del Quirinale whether they could not provide a"
"515" 1998 "quiet room for a philosopher. In a chamber high above the Piazza just"
"516" 1998 "mentioned, from which one obtained a general view of Rome and could"
"517" 1998 "hear the fountains plashing far below, the loneliest of all songs"
"518" 1998 "was composed--'The Night-Song'. About this time I was obsessed by an"
"519" 1998 "unspeakably sad melody, the refrain of which I recognised in the words,"
"520" 1998 "'dead through immortality.'\""
"521" 1998 ""
"522" 1998 "We remained somewhat too long in Rome that spring, and what with the"
"523" 1998 "effect of the increasing heat and the discouraging circumstances already"
"524" 1998 "described, my brother resolved not to write any more, or in any case,"
"525" 1998 "not to proceed with \"Zarathustra\", although I offered to relieve him"
"526" 1998 "of all trouble in connection with the proofs and the publisher. When,"
"527" 1998 "however, we returned to Switzerland towards the end of June, and he"
"528" 1998 "found himself once more in the familiar and exhilarating air of the"
"529" 1998 "mountains, all his joyous creative powers revived, and in a note to me"
"530" 1998 "announcing the dispatch of some manuscript, he wrote as follows: \"I have"
"531" 1998 "engaged a place here for three months: forsooth, I am the greatest fool"
"532" 1998 "to allow my courage to be sapped from me by the climate of Italy. Now"
"533" 1998 "and again I am troubled by the thought: WHAT NEXT? My 'future' is the"
"534" 1998 "darkest thing in the world to me, but as there still remains a great"
"535" 1998 "deal for me to do, I suppose I ought rather to think of doing this than"
"536" 1998 "of my future, and leave the rest to THEE and the gods.\""
"537" 1998 ""
"538" 1998 "The second part of \"Zarathustra\" was written between the 26th of June"
"539" 1998 "and the 6th July. \"This summer, finding myself once more in the sacred"
"540" 1998 "place where the first thought of 'Zarathustra' flashed across my mind,"
"541" 1998 "I conceived the second part. Ten days sufficed. Neither for the second,"
"542" 1998 "the first, nor the third part, have I required a day longer.\""
"543" 1998 ""
"544" 1998 "He often used to speak of the ecstatic mood in which he wrote"
"545" 1998 "\"Zarathustra\"; how in his walks over hill and dale the ideas would crowd"
"546" 1998 "into his mind, and how he would note them down hastily in a note-book"
"547" 1998 "from which he would transcribe them on his return, sometimes working"
"548" 1998 "till midnight. He says in a letter to me: \"You can have no idea of the"
"549" 1998 "vehemence of such composition,\" and in \"Ecce Homo\" (autumn 1888) he"
"550" 1998 "describes as follows with passionate enthusiasm the incomparable mood in"
"551" 1998 "which he created Zarathustra:--"
"552" 1998 ""
"553" 1998 "\"--Has any one at the end of the nineteenth century any distinct notion"
"554" 1998 "of what poets of a stronger age understood by the word inspiration? If"
"555" 1998 "not, I will describe it. If one had the smallest vestige of superstition"
"556" 1998 "in one, it would hardly be possible to set aside completely the idea"
"557" 1998 "that one is the mere incarnation, mouthpiece or medium of an almighty"
"558" 1998 "power. The idea of revelation in the sense that something becomes"
"559" 1998 "suddenly visible and audible with indescribable certainty and accuracy,"
"560" 1998 "which profoundly convulses and upsets one--describes simply the matter"
"561" 1998 "of fact. One hears--one does not seek; one takes--one does not ask"
"562" 1998 "who gives: a thought suddenly flashes up like lightning, it comes with"
"563" 1998 "necessity, unhesitatingly--I have never had any choice in the matter."
"564" 1998 "There is an ecstasy such that the immense strain of it is sometimes"
"565" 1998 "relaxed by a flood of tears, along with which one's steps either rush"
"566" 1998 "or involuntarily lag, alternately. There is the feeling that one is"
"567" 1998 "completely out of hand, with the very distinct consciousness of an"
"568" 1998 "endless number of fine thrills and quiverings to the very toes;--there"
"569" 1998 "is a depth of happiness in which the painfullest and gloomiest do not"
"570" 1998 "operate as antitheses, but as conditioned, as demanded in the sense of"
"571" 1998 "necessary shades of colour in such an overflow of light. There is an"
"572" 1998 "instinct for rhythmic relations which embraces wide areas of forms"
"573" 1998 "(length, the need of a wide-embracing rhythm, is almost the measure of"
"574" 1998 "the force of an inspiration, a sort of counterpart to its pressure and"
"575" 1998 "tension). Everything happens quite involuntarily, as if in a tempestuous"
"576" 1998 "outburst of freedom, of absoluteness, of power and divinity. The"
"577" 1998 "involuntariness of the figures and similes is the most remarkable"
"578" 1998 "thing; one loses all perception of what constitutes the figure and"
"579" 1998 "what constitutes the simile; everything seems to present itself as"
"580" 1998 "the readiest, the correctest and the simplest means of expression."
"581" 1998 "It actually seems, to use one of Zarathustra's own phrases, as if all"
"582" 1998 "things came unto one, and would fain be similes: 'Here do all things"
"583" 1998 "come caressingly to thy talk and flatter thee, for they want to ride"
"584" 1998 "upon thy back. On every simile dost thou here ride to every truth. Here"
"585" 1998 "fly open unto thee all being's words and word-cabinets; here all being"
"586" 1998 "wanteth to become words, here all becoming wanteth to learn of thee how"
"587" 1998 "to talk.' This is MY experience of inspiration. I do not doubt but that"
"588" 1998 "one would have to go back thousands of years in order to find some one"
"589" 1998 "who could say to me: It is mine also!--\""
"590" 1998 ""
"591" 1998 "In the autumn of 1883 my brother left the Engadine for Germany and"
"592" 1998 "stayed there a few weeks. In the following winter, after wandering"
"593" 1998 "somewhat erratically through Stresa, Genoa, and Spezia, he landed in"
"594" 1998 "Nice, where the climate so happily promoted his creative powers that"
"595" 1998 "he wrote the third part of \"Zarathustra\". \"In the winter, beneath the"
"596" 1998 "halcyon sky of Nice, which then looked down upon me for the first time"
"597" 1998 "in my life, I found the third 'Zarathustra'--and came to the end of my"
"598" 1998 "task; the whole having occupied me scarcely a year. Many hidden corners"
"599" 1998 "and heights in the landscapes round about Nice are hallowed to me by"
"600" 1998 "unforgettable moments. That decisive chapter entitled 'Old and New"
"601" 1998 "Tables' was composed in the very difficult ascent from the station"
"602" 1998 "to Eza--that wonderful Moorish village in the rocks. My most creative"
"603" 1998 "moments were always accompanied by unusual muscular activity. The body"
"604" 1998 "is inspired: let us waive the question of the 'soul.' I might often have"
"605" 1998 "been seen dancing in those days. Without a suggestion of fatigue I could"
"606" 1998 "then walk for seven or eight hours on end among the hills. I slept well"
"607" 1998 "and laughed well--I was perfectly robust and patient.\""
"608" 1998 ""
"609" 1998 "As we have seen, each of the three parts of \"Zarathustra\" was written,"
"610" 1998 "after a more or less short period of preparation, in about ten days."
"611" 1998 "The composition of the fourth part alone was broken by occasional"
"612" 1998 "interruptions. The first notes relating to this part were written while"
"613" 1998 "he and I were staying together in Zurich in September 1884. In the"
"614" 1998 "following November, while staying at Mentone, he began to elaborate"
"615" 1998 "these notes, and after a long pause, finished the manuscript at Nice"
"616" 1998 "between the end of January and the middle of February 1885. My brother"
"617" 1998 "then called this part the fourth and last; but even before, and shortly"
"618" 1998 "after it had been privately printed, he wrote to me saying that he still"
"619" 1998 "intended writing a fifth and sixth part, and notes relating to these"
"620" 1998 "parts are now in my possession. This fourth part (the original MS. of"
"621" 1998 "which contains this note: \"Only for my friends, not for the public\")"
"622" 1998 "is written in a particularly personal spirit, and those few to whom he"
"623" 1998 "presented a copy of it, he pledged to the strictest secrecy concerning"
"624" 1998 "its contents. He often thought of making this fourth part public also,"
"625" 1998 "but doubted whether he would ever be able to do so without considerably"
"626" 1998 "altering certain portions of it. At all events he resolved to distribute"
"627" 1998 "this manuscript production, of which only forty copies were printed,"
"628" 1998 "only among those who had proved themselves worthy of it, and it speaks"
"629" 1998 "eloquently of his utter loneliness and need of sympathy in those days,"
"630" 1998 "that he had occasion to present only seven copies of his book according"
"631" 1998 "to this resolution."
"632" 1998 ""
"633" 1998 "Already at the beginning of this history I hinted at the reasons which"
"634" 1998 "led my brother to select a Persian as the incarnation of his ideal of"
"635" 1998 "the majestic philosopher. His reasons, however, for choosing Zarathustra"
"636" 1998 "of all others to be his mouthpiece, he gives us in the following"
"637" 1998 "words:--\"People have never asked me, as they should have done, what the"
"638" 1998 "name Zarathustra precisely means in my mouth, in the mouth of the first"
"639" 1998 "Immoralist; for what distinguishes that philosopher from all others"
"640" 1998 "in the past is the very fact that he was exactly the reverse of an"
"641" 1998 "immoralist. Zarathustra was the first to see in the struggle between"
"642" 1998 "good and evil the essential wheel in the working of things. The"
"643" 1998 "translation of morality into the metaphysical, as force, cause, end in"
"644" 1998 "itself, was HIS work. But the very question suggests its own answer."
"645" 1998 "Zarathustra CREATED the most portentous error, MORALITY, consequently he"
"646" 1998 "should also be the first to PERCEIVE that error, not only because he"
"647" 1998 "has had longer and greater experience of the subject than any other"
"648" 1998 "thinker--all history is the experimental refutation of the theory of"
"649" 1998 "the so-called moral order of things:--the more important point is that"
"650" 1998 "Zarathustra was more truthful than any other thinker. In his teaching"
"651" 1998 "alone do we meet with truthfulness upheld as the highest virtue--i.e.:"
"652" 1998 "the reverse of the COWARDICE of the 'idealist' who flees from reality."
"653" 1998 "Zarathustra had more courage in his body than any other thinker before"
"654" 1998 "or after him. To tell the truth and TO AIM STRAIGHT: that is the first"
"655" 1998 "Persian virtue. Am I understood?... The overcoming of morality through"
"656" 1998 "itself--through truthfulness, the overcoming of the moralist through his"
"657" 1998 "opposite--THROUGH ME--: that is what the name Zarathustra means in my"
"658" 1998 "mouth.\""
"659" 1998 ""

"680" 1998 ""
"681" 1998 ""
"682" 1998 "1."
"683" 1998 ""
"684" 1998 "When Zarathustra was thirty years old, he left his home and the lake of"
"685" 1998 "his home, and went into the mountains. There he enjoyed his spirit and"
"686" 1998 "solitude, and for ten years did not weary of it. But at last his heart"
"687" 1998 "changed,--and rising one morning with the rosy dawn, he went before the"
"688" 1998 "sun, and spake thus unto it:"
"689" 1998 ""
"690" 1998 "Thou great star! What would be thy happiness if thou hadst not those for"
"691" 1998 "whom thou shinest!"
"692" 1998 ""
"693" 1998 "For ten years hast thou climbed hither unto my cave: thou wouldst have"
"694" 1998 "wearied of thy light and of the journey, had it not been for me, mine"
"695" 1998 "eagle, and my serpent."
"696" 1998 ""
"697" 1998 "But we awaited thee every morning, took from thee thine overflow and"
"698" 1998 "blessed thee for it."
"699" 1998 ""
"700" 1998 "Lo! I am weary of my wisdom, like the bee that hath gathered too much"
"701" 1998 "honey; I need hands outstretched to take it."
"702" 1998 ""
"703" 1998 "I would fain bestow and distribute, until the wise have once more become"
"704" 1998 "joyous in their folly, and the poor happy in their riches."
"705" 1998 ""
"706" 1998 "Therefore must I descend into the deep: as thou doest in the"
"707" 1998 "evening, when thou goest behind the sea, and givest light also to the"
"708" 1998 "nether-world, thou exuberant star!"
"709" 1998 ""
"710" 1998 "Like thee must I GO DOWN, as men say, to whom I shall descend."
"711" 1998 ""
"712" 1998 "Bless me, then, thou tranquil eye, that canst behold even the greatest"
"713" 1998 "happiness without envy!"
"714" 1998 ""
"715" 1998 "Bless the cup that is about to overflow, that the water may flow golden"
"716" 1998 "out of it, and carry everywhere the reflection of thy bliss!"
"717" 1998 ""
"718" 1998 "Lo! This cup is again going to empty itself, and Zarathustra is again"
"719" 1998 "going to be a man."
"720" 1998 ""
"721" 1998 "Thus began Zarathustra's down-going."
"722" 1998 ""
"723" 1998 "2."
"724" 1998 ""
"725" 1998 "Zarathustra went down the mountain alone, no one meeting him. When he"
"726" 1998 "entered the forest, however, there suddenly stood before him an old man,"
"727" 1998 "who had left his holy cot to seek roots. And thus spake the old man to"
"728" 1998 "Zarathustra:"
"729" 1998 ""
"730" 1998 "\"No stranger to me is this wanderer: many years ago passed he by."
"731" 1998 "Zarathustra he was called; but he hath altered."
"732" 1998 ""
"733" 1998 "Then thou carriedst thine ashes into the mountains: wilt thou now carry"
"734" 1998 "thy fire into the valleys? Fearest thou not the incendiary's doom?"
"735" 1998 ""
"736" 1998 "Yea, I recognise Zarathustra. Pure is his eye, and no loathing lurketh"
"737" 1998 "about his mouth. Goeth he not along like a dancer?"
"738" 1998 ""
"739" 1998 "Altered is Zarathustra; a child hath Zarathustra become; an awakened one"
"740" 1998 "is Zarathustra: what wilt thou do in the land of the sleepers?"
"741" 1998 ""
"742" 1998 "As in the sea hast thou lived in solitude, and it hath borne thee up."
"743" 1998 "Alas, wilt thou now go ashore? Alas, wilt thou again drag thy body"
"744" 1998 "thyself?\""
"745" 1998 ""
"746" 1998 "Zarathustra answered: \"I love mankind.\""
"747" 1998 ""
"748" 1998 "\"Why,\" said the saint, \"did I go into the forest and the desert? Was it"
"749" 1998 "not because I loved men far too well?"
"750" 1998 ""
"751" 1998 "Now I love God: men, I do not love. Man is a thing too imperfect for me."
"752" 1998 "Love to man would be fatal to me.\""
"753" 1998 ""
"754" 1998 "Zarathustra answered: \"What spake I of love! I am bringing gifts unto"
"755" 1998 "men.\""
"756" 1998 ""
"757" 1998 "\"Give them nothing,\" said the saint. \"Take rather part of their load,"
"758" 1998 "and carry it along with them--that will be most agreeable unto them: if"
"759" 1998 "only it be agreeable unto thee!"
"760" 1998 ""
"761" 1998 "If, however, thou wilt give unto them, give them no more than an alms,"
"762" 1998 "and let them also beg for it!\""
"763" 1998 ""
"764" 1998 "\"No,\" replied Zarathustra, \"I give no alms. I am not poor enough for"
"765" 1998 "that.\""
"766" 1998 ""
"767" 1998 "The saint laughed at Zarathustra, and spake thus: \"Then see to it that"
"768" 1998 "they accept thy treasures! They are distrustful of anchorites, and do"
"769" 1998 "not believe that we come with gifts."
"770" 1998 ""
"771" 1998 "The fall of our footsteps ringeth too hollow through their streets. And"
"772" 1998 "just as at night, when they are in bed and hear a man abroad long before"
"773" 1998 "sunrise, so they ask themselves concerning us: Where goeth the thief?"
"774" 1998 ""
"775" 1998 "Go not to men, but stay in the forest! Go rather to the animals! Why not"
"776" 1998 "be like me--a bear amongst bears, a bird amongst birds?\""
"777" 1998 ""
"778" 1998 "\"And what doeth the saint in the forest?\" asked Zarathustra."
"779" 1998 ""
"780" 1998 "The saint answered: \"I make hymns and sing them; and in making hymns I"
"781" 1998 "laugh and weep and mumble: thus do I praise God."
"782" 1998 ""
"783" 1998 "With singing, weeping, laughing, and mumbling do I praise the God who is"
"784" 1998 "my God. But what dost thou bring us as a gift?\""
"785" 1998 ""
"786" 1998 "When Zarathustra had heard these words, he bowed to the saint and said:"
"787" 1998 "\"What should I have to give thee! Let me rather hurry hence lest I take"
"788" 1998 "aught away from thee!\"--And thus they parted from one another, the old"
"789" 1998 "man and Zarathustra, laughing like schoolboys."
"790" 1998 ""
"791" 1998 "When Zarathustra was alone, however, he said to his heart: \"Could it be"
"792" 1998 "possible! This old saint in the forest hath not yet heard of it, that"
"793" 1998 "GOD IS DEAD!\""
"794" 1998 ""
"795" 1998 "3."
"796" 1998 ""
"797" 1998 "When Zarathustra arrived at the nearest town which adjoineth the forest,"
"798" 1998 "he found many people assembled in the market-place; for it had been"
"799" 1998 "announced that a rope-dancer would give a performance. And Zarathustra"
"800" 1998 "spake thus unto the people:"
"801" 1998 ""
"802" 1998 "I TEACH YOU THE SUPERMAN. Man is something that is to be surpassed. What"
"803" 1998 "have ye done to surpass man?"
"804" 1998 ""
"805" 1998 "All beings hitherto have created something beyond themselves: and ye"
"806" 1998 "want to be the ebb of that great tide, and would rather go back to the"
"807" 1998 "beast than surpass man?"
"808" 1998 ""
"809" 1998 "What is the ape to man? A laughing-stock, a thing of shame. And just the"
"810" 1998 "same shall man be to the Superman: a laughing-stock, a thing of shame."
"811" 1998 ""
"812" 1998 "Ye have made your way from the worm to man, and much within you is still"
"813" 1998 "worm. Once were ye apes, and even yet man is more of an ape than any of"
"814" 1998 "the apes."
"815" 1998 ""
"816" 1998 "Even the wisest among you is only a disharmony and hybrid of plant and"
"817" 1998 "phantom. But do I bid you become phantoms or plants?"
"818" 1998 ""
"819" 1998 "Lo, I teach you the Superman!"
"820" 1998 ""
"821" 1998 "The Superman is the meaning of the earth. Let your will say: The"
"822" 1998 "Superman SHALL BE the meaning of the earth!"
"823" 1998 ""
"824" 1998 "I conjure you, my brethren, REMAIN TRUE TO THE EARTH, and believe not"
"825" 1998 "those who speak unto you of superearthly hopes! Poisoners are they,"
"826" 1998 "whether they know it or not."
"827" 1998 ""
"828" 1998 "Despisers of life are they, decaying ones and poisoned ones themselves,"
"829" 1998 "of whom the earth is weary: so away with them!"
"830" 1998 ""
"831" 1998 "Once blasphemy against God was the greatest blasphemy; but God died,"
"832" 1998 "and therewith also those blasphemers. To blaspheme the earth is now the"
"833" 1998 "dreadfulest sin, and to rate the heart of the unknowable higher than the"
"834" 1998 "meaning of the earth!"
"835" 1998 ""
"836" 1998 "Once the soul looked contemptuously on the body, and then that contempt"
"837" 1998 "was the supreme thing:--the soul wished the body meagre, ghastly, and"
"838" 1998 "famished. Thus it thought to escape from the body and the earth."
"839" 1998 ""
"840" 1998 "Oh, that soul was itself meagre, ghastly, and famished; and cruelty was"
"841" 1998 "the delight of that soul!"
"842" 1998 ""
"843" 1998 "But ye, also, my brethren, tell me: What doth your body say about"
"844" 1998 "your soul? Is your soul not poverty and pollution and wretched"
"845" 1998 "self-complacency?"
"846" 1998 ""
"847" 1998 "Verily, a polluted stream is man. One must be a sea, to receive a"
"848" 1998 "polluted stream without becoming impure."
"849" 1998 ""
"850" 1998 "Lo, I teach you the Superman: he is that sea; in him can your great"
"851" 1998 "contempt be submerged."
"852" 1998 ""
"853" 1998 "What is the greatest thing ye can experience? It is the hour of great"
"854" 1998 "contempt. The hour in which even your happiness becometh loathsome unto"
"855" 1998 "you, and so also your reason and virtue."
"856" 1998 ""
"857" 1998 "The hour when ye say: \"What good is my happiness! It is poverty and"
"858" 1998 "pollution and wretched self-complacency. But my happiness should justify"
"859" 1998 "existence itself!\""
"860" 1998 ""
"861" 1998 "The hour when ye say: \"What good is my reason! Doth it long for"
"862" 1998 "knowledge as the lion for his food? It is poverty and pollution and"
"863" 1998 "wretched self-complacency!\""
"864" 1998 ""
"865" 1998 "The hour when ye say: \"What good is my virtue! As yet it hath not made"
"866" 1998 "me passionate. How weary I am of my good and my bad! It is all poverty"
"867" 1998 "and pollution and wretched self-complacency!\""
"868" 1998 ""
"869" 1998 "The hour when ye say: \"What good is my justice! I do not see that I am"
"870" 1998 "fervour and fuel. The just, however, are fervour and fuel!\""
"871" 1998 ""
"872" 1998 "The hour when ye say: \"What good is my pity! Is not pity the cross on"
"873" 1998 "which he is nailed who loveth man? But my pity is not a crucifixion.\""
"874" 1998 ""
"875" 1998 "Have ye ever spoken thus? Have ye ever cried thus? Ah! would that I had"
"876" 1998 "heard you crying thus!"
"877" 1998 ""
"878" 1998 "It is not your sin--it is your self-satisfaction that crieth unto"
"879" 1998 "heaven; your very sparingness in sin crieth unto heaven!"
"880" 1998 ""
"881" 1998 "Where is the lightning to lick you with its tongue? Where is the frenzy"
"882" 1998 "with which ye should be inoculated?"
"883" 1998 ""
"884" 1998 "Lo, I teach you the Superman: he is that lightning, he is that frenzy!--"
"885" 1998 ""
"886" 1998 "When Zarathustra had thus spoken, one of the people called out: \"We have"
"887" 1998 "now heard enough of the rope-dancer; it is time now for us to see him!\""
"888" 1998 "And all the people laughed at Zarathustra. But the rope-dancer, who"
"889" 1998 "thought the words applied to him, began his performance."
"890" 1998 ""
"891" 1998 "4."
"892" 1998 ""
"893" 1998 "Zarathustra, however, looked at the people and wondered. Then he spake"
"894" 1998 "thus:"
"895" 1998 ""
"896" 1998 "Man is a rope stretched between the animal and the Superman--a rope over"
"897" 1998 "an abyss."
"898" 1998 ""
"899" 1998 "A dangerous crossing, a dangerous wayfaring, a dangerous looking-back, a"
"900" 1998 "dangerous trembling and halting."
"901" 1998 ""
"902" 1998 "What is great in man is that he is a bridge and not a goal: what is"
"903" 1998 "lovable in man is that he is an OVER-GOING and a DOWN-GOING."
"904" 1998 ""
"905" 1998 "I love those that know not how to live except as down-goers, for they"
"906" 1998 "are the over-goers."
"907" 1998 ""
"908" 1998 "I love the great despisers, because they are the great adorers, and"
"909" 1998 "arrows of longing for the other shore."
"910" 1998 ""

"914" 1998 ""

"922" 1998 ""

"929" 1998 ""

"932" 1998 ""

"1328" 1998 ""
"1329" 1998 "Is it not this: To humiliate oneself in order to mortify one's pride? To"
"1330" 1998 "exhibit one's folly in order to mock at one's wisdom?"
"1331" 1998 ""
"1332" 1998 "Or is it this: To desert our cause when it celebrateth its triumph? To"
"1333" 1998 "ascend high mountains to tempt the tempter?"
"1334" 1998 ""
"1335" 1998 "Or is it this: To feed on the acorns and grass of knowledge, and for the"
"1336" 1998 "sake of truth to suffer hunger of soul?"
"1337" 1998 ""
"1338" 1998 "Or is it this: To be sick and dismiss comforters, and make friends of"
"1339" 1998 "the deaf, who never hear thy requests?"
"1340" 1998 ""
"1341" 1998 "Or is it this: To go into foul water when it is the water of truth, and"
"1342" 1998 "not disclaim cold frogs and hot toads?"
"1343" 1998 ""
"1344" 1998 "Or is it this: To love those who despise us, and give one's hand to the"
"1345" 1998 "phantom when it is going to frighten us?"
"1346" 1998 ""
"1347" 1998 "All these heaviest things the load-bearing spirit taketh upon itself:"
"1348" 1998 "and like the camel, which, when laden, hasteneth into the wilderness, so"
"1349" 1998 "hasteneth the spirit into its wilderness."
"1350" 1998 ""
"1351" 1998 "But in the loneliest wilderness happeneth the second metamorphosis: here"
"1352" 1998 "the spirit becometh a lion; freedom will it capture, and lordship in its"
"1353" 1998 "own wilderness."
"1354" 1998 ""
"1355" 1998 "Its last Lord it here seeketh: hostile will it be to him, and to its"
"1356" 1998 "last God; for victory will it struggle with the great dragon."
"1357" 1998 ""
"1358" 1998 "What is the great dragon which the spirit is no longer inclined to call"
"1359" 1998 "Lord and God? \"Thou-shalt,\" is the great dragon called. But the spirit"
"1360" 1998 "of the lion saith, \"I will.\""
"1361" 1998 ""
"1362" 1998 "\"Thou-shalt,\" lieth in its path, sparkling with gold--a scale-covered"
"1363" 1998 "beast; and on every scale glittereth golden, \"Thou shalt!\""
"1364" 1998 ""
"1365" 1998 "The values of a thousand years glitter on those scales, and"
"1366" 1998 "thus speaketh the mightiest of all dragons: \"All the values of"
"1367" 1998 "things--glitter on me."
"1368" 1998 ""
"1369" 1998 "All values have already been created, and all created values--do I"
"1370" 1998 "represent. Verily, there shall be no 'I will' any more. Thus speaketh"
"1371" 1998 "the dragon."
"1372" 1998 ""
"1373" 1998 "My brethren, wherefore is there need of the lion in the spirit? Why"
"1374" 1998 "sufficeth not the beast of burden, which renounceth and is reverent?"
"1375" 1998 ""
"1376" 1998 "To create new values--that, even the lion cannot yet accomplish: but to"
"1377" 1998 "create itself freedom for new creating--that can the might of the lion"
"1378" 1998 "do."
"1379" 1998 ""
"1380" 1998 "To create itself freedom, and give a holy Nay even unto duty: for that,"
"1381" 1998 "my brethren, there is need of the lion."
"1382" 1998 ""
"1383" 1998 "To assume the right to new values--that is the most formidable"
"1384" 1998 "assumption for a load-bearing and reverent spirit. Verily, unto such a"
"1385" 1998 "spirit it is preying, and the work of a beast of prey."
"1386" 1998 ""
"1387" 1998 "As its holiest, it once loved \"Thou-shalt\": now is it forced to find"
"1388" 1998 "illusion and arbitrariness even in the holiest things, that it may"
"1389" 1998 "capture freedom from its love: the lion is needed for this capture."
"1390" 1998 ""
"1391" 1998 "But tell me, my brethren, what the child can do, which even the lion"
"1392" 1998 "could not do? Why hath the preying lion still to become a child?"
"1393" 1998 ""
"1394" 1998 "Innocence is the child, and forgetfulness, a new beginning, a game, a"
"1395" 1998 "self-rolling wheel, a first movement, a holy Yea."
"1396" 1998 ""
"1397" 1998 "Aye, for the game of creating, my brethren, there is needed a holy Yea"
"1398" 1998 "unto life: ITS OWN will, willeth now the spirit; HIS OWN world winneth"
"1399" 1998 "the world's outcast."
"1400" 1998 ""
"1401" 1998 "Three metamorphoses of the spirit have I designated to you: how the"
"1402" 1998 "spirit became a camel, the camel a lion, and the lion at last a child.--"
"1403" 1998 ""
"1404" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra. And at that time he abode in the town which is"
"1405" 1998 "called The Pied Cow."
"1406" 1998 ""
"1407" 1998 ""
"1408" 1998 ""
"1409" 1998 ""
"1410" 1998 "II. THE ACADEMIC CHAIRS OF VIRTUE."
"1411" 1998 ""
"1412" 1998 "People commended unto Zarathustra a wise man, as one who could discourse"
"1413" 1998 "well about sleep and virtue: greatly was he honoured and rewarded for"
"1414" 1998 "it, and all the youths sat before his chair. To him went Zarathustra,"
"1415" 1998 "and sat among the youths before his chair. And thus spake the wise man:"
"1416" 1998 ""
"1417" 1998 "Respect and modesty in presence of sleep! That is the first thing! And"
"1418" 1998 "to go out of the way of all who sleep badly and keep awake at night!"
"1419" 1998 ""
"1420" 1998 "Modest is even the thief in presence of sleep: he always stealeth softly"
"1421" 1998 "through the night. Immodest, however, is the night-watchman; immodestly"
"1422" 1998 "he carrieth his horn."
"1423" 1998 ""
"1424" 1998 "No small art is it to sleep: it is necessary for that purpose to keep"
"1425" 1998 "awake all day."
"1426" 1998 ""
"1427" 1998 "Ten times a day must thou overcome thyself: that causeth wholesome"
"1428" 1998 "weariness, and is poppy to the soul."
"1429" 1998 ""
"1430" 1998 "Ten times must thou reconcile again with thyself; for overcoming is"
"1431" 1998 "bitterness, and badly sleep the unreconciled."
"1432" 1998 ""
"1433" 1998 "Ten truths must thou find during the day; otherwise wilt thou seek truth"
"1434" 1998 "during the night, and thy soul will have been hungry."
"1435" 1998 ""
"1436" 1998 "Ten times must thou laugh during the day, and be cheerful; otherwise thy"
"1437" 1998 "stomach, the father of affliction, will disturb thee in the night."
"1438" 1998 ""
"1439" 1998 "Few people know it, but one must have all the virtues in order to sleep"
"1440" 1998 "well. Shall I bear false witness? Shall I commit adultery?"
"1441" 1998 ""
"1442" 1998 "Shall I covet my neighbour's maidservant? All that would ill accord with"
"1443" 1998 "good sleep."
"1444" 1998 ""
"1445" 1998 "And even if one have all the virtues, there is still one thing needful:"
"1446" 1998 "to send the virtues themselves to sleep at the right time."
"1447" 1998 ""
"1448" 1998 "That they may not quarrel with one another, the good females! And about"
"1449" 1998 "thee, thou unhappy one!"
"1450" 1998 ""
"1451" 1998 "Peace with God and thy neighbour: so desireth good sleep. And peace also"
"1452" 1998 "with thy neighbour's devil! Otherwise it will haunt thee in the night."
"1453" 1998 ""
"1454" 1998 "Honour to the government, and obedience, and also to the crooked"
"1455" 1998 "government! So desireth good sleep. How can I help it, if power like to"
"1456" 1998 "walk on crooked legs?"
"1457" 1998 ""
"1458" 1998 "He who leadeth his sheep to the greenest pasture, shall always be for me"
"1459" 1998 "the best shepherd: so doth it accord with good sleep."
"1460" 1998 ""
"1461" 1998 "Many honours I want not, nor great treasures: they excite the spleen."
"1462" 1998 "But it is bad sleeping without a good name and a little treasure."
"1463" 1998 ""
"1464" 1998 "A small company is more welcome to me than a bad one: but they must come"
"1465" 1998 "and go at the right time. So doth it accord with good sleep."
"1466" 1998 ""
"1467" 1998 "Well, also, do the poor in spirit please me: they promote sleep. Blessed"
"1468" 1998 "are they, especially if one always give in to them."
"1469" 1998 ""
"1470" 1998 "Thus passeth the day unto the virtuous. When night cometh, then take I"
"1471" 1998 "good care not to summon sleep. It disliketh to be summoned--sleep, the"
"1472" 1998 "lord of the virtues!"
"1473" 1998 ""
"1474" 1998 "But I think of what I have done and thought during the day. Thus"
"1475" 1998 "ruminating, patient as a cow, I ask myself: What were thy ten"
"1476" 1998 "overcomings?"
"1477" 1998 ""
"1478" 1998 "And what were the ten reconciliations, and the ten truths, and the ten"
"1479" 1998 "laughters with which my heart enjoyed itself?"
"1480" 1998 ""
"1481" 1998 "Thus pondering, and cradled by forty thoughts, it overtaketh me all at"
"1482" 1998 "once--sleep, the unsummoned, the lord of the virtues."
"1483" 1998 ""
"1484" 1998 "Sleep tappeth on mine eye, and it turneth heavy. Sleep toucheth my"
"1485" 1998 "mouth, and it remaineth open."
"1486" 1998 ""
"1487" 1998 "Verily, on soft soles doth it come to me, the dearest of thieves, and"
"1488" 1998 "stealeth from me my thoughts: stupid do I then stand, like this academic"
"1489" 1998 "chair."
"1490" 1998 ""
"1491" 1998 "But not much longer do I then stand: I already lie.--"
"1492" 1998 ""
"1493" 1998 "When Zarathustra heard the wise man thus speak, he laughed in his heart:"
"1494" 1998 "for thereby had a light dawned upon him. And thus spake he to his heart:"
"1495" 1998 ""
"1496" 1998 "A fool seemeth this wise man with his forty thoughts: but I believe he"
"1497" 1998 "knoweth well how to sleep."
"1498" 1998 ""
"1499" 1998 "Happy even is he who liveth near this wise man! Such sleep is"
"1500" 1998 "contagious--even through a thick wall it is contagious."
"1501" 1998 ""
"1502" 1998 "A magic resideth even in his academic chair. And not in vain did the"
"1503" 1998 "youths sit before the preacher of virtue."
"1504" 1998 ""
"1505" 1998 "His wisdom is to keep awake in order to sleep well. And verily, if"
"1506" 1998 "life had no sense, and had I to choose nonsense, this would be the"
"1507" 1998 "desirablest nonsense for me also."
"1508" 1998 ""
"1509" 1998 "Now know I well what people sought formerly above all else when they"
"1510" 1998 "sought teachers of virtue. Good sleep they sought for themselves, and"
"1511" 1998 "poppy-head virtues to promote it!"
"1512" 1998 ""
"1513" 1998 "To all those belauded sages of the academic chairs, wisdom was sleep"
"1514" 1998 "without dreams: they knew no higher significance of life."
"1515" 1998 ""
"1516" 1998 "Even at present, to be sure, there are some like this preacher of"
"1517" 1998 "virtue, and not always so honourable: but their time is past. And not"
"1518" 1998 "much longer do they stand: there they already lie."
"1519" 1998 ""
"1520" 1998 "Blessed are those drowsy ones: for they shall soon nod to sleep.--"
"1521" 1998 ""
"1522" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra."
"1523" 1998 ""
"1524" 1998 ""
"1525" 1998 ""
"1526" 1998 ""
"1527" 1998 "III. BACKWORLDSMEN."
"1528" 1998 ""
"1529" 1998 "Once on a time, Zarathustra also cast his fancy beyond man, like all"
"1530" 1998 "backworldsmen. The work of a suffering and tortured God, did the world"
"1531" 1998 "then seem to me."
"1532" 1998 ""
"1533" 1998 "The dream--and diction--of a God, did the world then seem to me;"
"1534" 1998 "coloured vapours before the eyes of a divinely dissatisfied one."
"1535" 1998 ""
"1536" 1998 "Good and evil, and joy and woe, and I and thou--coloured vapours did"
"1537" 1998 "they seem to me before creative eyes. The creator wished to look away"
"1538" 1998 "from himself,--thereupon he created the world."
"1539" 1998 ""
"1540" 1998 "Intoxicating joy is it for the sufferer to look away from his suffering"
"1541" 1998 "and forget himself. Intoxicating joy and self-forgetting, did the world"
"1542" 1998 "once seem to me."
"1543" 1998 ""
"1544" 1998 "This world, the eternally imperfect, an eternal contradiction's image"
"1545" 1998 "and imperfect image--an intoxicating joy to its imperfect creator:--thus"
"1546" 1998 "did the world once seem to me."
"1547" 1998 ""
"1548" 1998 "Thus, once on a time, did I also cast my fancy beyond man, like all"
"1549" 1998 "backworldsmen. Beyond man, forsooth?"
"1550" 1998 ""
"1551" 1998 "Ah, ye brethren, that God whom I created was human work and human"
"1552" 1998 "madness, like all the Gods!"
"1553" 1998 ""
"1554" 1998 "A man was he, and only a poor fragment of a man and ego. Out of mine own"
"1555" 1998 "ashes and glow it came unto me, that phantom. And verily, it came not"
"1556" 1998 "unto me from the beyond!"
"1557" 1998 ""
"1558" 1998 "What happened, my brethren? I surpassed myself, the suffering one; I"
"1559" 1998 "carried mine own ashes to the mountain; a brighter flame I contrived for"
"1560" 1998 "myself. And lo! Thereupon the phantom WITHDREW from me!"
"1561" 1998 ""
"1562" 1998 "To me the convalescent would it now be suffering and torment to believe"
"1563" 1998 "in such phantoms: suffering would it now be to me, and humiliation. Thus"
"1564" 1998 "speak I to backworldsmen."
"1565" 1998 ""
"1566" 1998 "Suffering was it, and impotence--that created all backworlds; and"
"1567" 1998 "the short madness of happiness, which only the greatest sufferer"
"1568" 1998 "experienceth."
"1569" 1998 ""
"1570" 1998 "Weariness, which seeketh to get to the ultimate with one leap, with"
"1571" 1998 "a death-leap; a poor ignorant weariness, unwilling even to will any"
"1572" 1998 "longer: that created all Gods and backworlds."
"1573" 1998 ""
"1574" 1998 "Believe me, my brethren! It was the body which despaired of the body--it"
"1575" 1998 "groped with the fingers of the infatuated spirit at the ultimate walls."
"1576" 1998 ""
"1577" 1998 "Believe me, my brethren! It was the body which despaired of the"
"1578" 1998 "earth--it heard the bowels of existence speaking unto it."
"1579" 1998 ""
"1580" 1998 "And then it sought to get through the ultimate walls with its head--and"
"1581" 1998 "not with its head only--into \"the other world.\""
"1582" 1998 ""
"1583" 1998 "But that \"other world\" is well concealed from man, that dehumanised,"
"1584" 1998 "inhuman world, which is a celestial naught; and the bowels of existence"
"1585" 1998 "do not speak unto man, except as man."
"1586" 1998 ""
"1587" 1998 "Verily, it is difficult to prove all being, and hard to make it speak."
"1588" 1998 "Tell me, ye brethren, is not the strangest of all things best proved?"
"1589" 1998 ""
"1590" 1998 "Yea, this ego, with its contradiction and perplexity, speaketh most"
"1591" 1998 "uprightly of its being--this creating, willing, evaluing ego, which is"
"1592" 1998 "the measure and value of things."
"1593" 1998 ""
"1594" 1998 "And this most upright existence, the ego--it speaketh of the body, and"
"1595" 1998 "still implieth the body, even when it museth and raveth and fluttereth"
"1596" 1998 "with broken wings."
"1597" 1998 ""
"1598" 1998 "Always more uprightly learneth it to speak, the ego; and the more it"
"1599" 1998 "learneth, the more doth it find titles and honours for the body and the"
"1600" 1998 "earth."
"1601" 1998 ""
"1602" 1998 "A new pride taught me mine ego, and that teach I unto men: no longer"
"1603" 1998 "to thrust one's head into the sand of celestial things, but to carry it"
"1604" 1998 "freely, a terrestrial head, which giveth meaning to the earth!"
"1605" 1998 ""
"1606" 1998 "A new will teach I unto men: to choose that path which man hath followed"
"1607" 1998 "blindly, and to approve of it--and no longer to slink aside from it,"
"1608" 1998 "like the sick and perishing!"
"1609" 1998 ""
"1610" 1998 "The sick and perishing--it was they who despised the body and the earth,"
"1611" 1998 "and invented the heavenly world, and the redeeming blood-drops; but even"
"1612" 1998 "those sweet and sad poisons they borrowed from the body and the earth!"
"1613" 1998 ""
"1614" 1998 "From their misery they sought escape, and the stars were too remote for"
"1615" 1998 "them. Then they sighed: \"O that there were heavenly paths by which to"
"1616" 1998 "steal into another existence and into happiness!\" Then they contrived"
"1617" 1998 "for themselves their by-paths and bloody draughts!"
"1618" 1998 ""
"1619" 1998 "Beyond the sphere of their body and this earth they now fancied"
"1620" 1998 "themselves transported, these ungrateful ones. But to what did they owe"
"1621" 1998 "the convulsion and rapture of their transport? To their body and this"
"1622" 1998 "earth."
"1623" 1998 ""
"1624" 1998 "Gentle is Zarathustra to the sickly. Verily, he is not indignant"
"1625" 1998 "at their modes of consolation and ingratitude. May they become"
"1626" 1998 "convalescents and overcomers, and create higher bodies for themselves!"
"1627" 1998 ""
"1628" 1998 "Neither is Zarathustra indignant at a convalescent who looketh tenderly"
"1629" 1998 "on his delusions, and at midnight stealeth round the grave of his God;"
"1630" 1998 "but sickness and a sick frame remain even in his tears."
"1631" 1998 ""
"1632" 1998 "Many sickly ones have there always been among those who muse, and"
"1633" 1998 "languish for God; violently they hate the discerning ones, and the"
"1634" 1998 "latest of virtues, which is uprightness."
"1635" 1998 ""
"1636" 1998 "Backward they always gaze toward dark ages: then, indeed, were delusion"
"1637" 1998 "and faith something different. Raving of the reason was likeness to God,"
"1638" 1998 "and doubt was sin."
"1639" 1998 ""
"1640" 1998 "Too well do I know those godlike ones: they insist on being believed in,"
"1641" 1998 "and that doubt is sin. Too well, also, do I know what they themselves"
"1642" 1998 "most believe in."
"1643" 1998 ""
"1644" 1998 "Verily, not in backworlds and redeeming blood-drops: but in the body"
"1645" 1998 "do they also believe most; and their own body is for them the"
"1646" 1998 "thing-in-itself."
"1647" 1998 ""
"1648" 1998 "But it is a sickly thing to them, and gladly would they get out of their"
"1649" 1998 "skin. Therefore hearken they to the preachers of death, and themselves"
"1650" 1998 "preach backworlds."
"1651" 1998 ""
"1652" 1998 "Hearken rather, my brethren, to the voice of the healthy body; it is a"
"1653" 1998 "more upright and pure voice."
"1654" 1998 ""
"1655" 1998 "More uprightly and purely speaketh the healthy body, perfect and"
"1656" 1998 "square-built; and it speaketh of the meaning of the earth.--"
"1657" 1998 ""
"1658" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra."
"1659" 1998 ""
"1660" 1998 ""
"1661" 1998 ""
"1662" 1998 ""
"1663" 1998 "IV. THE DESPISERS OF THE BODY."
"1664" 1998 ""
"1665" 1998 "To the despisers of the body will I speak my word. I wish them neither"
"1666" 1998 "to learn afresh, nor teach anew, but only to bid farewell to their own"
"1667" 1998 "bodies,--and thus be dumb."
"1668" 1998 ""
"1669" 1998 "\"Body am I, and soul\"--so saith the child. And why should one not speak"
"1670" 1998 "like children?"
"1671" 1998 ""
"1672" 1998 "But the awakened one, the knowing one, saith: \"Body am I entirely, and"
"1673" 1998 "nothing more; and soul is only the name of something in the body.\""
"1674" 1998 ""
"1675" 1998 "The body is a big sagacity, a plurality with one sense, a war and a"
"1676" 1998 "peace, a flock and a shepherd."
"1677" 1998 ""
"1678" 1998 "An instrument of thy body is also thy little sagacity, my brother, which"
"1679" 1998 "thou callest \"spirit\"--a little instrument and plaything of thy big"
"1680" 1998 "sagacity."
"1681" 1998 ""
"1682" 1998 "\"Ego,\" sayest thou, and art proud of that word. But the greater"
"1683" 1998 "thing--in which thou art unwilling to believe--is thy body with its big"
"1684" 1998 "sagacity; it saith not \"ego,\" but doeth it."
"1685" 1998 ""
"1686" 1998 "What the sense feeleth, what the spirit discerneth, hath never its end"
"1687" 1998 "in itself. But sense and spirit would fain persuade thee that they are"
"1688" 1998 "the end of all things: so vain are they."
"1689" 1998 ""
"1690" 1998 "Instruments and playthings are sense and spirit: behind them there"
"1691" 1998 "is still the Self. The Self seeketh with the eyes of the senses, it"
"1692" 1998 "hearkeneth also with the ears of the spirit."
"1693" 1998 ""
"1694" 1998 "Ever hearkeneth the Self, and seeketh; it compareth, mastereth,"
"1695" 1998 "conquereth, and destroyeth. It ruleth, and is also the ego's ruler."
"1696" 1998 ""
"1697" 1998 "Behind thy thoughts and feelings, my brother, there is a mighty lord,"
"1698" 1998 "an unknown sage--it is called Self; it dwelleth in thy body, it is thy"
"1699" 1998 "body."
"1700" 1998 ""
"1701" 1998 "There is more sagacity in thy body than in thy best wisdom. And who then"
"1702" 1998 "knoweth why thy body requireth just thy best wisdom?"
"1703" 1998 ""
"1704" 1998 "Thy Self laugheth at thine ego, and its proud prancings. \"What are these"
"1705" 1998 "prancings and flights of thought unto me?\" it saith to itself. \"A by-way"
"1706" 1998 "to my purpose. I am the leading-string of the ego, and the prompter of"
"1707" 1998 "its notions.\""
"1708" 1998 ""
"1709" 1998 "The Self saith unto the ego: \"Feel pain!\" And thereupon it suffereth,"
"1710" 1998 "and thinketh how it may put an end thereto--and for that very purpose it"
"1711" 1998 "IS MEANT to think."
"1712" 1998 ""
"1713" 1998 "The Self saith unto the ego: \"Feel pleasure!\" Thereupon it rejoiceth,"
"1714" 1998 "and thinketh how it may ofttimes rejoice--and for that very purpose it"
"1715" 1998 "IS MEANT to think."
"1716" 1998 ""
"1717" 1998 "To the despisers of the body will I speak a word. That they despise is"
"1718" 1998 "caused by their esteem. What is it that created esteeming and despising"
"1719" 1998 "and worth and will?"
"1720" 1998 ""
"1721" 1998 "The creating Self created for itself esteeming and despising, it created"
"1722" 1998 "for itself joy and woe. The creating body created for itself spirit, as"
"1723" 1998 "a hand to its will."
"1724" 1998 ""
"1725" 1998 "Even in your folly and despising ye each serve your Self, ye despisers"
"1726" 1998 "of the body. I tell you, your very Self wanteth to die, and turneth away"
"1727" 1998 "from life."
"1728" 1998 ""
"1729" 1998 "No longer can your Self do that which it desireth most:--create beyond"
"1730" 1998 "itself. That is what it desireth most; that is all its fervour."
"1731" 1998 ""
"1732" 1998 "But it is now too late to do so:--so your Self wisheth to succumb, ye"
"1733" 1998 "despisers of the body."
"1734" 1998 ""
"1735" 1998 "To succumb--so wisheth your Self; and therefore have ye become despisers"
"1736" 1998 "of the body. For ye can no longer create beyond yourselves."
"1737" 1998 ""
"1738" 1998 "And therefore are ye now angry with life and with the earth. And"
"1739" 1998 "unconscious envy is in the sidelong look of your contempt."
"1740" 1998 ""
"1741" 1998 "I go not your way, ye despisers of the body! Ye are no bridges for me to"
"1742" 1998 "the Superman!--"
"1743" 1998 ""
"1744" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra."
"1745" 1998 ""
"1746" 1998 ""
"1747" 1998 ""
"1748" 1998 ""
"1749" 1998 "V. JOYS AND PASSIONS."
"1750" 1998 ""
"1751" 1998 "My brother, when thou hast a virtue, and it is thine own virtue, thou"
"1752" 1998 "hast it in common with no one."
"1753" 1998 ""
"1754" 1998 "To be sure, thou wouldst call it by name and caress it; thou wouldst"
"1755" 1998 "pull its ears and amuse thyself with it."
"1756" 1998 ""
"1757" 1998 "And lo! Then hast thou its name in common with the people, and hast"
"1758" 1998 "become one of the people and the herd with thy virtue!"
"1759" 1998 ""
"1760" 1998 "Better for thee to say: \"Ineffable is it, and nameless, that which is"
"1761" 1998 "pain and sweetness to my soul, and also the hunger of my bowels.\""
"1762" 1998 ""
"1763" 1998 "Let thy virtue be too high for the familiarity of names, and if thou"
"1764" 1998 "must speak of it, be not ashamed to stammer about it."
"1765" 1998 ""
"1766" 1998 "Thus speak and stammer: \"That is MY good, that do I love, thus doth it"
"1767" 1998 "please me entirely, thus only do _I_ desire the good."
"1768" 1998 ""
"1769" 1998 "Not as the law of a God do I desire it, not as a human law or a human"
"1770" 1998 "need do I desire it; it is not to be a guide-post for me to superearths"
"1771" 1998 "and paradises."
"1772" 1998 ""
"1773" 1998 "An earthly virtue is it which I love: little prudence is therein, and"
"1774" 1998 "the least everyday wisdom."
"1775" 1998 ""
"1776" 1998 "But that bird built its nest beside me: therefore, I love and cherish"
"1777" 1998 "it--now sitteth it beside me on its golden eggs.\""
"1778" 1998 ""
"1779" 1998 "Thus shouldst thou stammer, and praise thy virtue."
"1780" 1998 ""
"1781" 1998 "Once hadst thou passions and calledst them evil. But now hast thou only"
"1782" 1998 "thy virtues: they grew out of thy passions."
"1783" 1998 ""
"1784" 1998 "Thou implantedst thy highest aim into the heart of those passions: then"
"1785" 1998 "became they thy virtues and joys."
"1786" 1998 ""
"1787" 1998 "And though thou wert of the race of the hot-tempered, or of the"
"1788" 1998 "voluptuous, or of the fanatical, or the vindictive;"
"1789" 1998 ""
"1790" 1998 "All thy passions in the end became virtues, and all thy devils angels."
"1791" 1998 ""
"1792" 1998 "Once hadst thou wild dogs in thy cellar: but they changed at last into"
"1793" 1998 "birds and charming songstresses."
"1794" 1998 ""
"1795" 1998 "Out of thy poisons brewedst thou balsam for thyself; thy cow,"
"1796" 1998 "affliction, milkedst thou--now drinketh thou the sweet milk of her"
"1797" 1998 "udder."
"1798" 1998 ""
"1799" 1998 "And nothing evil groweth in thee any longer, unless it be the evil that"
"1800" 1998 "groweth out of the conflict of thy virtues."
"1801" 1998 ""
"1802" 1998 "My brother, if thou be fortunate, then wilt thou have one virtue and no"
"1803" 1998 "more: thus goest thou easier over the bridge."
"1804" 1998 ""
"1805" 1998 "Illustrious is it to have many virtues, but a hard lot; and many a one"
"1806" 1998 "hath gone into the wilderness and killed himself, because he was weary"
"1807" 1998 "of being the battle and battlefield of virtues."
"1808" 1998 ""
"1809" 1998 "My brother, are war and battle evil? Necessary, however, is the evil;"
"1810" 1998 "necessary are the envy and the distrust and the back-biting among the"
"1811" 1998 "virtues."
"1812" 1998 ""
"1813" 1998 "Lo! how each of thy virtues is covetous of the highest place; it wanteth"
"1814" 1998 "thy whole spirit to be ITS herald, it wanteth thy whole power, in wrath,"
"1815" 1998 "hatred, and love."
"1816" 1998 ""
"1817" 1998 "Jealous is every virtue of the others, and a dreadful thing is jealousy."
"1818" 1998 "Even virtues may succumb by jealousy."
"1819" 1998 ""
"1820" 1998 "He whom the flame of jealousy encompasseth, turneth at last, like the"
"1821" 1998 "scorpion, the poisoned sting against himself."
"1822" 1998 ""
"1823" 1998 "Ah! my brother, hast thou never seen a virtue backbite and stab itself?"
"1824" 1998 ""
"1825" 1998 "Man is something that hath to be surpassed: and therefore shalt thou"
"1826" 1998 "love thy virtues,--for thou wilt succumb by them.--"
"1827" 1998 ""
"1828" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra."
"1829" 1998 ""
"1830" 1998 ""
"1831" 1998 ""
"1832" 1998 ""
"1833" 1998 "VI. THE PALE CRIMINAL."
"1834" 1998 ""
"1835" 1998 "Ye do not mean to slay, ye judges and sacrificers, until the animal hath"
"1836" 1998 "bowed its head? Lo! the pale criminal hath bowed his head: out of his"
"1837" 1998 "eye speaketh the great contempt."
"1838" 1998 ""
"1839" 1998 "\"Mine ego is something which is to be surpassed: mine ego is to me the"
"1840" 1998 "great contempt of man\": so speaketh it out of that eye."
"1841" 1998 ""
"1842" 1998 "When he judged himself--that was his supreme moment; let not the exalted"
"1843" 1998 "one relapse again into his low estate!"
"1844" 1998 ""
"1845" 1998 "There is no salvation for him who thus suffereth from himself, unless it"
"1846" 1998 "be speedy death."
"1847" 1998 ""
"1848" 1998 "Your slaying, ye judges, shall be pity, and not revenge; and in that ye"
"1849" 1998 "slay, see to it that ye yourselves justify life!"
"1850" 1998 ""
"1851" 1998 "It is not enough that ye should reconcile with him whom ye slay. Let"
"1852" 1998 "your sorrow be love to the Superman: thus will ye justify your own"
"1853" 1998 "survival!"
"1854" 1998 ""
"1855" 1998 "\"Enemy\" shall ye say but not \"villain,\" \"invalid\" shall ye say but not"
"1856" 1998 "\"wretch,\" \"fool\" shall ye say but not \"sinner.\""
"1857" 1998 ""
"1858" 1998 "And thou, red judge, if thou would say audibly all thou hast done in"
"1859" 1998 "thought, then would every one cry: \"Away with the nastiness and the"
"1860" 1998 "virulent reptile!\""
"1861" 1998 ""
"1862" 1998 "But one thing is the thought, another thing is the deed, and another"
"1863" 1998 "thing is the idea of the deed. The wheel of causality doth not roll"
"1864" 1998 "between them."
"1865" 1998 ""
"1866" 1998 "An idea made this pale man pale. Adequate was he for his deed when he"
"1867" 1998 "did it, but the idea of it, he could not endure when it was done."
"1868" 1998 ""
"1869" 1998 "Evermore did he now see himself as the doer of one deed. Madness, I call"
"1870" 1998 "this: the exception reversed itself to the rule in him."
"1871" 1998 ""
"1872" 1998 "The streak of chalk bewitcheth the hen; the stroke he struck bewitched"
"1873" 1998 "his weak reason. Madness AFTER the deed, I call this."
"1874" 1998 ""
"1875" 1998 "Hearken, ye judges! There is another madness besides, and it is BEFORE"
"1876" 1998 "the deed. Ah! ye have not gone deep enough into this soul!"
"1877" 1998 ""
"1878" 1998 "Thus speaketh the red judge: \"Why did this criminal commit murder? He"
"1879" 1998 "meant to rob.\" I tell you, however, that his soul wanted blood, not"
"1880" 1998 "booty: he thirsted for the happiness of the knife!"
"1881" 1998 ""
"1882" 1998 "But his weak reason understood not this madness, and it persuaded him."
"1883" 1998 "\"What matter about blood!\" it said; \"wishest thou not, at least, to make"
"1884" 1998 "booty thereby? Or take revenge?\""
"1885" 1998 ""
"1886" 1998 "And he hearkened unto his weak reason: like lead lay its words upon"
"1887" 1998 "him--thereupon he robbed when he murdered. He did not mean to be"
"1888" 1998 "ashamed of his madness."
"1889" 1998 ""
"1890" 1998 "And now once more lieth the lead of his guilt upon him, and once more is"
"1891" 1998 "his weak reason so benumbed, so paralysed, and so dull."
"1892" 1998 ""
"1893" 1998 "Could he only shake his head, then would his burden roll off; but who"
"1894" 1998 "shaketh that head?"
"1895" 1998 ""
"1896" 1998 "What is this man? A mass of diseases that reach out into the world"
"1897" 1998 "through the spirit; there they want to get their prey."
"1898" 1998 ""
"1899" 1998 "What is this man? A coil of wild serpents that are seldom at peace among"
"1900" 1998 "themselves--so they go forth apart and seek prey in the world."
"1901" 1998 ""
"1902" 1998 "Look at that poor body! What it suffered and craved, the poor soul"
"1903" 1998 "interpreted to itself--it interpreted it as murderous desire, and"
"1904" 1998 "eagerness for the happiness of the knife."
"1905" 1998 ""
"1906" 1998 "Him who now turneth sick, the evil overtaketh which is now the evil: he"
"1907" 1998 "seeketh to cause pain with that which causeth him pain. But there have"
"1908" 1998 "been other ages, and another evil and good."
"1909" 1998 ""
"1910" 1998 "Once was doubt evil, and the will to Self. Then the invalid became a"
"1911" 1998 "heretic or sorcerer; as heretic or sorcerer he suffered, and sought to"
"1912" 1998 "cause suffering."
"1913" 1998 ""
"1914" 1998 "But this will not enter your ears; it hurteth your good people, ye tell"
"1915" 1998 "me. But what doth it matter to me about your good people!"
"1916" 1998 ""
"1917" 1998 "Many things in your good people cause me disgust, and verily, not their"
"1918" 1998 "evil. I would that they had a madness by which they succumbed, like this"
"1919" 1998 "pale criminal!"
"1920" 1998 ""
"1921" 1998 "Verily, I would that their madness were called truth, or fidelity,"
"1922" 1998 "or justice: but they have their virtue in order to live long, and in"
"1923" 1998 "wretched self-complacency."
"1924" 1998 ""
"1925" 1998 "I am a railing alongside the torrent; whoever is able to grasp me may"
"1926" 1998 "grasp me! Your crutch, however, I am not.--"
"1927" 1998 ""
"1928" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra."
"1929" 1998 ""
"1930" 1998 ""
"1931" 1998 ""
"1932" 1998 ""
"1933" 1998 "VII. READING AND WRITING."
"1934" 1998 ""
"1935" 1998 "Of all that is written, I love only what a person hath written with his"
"1936" 1998 "blood. Write with blood, and thou wilt find that blood is spirit."
"1937" 1998 ""
"1938" 1998 "It is no easy task to understand unfamiliar blood; I hate the reading"
"1939" 1998 "idlers."
"1940" 1998 ""
"1941" 1998 "He who knoweth the reader, doeth nothing more for the reader. Another"
"1942" 1998 "century of readers--and spirit itself will stink."
"1943" 1998 ""
"1944" 1998 "Every one being allowed to learn to read, ruineth in the long run not"
"1945" 1998 "only writing but also thinking."
"1946" 1998 ""
"1947" 1998 "Once spirit was God, then it became man, and now it even becometh"
"1948" 1998 "populace."
"1949" 1998 ""
"1950" 1998 "He that writeth in blood and proverbs doth not want to be read, but"
"1951" 1998 "learnt by heart."
"1952" 1998 ""
"1953" 1998 "In the mountains the shortest way is from peak to peak, but for that"
"1954" 1998 "route thou must have long legs. Proverbs should be peaks, and those"
"1955" 1998 "spoken to should be big and tall."
"1956" 1998 ""
"1957" 1998 "The atmosphere rare and pure, danger near and the spirit full of a"
"1958" 1998 "joyful wickedness: thus are things well matched."
"1959" 1998 ""
"1960" 1998 "I want to have goblins about me, for I am courageous. The courage which"
"1961" 1998 "scareth away ghosts, createth for itself goblins--it wanteth to laugh."
"1962" 1998 ""
"1963" 1998 "I no longer feel in common with you; the very cloud which I see"
"1964" 1998 "beneath me, the blackness and heaviness at which I laugh--that is your"
"1965" 1998 "thunder-cloud."
"1966" 1998 ""
"1967" 1998 "Ye look aloft when ye long for exaltation; and I look downward because I"
"1968" 1998 "am exalted."
"1969" 1998 ""
"1970" 1998 "Who among you can at the same time laugh and be exalted?"
"1971" 1998 ""
"1972" 1998 "He who climbeth on the highest mountains, laugheth at all tragic plays"
"1973" 1998 "and tragic realities."
"1974" 1998 ""
"1975" 1998 "Courageous, unconcerned, scornful, coercive--so wisdom wisheth us; she"
"1976" 1998 "is a woman, and ever loveth only a warrior."
"1977" 1998 ""
"1978" 1998 "Ye tell me, \"Life is hard to bear.\" But for what purpose should ye have"
"1979" 1998 "your pride in the morning and your resignation in the evening?"
"1980" 1998 ""
"1981" 1998 "Life is hard to bear: but do not affect to be so delicate! We are all of"
"1982" 1998 "us fine sumpter asses and assesses."
"1983" 1998 ""
