"10093" 1998 "FOURTH AND LAST PART."
"10094" 1998 ""
"10095" 1998 "Ah, where in the world have there been greater follies than with the"
"10096" 1998 "pitiful? And what in the world hath caused more suffering than the"
"10097" 1998 "follies of the pitiful?"
"10098" 1998 ""
"10099" 1998 "Woe unto all loving ones who have not an elevation which is above their"
"10100" 1998 "pity!"
"10101" 1998 ""
"10102" 1998 "Thus spake the devil unto me, once on a time: \"Even God hath his hell:"
"10103" 1998 "it is his love for man.\""
"10104" 1998 ""
"10105" 1998 "And lately did I hear him say these words: \"God is dead: of his pity for"
"10106" 1998 "man hath God died.\"--ZARATHUSTRA, II., \"The Pitiful.\""
"10107" 1998 ""
"10108" 1998 ""
"10109" 1998 ""
"10110" 1998 ""
"10111" 1998 "LXI. THE HONEY SACRIFICE."
"10112" 1998 ""
"10113" 1998 "--And again passed moons and years over Zarathustra's soul, and he"
"10114" 1998 "heeded it not; his hair, however, became white. One day when he sat on"
"10115" 1998 "a stone in front of his cave, and gazed calmly into the distance--one"
"10116" 1998 "there gazeth out on the sea, and away beyond sinuous abysses,--then went"
"10117" 1998 "his animals thoughtfully round about him, and at last set themselves in"
"10118" 1998 "front of him."
"10119" 1998 ""
"10120" 1998 "\"O Zarathustra,\" said they, \"gazest thou out perhaps for thy"
"10121" 1998 "happiness?\"--\"Of what account is my happiness!\" answered he, \"I have"
"10122" 1998 "long ceased to strive any more for happiness, I strive for my work.\"--\"O"
"10123" 1998 "Zarathustra,\" said the animals once more, \"that sayest thou as one"
"10124" 1998 "who hath overmuch of good things. Liest thou not in a sky-blue lake of"
"10125" 1998 "happiness?\"--\"Ye wags,\" answered Zarathustra, and smiled, \"how well did"
"10126" 1998 "ye choose the simile! But ye know also that my happiness is heavy, and"
"10127" 1998 "not like a fluid wave of water: it presseth me and will not leave me,"
"10128" 1998 "and is like molten pitch.\"--"
"10129" 1998 ""
"10130" 1998 "Then went his animals again thoughtfully around him, and placed"
"10131" 1998 "themselves once more in front of him. \"O Zarathustra,\" said they, \"it is"
"10132" 1998 "consequently FOR THAT REASON that thou thyself always becometh yellower"
"10133" 1998 "and darker, although thy hair looketh white and flaxen? Lo, thou sittest"
"10134" 1998 "in thy pitch!\"--\"What do ye say, mine animals?\" said Zarathustra,"
"10135" 1998 "laughing; \"verily I reviled when I spake of pitch. As it happeneth with"
"10136" 1998 "me, so is it with all fruits that turn ripe. It is the HONEY in my veins"
"10137" 1998 "that maketh my blood thicker, and also my soul stiller.\"--\"So will it"
"10138" 1998 "be, O Zarathustra,\" answered his animals, and pressed up to him; \"but"
"10139" 1998 "wilt thou not to-day ascend a high mountain? The air is pure, and to-day"
"10140" 1998 "one seeth more of the world than ever.\"--\"Yea, mine animals,\" answered"
"10141" 1998 "he, \"ye counsel admirably and according to my heart: I will to-day"
"10142" 1998 "ascend a high mountain! But see that honey is there ready to hand,"
"10143" 1998 "yellow, white, good, ice-cool, golden-comb-honey. For know that when"
"10144" 1998 "aloft I will make the honey-sacrifice.\"--"
"10145" 1998 ""
"10146" 1998 "When Zarathustra, however, was aloft on the summit, he sent his animals"
"10147" 1998 "home that had accompanied him, and found that he was now alone:--then he"
"10148" 1998 "laughed from the bottom of his heart, looked around him, and spake thus:"
"10149" 1998 ""
"10150" 1998 "That I spake of sacrifices and honey-sacrifices, it was merely a ruse"
"10151" 1998 "in talking and verily, a useful folly! Here aloft can I now speak freer"
"10152" 1998 "than in front of mountain-caves and anchorites' domestic animals."
"10153" 1998 ""
"10154" 1998 "What to sacrifice! I squander what is given me, a squanderer with a"
"10155" 1998 "thousand hands: how could I call that--sacrificing?"
"10156" 1998 ""
"10157" 1998 "And when I desired honey I only desired bait, and sweet mucus and"
"10158" 1998 "mucilage, for which even the mouths of growling bears, and strange,"
"10159" 1998 "sulky, evil birds, water:"
"10160" 1998 ""
"10161" 1998 "--The best bait, as huntsmen and fishermen require it. For if the world"
"10162" 1998 "be as a gloomy forest of animals, and a pleasure-ground for all wild"
"10163" 1998 "huntsmen, it seemeth to me rather--and preferably--a fathomless, rich"
"10164" 1998 "sea;"
"10165" 1998 ""
"10166" 1998 "--A sea full of many-hued fishes and crabs, for which even the Gods"
"10167" 1998 "might long, and might be tempted to become fishers in it, and casters of"
"10168" 1998 "nets,--so rich is the world in wonderful things, great and small!"
"10169" 1998 ""
"10170" 1998 "Especially the human world, the human sea:--towards IT do I now throw"
"10171" 1998 "out my golden angle-rod and say: Open up, thou human abyss!"
"10172" 1998 ""
"10173" 1998 "Open up, and throw unto me thy fish and shining crabs! With my best bait"
"10174" 1998 "shall I allure to myself to-day the strangest human fish!"
"10175" 1998 ""
"10176" 1998 "--My happiness itself do I throw out into all places far and wide 'twixt"
"10177" 1998 "orient, noontide, and occident, to see if many human fish will not learn"
"10178" 1998 "to hug and tug at my happiness;--"
"10179" 1998 ""
"10180" 1998 "Until, biting at my sharp hidden hooks, they have to come up unto MY"
"10181" 1998 "height, the motleyest abyss-groundlings, to the wickedest of all fishers"
"10182" 1998 "of men."
"10183" 1998 ""
"10184" 1998 "For THIS am I from the heart and from the beginning--drawing,"
"10185" 1998 "hither-drawing, upward-drawing, upbringing; a drawer, a trainer, a"
"10186" 1998 "training-master, who not in vain counselled himself once on a time:"
"10187" 1998 "\"Become what thou art!\""
"10188" 1998 ""
"10189" 1998 "Thus may men now come UP to me; for as yet do I await the signs that it"
"10190" 1998 "is time for my down-going; as yet do I not myself go down, as I must do,"
"10191" 1998 "amongst men."
"10192" 1998 ""
"10193" 1998 "Therefore do I here wait, crafty and scornful upon high mountains,"
"10194" 1998 "no impatient one, no patient one; rather one who hath even unlearnt"
"10195" 1998 "patience,--because he no longer \"suffereth.\""
"10196" 1998 ""
"10197" 1998 "For my fate giveth me time: it hath forgotten me perhaps? Or doth it sit"
"10198" 1998 "behind a big stone and catch flies?"
"10199" 1998 ""
"10200" 1998 "And verily, I am well-disposed to mine eternal fate, because it doth not"
"10201" 1998 "hound and hurry me, but leaveth me time for merriment and mischief; so"
"10202" 1998 "that I have to-day ascended this high mountain to catch fish."
"10203" 1998 ""
"10204" 1998 "Did ever any one catch fish upon high mountains? And though it be a"
"10205" 1998 "folly what I here seek and do, it is better so than that down below I"
"10206" 1998 "should become solemn with waiting, and green and yellow--"
"10207" 1998 ""
"10208" 1998 "--A posturing wrath-snorter with waiting, a holy howl-storm from"
"10209" 1998 "the mountains, an impatient one that shouteth down into the valleys:"
"10210" 1998 "\"Hearken, else I will scourge you with the scourge of God!\""
"10211" 1998 ""
"10212" 1998 "Not that I would have a grudge against such wrathful ones on that"
"10213" 1998 "account: they are well enough for laughter to me! Impatient must they"
"10214" 1998 "now be, those big alarm-drums, which find a voice now or never!"
"10215" 1998 ""
"10216" 1998 "Myself, however, and my fate--we do not talk to the Present, neither"
"10217" 1998 "do we talk to the Never: for talking we have patience and time and more"
"10218" 1998 "than time. For one day must it yet come, and may not pass by."
"10219" 1998 ""
"10220" 1998 "What must one day come and may not pass by? Our great Hazar, that is"
"10221" 1998 "to say, our great, remote human-kingdom, the Zarathustra-kingdom of a"
"10222" 1998 "thousand years--"
"10223" 1998 ""
"10224" 1998 "How remote may such \"remoteness\" be? What doth it concern me? But on"
"10225" 1998 "that account it is none the less sure unto me--, with both feet stand I"
"10226" 1998 "secure on this ground;"
"10227" 1998 ""
"10228" 1998 "--On an eternal ground, on hard primary rock, on this highest, hardest,"
"10229" 1998 "primary mountain-ridge, unto which all winds come, as unto the"
"10230" 1998 "storm-parting, asking Where? and Whence? and Whither?"
"10231" 1998 ""
"10232" 1998 "Here laugh, laugh, my hearty, healthy wickedness! From high mountains"
"10233" 1998 "cast down thy glittering scorn-laughter! Allure for me with thy"
"10234" 1998 "glittering the finest human fish!"
"10235" 1998 ""
"10236" 1998 "And whatever belongeth unto ME in all seas, my in-and-for-me in all"
"10237" 1998 "things--fish THAT out for me, bring THAT up to me: for that do I wait,"
"10238" 1998 "the wickedest of all fish-catchers."
"10239" 1998 ""
"10240" 1998 "Out! out! my fishing-hook! In and down, thou bait of my happiness! Drip"
"10241" 1998 "thy sweetest dew, thou honey of my heart! Bite, my fishing-hook, into"
"10242" 1998 "the belly of all black affliction!"
"10243" 1998 ""
"10244" 1998 "Look out, look out, mine eye! Oh, how many seas round about me, what"
"10245" 1998 "dawning human futures! And above me--what rosy red stillness! What"
"10246" 1998 "unclouded silence!"
"10247" 1998 ""
"10248" 1998 ""
"10249" 1998 ""
"10250" 1998 ""
"10251" 1998 "LXII. THE CRY OF DISTRESS."
"10252" 1998 ""
"10253" 1998 "The next day sat Zarathustra again on the stone in front of his cave,"
"10254" 1998 "whilst his animals roved about in the world outside to bring home new"
"10255" 1998 "food,--also new honey: for Zarathustra had spent and wasted the old"
"10256" 1998 "honey to the very last particle. When he thus sat, however, with a"
"10257" 1998 "stick in his hand, tracing the shadow of his figure on the earth, and"
"10258" 1998 "reflecting--verily! not upon himself and his shadow,--all at once he"
"10259" 1998 "startled and shrank back: for he saw another shadow beside his own."
"10260" 1998 "And when he hastily looked around and stood up, behold, there stood the"
"10261" 1998 "soothsayer beside him, the same whom he had once given to eat and drink"
"10262" 1998 "at his table, the proclaimer of the great weariness, who taught: \"All is"
"10263" 1998 "alike, nothing is worth while, the world is without meaning, knowledge"
"10264" 1998 "strangleth.\" But his face had changed since then; and when Zarathustra"
"10265" 1998 "looked into his eyes, his heart was startled once more: so much evil"
"10266" 1998 "announcement and ashy-grey lightnings passed over that countenance."
"10267" 1998 ""
"10268" 1998 "The soothsayer, who had perceived what went on in Zarathustra's soul,"
"10269" 1998 "wiped his face with his hand, as if he would wipe out the impression;"
"10270" 1998 "the same did also Zarathustra. And when both of them had thus silently"
"10271" 1998 "composed and strengthened themselves, they gave each other the hand, as"
"10272" 1998 "a token that they wanted once more to recognise each other."
"10273" 1998 ""
"10274" 1998 "\"Welcome hither,\" said Zarathustra, \"thou soothsayer of the great"
"10275" 1998 "weariness, not in vain shalt thou once have been my messmate and guest."
"10276" 1998 "Eat and drink also with me to-day, and forgive it that a cheerful old"
"10277" 1998 "man sitteth with thee at table!\"--\"A cheerful old man?\" answered the"
"10278" 1998 "soothsayer, shaking his head, \"but whoever thou art, or wouldst be, O"
"10279" 1998 "Zarathustra, thou hast been here aloft the longest time,--in a little"
"10280" 1998 "while thy bark shall no longer rest on dry land!\"--\"Do I then rest"
"10281" 1998 "on dry land?\"--asked Zarathustra, laughing.--\"The waves around thy"
"10282" 1998 "mountain,\" answered the soothsayer, \"rise and rise, the waves of great"
"10283" 1998 "distress and affliction: they will soon raise thy bark also and carry"
"10284" 1998 "thee away.\"--Thereupon was Zarathustra silent and wondered.--\"Dost thou"
"10285" 1998 "still hear nothing?\" continued the soothsayer: \"doth it not rush and"
"10286" 1998 "roar out of the depth?\"--Zarathustra was silent once more and listened:"
"10287" 1998 "then heard he a long, long cry, which the abysses threw to one another"
"10288" 1998 "and passed on; for none of them wished to retain it: so evil did it"
"10289" 1998 "sound."
"10290" 1998 ""
"10291" 1998 "\"Thou ill announcer,\" said Zarathustra at last, \"that is a cry of"
"10292" 1998 "distress, and the cry of a man; it may come perhaps out of a black sea."
"10293" 1998 "But what doth human distress matter to me! My last sin which hath been"
"10294" 1998 "reserved for me,--knowest thou what it is called?\""
"10295" 1998 ""
"10296" 1998 "--\"PITY!\" answered the soothsayer from an overflowing heart, and raised"
"10297" 1998 "both his hands aloft--\"O Zarathustra, I have come that I may seduce thee"
"10298" 1998 "to thy last sin!\"--"
"10299" 1998 ""
"10300" 1998 "And hardly had those words been uttered when there sounded the cry"
"10301" 1998 "once more, and longer and more alarming than before--also much nearer."
"10302" 1998 "\"Hearest thou? Hearest thou, O Zarathustra?\" called out the soothsayer,"
"10303" 1998 "\"the cry concerneth thee, it calleth thee: Come, come, come; it is time,"
"10304" 1998 "it is the highest time!\"--"
"10305" 1998 ""
"10306" 1998 "Zarathustra was silent thereupon, confused and staggered; at last he"
"10307" 1998 "asked, like one who hesitateth in himself: \"And who is it that there"
"10308" 1998 "calleth me?\""
"10309" 1998 ""
"10310" 1998 "\"But thou knowest it, certainly,\" answered the soothsayer warmly, \"why"
"10311" 1998 "dost thou conceal thyself? It is THE HIGHER MAN that crieth for thee!\""
"10312" 1998 ""
"10313" 1998 "\"The higher man?\" cried Zarathustra, horror-stricken: \"what wanteth HE?"
"10314" 1998 "What wanteth HE? The higher man! What wanteth he here?\"--and his skin"
"10315" 1998 "covered with perspiration."
"10316" 1998 ""
"10317" 1998 "The soothsayer, however, did not heed Zarathustra's alarm, but listened"
"10318" 1998 "and listened in the downward direction. When, however, it had been still"
"10319" 1998 "there for a long while, he looked behind, and saw Zarathustra standing"
"10320" 1998 "trembling."
"10321" 1998 ""
"10322" 1998 "\"O Zarathustra,\" he began, with sorrowful voice, \"thou dost not stand"
"10323" 1998 "there like one whose happiness maketh him giddy: thou wilt have to dance"
"10324" 1998 "lest thou tumble down!"
"10325" 1998 ""
"10326" 1998 "But although thou shouldst dance before me, and leap all thy side-leaps,"
"10327" 1998 "no one may say unto me: 'Behold, here danceth the last joyous man!'"
"10328" 1998 ""
"10329" 1998 "In vain would any one come to this height who sought HIM here: caves"
"10330" 1998 "would he find, indeed, and back-caves, hiding-places for hidden ones;"
"10331" 1998 "but not lucky mines, nor treasure-chambers, nor new gold-veins of"
"10332" 1998 "happiness."
"10333" 1998 ""
"10334" 1998 "Happiness--how indeed could one find happiness among such buried-alive"
"10335" 1998 "and solitary ones! Must I yet seek the last happiness on the Happy"
"10336" 1998 "Isles, and far away among forgotten seas?"
"10337" 1998 ""
"10338" 1998 "But all is alike, nothing is worth while, no seeking is of service,"
"10339" 1998 "there are no longer any Happy Isles!\"--"
"10340" 1998 ""
"10341" 1998 "Thus sighed the soothsayer; with his last sigh, however, Zarathustra"
"10342" 1998 "again became serene and assured, like one who hath come out of a deep"
"10343" 1998 "chasm into the light. \"Nay! Nay! Three times Nay!\" exclaimed he with a"
"10344" 1998 "strong voice, and stroked his beard--\"THAT do I know better! There are"
"10345" 1998 "still Happy Isles! Silence THEREON, thou sighing sorrow-sack!"
"10346" 1998 ""
"10347" 1998 "Cease to splash THEREON, thou rain-cloud of the forenoon! Do I not"
"10348" 1998 "already stand here wet with thy misery, and drenched like a dog?"
"10349" 1998 ""
"10350" 1998 "Now do I shake myself and run away from thee, that I may again become"
"10351" 1998 "dry: thereat mayest thou not wonder! Do I seem to thee discourteous?"
"10352" 1998 "Here however is MY court."
"10353" 1998 ""
"10354" 1998 "But as regards the higher man: well! I shall seek him at once in those"
"10355" 1998 "forests: FROM THENCE came his cry. Perhaps he is there hard beset by an"
"10356" 1998 "evil beast."
"10357" 1998 ""
"10358" 1998 "He is in MY domain: therein shall he receive no scath! And verily, there"
"10359" 1998 "are many evil beasts about me.\"--"
"10360" 1998 ""
"10361" 1998 "With those words Zarathustra turned around to depart. Then said the"
"10362" 1998 "soothsayer: \"O Zarathustra, thou art a rogue!"
"10363" 1998 ""
"10364" 1998 "I know it well: thou wouldst fain be rid of me! Rather wouldst thou run"
"10365" 1998 "into the forest and lay snares for evil beasts!"
"10366" 1998 ""
"10367" 1998 "But what good will it do thee? In the evening wilt thou have me again:"
"10368" 1998 "in thine own cave will I sit, patient and heavy like a block--and wait"
"10369" 1998 "for thee!\""
"10370" 1998 ""
"10371" 1998 "\"So be it!\" shouted back Zarathustra, as he went away: \"and what is mine"
"10372" 1998 "in my cave belongeth also unto thee, my guest!"
"10373" 1998 ""
"10374" 1998 "Shouldst thou however find honey therein, well! just lick it up, thou"
"10375" 1998 "growling bear, and sweeten thy soul! For in the evening we want both to"
"10376" 1998 "be in good spirits;"
"10377" 1998 ""
"10378" 1998 "--In good spirits and joyful, because this day hath come to an end! And"
"10379" 1998 "thou thyself shalt dance to my lays, as my dancing-bear."
"10380" 1998 ""
"10381" 1998 "Thou dost not believe this? Thou shakest thy head? Well! Cheer up, old"
"10382" 1998 "bear! But I also--am a soothsayer.\""
"10383" 1998 ""
"10384" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra."
"10385" 1998 ""
"10386" 1998 ""
"10387" 1998 ""
"10388" 1998 ""
"10389" 1998 "LXIII. TALK WITH THE KINGS."
"10390" 1998 ""
"10391" 1998 "1."
"10392" 1998 ""
"10393" 1998 "Ere Zarathustra had been an hour on his way in the mountains and"
"10394" 1998 "forests, he saw all at once a strange procession. Right on the path"
"10395" 1998 "which he was about to descend came two kings walking, bedecked with"
"10396" 1998 "crowns and purple girdles, and variegated like flamingoes: they drove"
"10397" 1998 "before them a laden ass. \"What do these kings want in my domain?\" said"
"10398" 1998 "Zarathustra in astonishment to his heart, and hid himself hastily behind"
"10399" 1998 "a thicket. When however the kings approached to him, he said half-aloud,"
"10400" 1998 "like one speaking only to himself: \"Strange! Strange! How doth this"

"10402" 1998 ""

"10559" 1998 "LXIV. THE LEECH."
"10560" 1998 ""
"10561" 1998 "And Zarathustra went thoughtfully on, further and lower down, through"
"10562" 1998 "forests and past moory bottoms; as it happeneth, however, to every one"
"10563" 1998 "who meditateth upon hard matters, he trod thereby unawares upon a man."
"10564" 1998 "And lo, there spurted into his face all at once a cry of pain, and two"

"10567" 1998 "he regained his composure, and his heart laughed at the folly he had"
"10568" 1998 "just committed."
"10569" 1998 ""
"10570" 1998 "\"Pardon me,\" said he to the trodden one, who had got up enraged, and had"

"10572" 1998 ""
"10573" 1998 "As a wanderer who dreameth of remote things on a lonesome highway,"

"10575" 1998 ""

"10581" 1998 ""

"10585" 1998 "Lo! am I then a dog?\"--And thereupon the sitting one got up, and pulled"
"10586" 1998 "his naked arm out of the swamp. For at first he had lain outstretched"
"10587" 1998 "on the ground, hidden and indiscernible, like those who lie in wait for"
"10588" 1998 "swamp-game."
"10589" 1998 ""
"10590" 1998 "\"But whatever art thou about!\" called out Zarathustra in alarm, for he"
"10591" 1998 "saw a deal of blood streaming over the naked arm,--\"what hath hurt thee?"
"10592" 1998 "Hath an evil beast bit thee, thou unfortunate one?\""
"10593" 1998 ""

"10601" 1998 "and therein shall no one receive any hurt."
"10602" 1998 ""

"10605" 1998 ""

"10608" 1998 ""
"10609" 1998 "It hath gone badly with thee, thou unfortunate one, in this life: first"

"10611" 1998 ""

"10616" 1998 ""

"10638" 1998 ""
"10639" 1998 "--What matter if it be great or small? If it be called swamp or sky?"

"10642" 1998 ""

"10649" 1998 ""

"10700" 1998 "LXV. THE MAGICIAN."
"10701" 1998 ""
"10702" 1998 "1."
"10703" 1998 ""

"10837" 1998 "his staff and struck the wailer with all his might. \"Stop this,\" cried"

"10839" 1998 "false coiner! Thou liar from the very heart! I know thee well!"
"10840" 1998 ""
"10841" 1998 "I will soon make warm legs to thee, thou evil magician: I know well"
"10842" 1998 "how--to make it hot for such as thou!\""
"10843" 1998 ""
"10844" 1998 "--\"Leave off,\" said the old man, and sprang up from the ground, \"strike"
"10845" 1998 "me no more, O Zarathustra! I did it only for amusement!"
"10846" 1998 ""
"10847" 1998 "That kind of thing belongeth to mine art. Thee thyself, I wanted to put"
"10848" 1998 "to the proof when I gave this performance. And verily, thou hast well"
"10849" 1998 "detected me!"
"10850" 1998 ""
"10851" 1998 "But thou thyself--hast given me no small proof of thyself: thou art"
"10852" 1998 "HARD, thou wise Zarathustra! Hard strikest thou with thy 'truths,' thy"
"10853" 1998 "cudgel forceth from me--THIS truth!\""
"10854" 1998 ""
"10855" 1998 "--\"Flatter not,\" answered Zarathustra, still excited and frowning,"
"10856" 1998 "\"thou stage-player from the heart! Thou art false: why speakest thou--of"
"10857" 1998 "truth!"
"10858" 1998 ""
"10859" 1998 "Thou peacock of peacocks, thou sea of vanity; WHAT didst thou represent"
"10860" 1998 "before me, thou evil magician; WHOM was I meant to believe in when thou"
"10861" 1998 "wailedst in such wise?\""
"10862" 1998 ""
"10863" 1998 "\"THE PENITENT IN SPIRIT,\" said the old man, \"it was him--I represented;"
"10864" 1998 "thou thyself once devisedst this expression--"
"10865" 1998 ""
"10866" 1998 "--The poet and magician who at last turneth his spirit against himself,"
"10867" 1998 "the transformed one who freezeth to death by his bad science and"
"10868" 1998 "conscience."
"10869" 1998 ""
"10870" 1998 "And just acknowledge it: it was long, O Zarathustra, before thou"

"10873" 1998 ""

"10876" 1998 ""
"10877" 1998 "\"Thou mayest have deceived subtler ones than I,\" said Zarathustra"

"10880" 1998 ""

"10899" 1998 ""

"10904" 1998 ""

"10908" 1998 ""
"10909" 1998 "A great man I wanted to appear, and persuaded many; but the lie hath"

"10911" 1998 ""

"10918" 1998 ""

"10922" 1998 ""

"10925" 1998 ""

"10928" 1998 ""
"10929" 1998 "--Wherein didst thou put ME to the test?\""
"10930" 1998 ""
"10931" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra, and his eyes sparkled. But the old magician kept"

"10934" 1998 ""
"10935" 1998 "O Zarathustra, I seek a genuine one, a right one, a simple one, an"

"10937" 1998 "of knowledge, a great man!"
"10938" 1998 ""

"10940" 1998 ""
"10941" 1998 "--And here there arose a long silence between them: Zarathustra,"

"10948" 1998 ""

"10951" 1998 ""

"10955" 1998 ""
"10956" 1998 "Many a one have I found who stretched and inflated himself, and the"

"10958" 1998 "The wind cometh out at last."
"10959" 1998 ""
"10960" 1998 "At last bursteth the frog which hath inflated itself too long: then"
"10961" 1998 "cometh out the wind. To prick a swollen one in the belly, I call good"

"10963" 1998 ""

"10967" 1998 ""

"10970" 1998 "me?\"--"
"10971" 1998 ""
"10972" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra, comforted in his heart, and went laughing on his"

"10975" 1998 ""

"10977" 1998 ""
"10978" 1998 "LXVI. OUT OF SERVICE."
"10979" 1998 ""
"10980" 1998 "Not long, however, after Zarathustra had freed himself from the"
"10981" 1998 "magician, he again saw a person sitting beside the path which he"
"10982" 1998 "followed, namely a tall, black man, with a haggard, pale countenance:"
"10983" 1998 "THIS MAN grieved him exceedingly. \"Alas,\" said he to his heart, \"there"
"10984" 1998 "sitteth disguised affliction; methinketh he is of the type of the"
"10985" 1998 "priests: what do THEY want in my domain?"
"10986" 1998 ""
"10987" 1998 "What! Hardly have I escaped from that magician, and must another"
"10988" 1998 "necromancer again run across my path,--"
"10989" 1998 ""
"10990" 1998 "--Some sorcerer with laying-on-of-hands, some sombre wonder-worker by"

"10992" 1998 "take!"
"10993" 1998 ""
"10994" 1998 "But the devil is never at the place which would be his right place: he"

"10996" 1998 ""

"11000" 1998 "perceived him; and not unlike one whom an unexpected happiness"

"11002" 1998 "Zarathustra."
"11003" 1998 ""
"11004" 1998 "\"Whoever thou art, thou traveller,\" said he, \"help a strayed one, a"
"11005" 1998 "seeker, an old man, who may here easily come to grief!"
"11006" 1998 ""
"11007" 1998 "The world here is strange to me, and remote; wild beasts also did I hear"
"11008" 1998 "howling; and he who could have given me protection--he is himself no"
"11009" 1998 "more."
"11010" 1998 ""
"11011" 1998 "I was seeking the pious man, a saint and an anchorite, who, alone in his"
"11012" 1998 "forest, had not yet heard of what all the world knoweth at present.\""
"11013" 1998 ""
"11014" 1998 "\"WHAT doth all the world know at present?\" asked Zarathustra. \"Perhaps"
"11015" 1998 "that the old God no longer liveth, in whom all the world once believed?\""
"11016" 1998 ""
"11017" 1998 "\"Thou sayest it,\" answered the old man sorrowfully. \"And I served that"
"11018" 1998 "old God until his last hour."
"11019" 1998 ""
"11020" 1998 "Now, however, am I out of service, without master, and yet not free;"
"11021" 1998 "likewise am I no longer merry even for an hour, except it be in"
"11022" 1998 "recollections."
"11023" 1998 ""
"11024" 1998 "Therefore did I ascend into these mountains, that I might finally have"
"11025" 1998 "a festival for myself once more, as becometh an old pope and"
"11026" 1998 "church-father: for know it, that I am the last pope!--a festival of"
"11027" 1998 "pious recollections and divine services."
"11028" 1998 ""
"11029" 1998 "Now, however, is he himself dead, the most pious of men, the saint in"
"11030" 1998 "the forest, who praised his God constantly with singing and mumbling."
"11031" 1998 ""
"11032" 1998 "He himself found I no longer when I found his cot--but two wolves found"

"11035" 1998 ""
"11036" 1998 "Had I thus come in vain into these forests and mountains? Then did my"

"11038" 1998 "those who believe not in God--, my heart determined that I should seek"
"11039" 1998 "Zarathustra!\""
"11040" 1998 ""
"11041" 1998 "Thus spake the hoary man, and gazed with keen eyes at him who stood"

"11044" 1998 ""
"11045" 1998 "\"Lo! thou venerable one,\" said he then, \"what a fine and long hand! That"

"11047" 1998 "it hold fast him whom thou seekest, me, Zarathustra."
"11048" 1998 ""
"11049" 1998 "It is I, the ungodly Zarathustra, who saith: 'Who is ungodlier than I,"

"11051" 1998 ""

"11054" 1998 ""

"11056" 1998 ""

"11059" 1998 ""

"11063" 1998 ""

"11066" 1998 ""
"11067" 1998 "The old pope however did not answer, but looked aside timidly, with a"

"11069" 1998 ""

"11072" 1998 ""

"11076" 1998 ""

"11080" 1998 ""

"11084" 1998 ""
"11085" 1998 "He was a hidden God, full of secrecy. Verily, he did not come by his"

"11088" 1998 ""
"11089" 1998 "Whoever extolleth him as a God of love, doth not think highly enough of"

"11092" 1998 ""
"11093" 1998 "When he was young, that God out of the Orient, then was he harsh and"

"11095" 1998 ""
"11096" 1998 "At last, however, he became old and soft and mellow and pitiful,"
"11097" 1998 "more like a grandfather than a father, but most like a tottering old"

"11099" 1998 ""

"11103" 1998 ""

"11107" 1998 ""

"11111" 1998 ""
"11112" 1998 "I love everything that looketh bright and speaketh honestly. But"
"11113" 1998 "he--thou knowest it, forsooth, thou old priest, there was something of"
"11114" 1998 "thy type in him, the priest-type--he was equivocal."
"11115" 1998 ""
"11116" 1998 "He was also indistinct. How he raged at us, this wrath-snorter, because"
"11117" 1998 "we understood him badly! But why did he not speak more clearly?"
"11118" 1998 ""
"11119" 1998 "And if the fault lay in our ears, why did he give us ears that heard him"
"11120" 1998 "badly? If there was dirt in our ears, well! who put it in them?"
"11121" 1998 ""
"11122" 1998 "Too much miscarried with him, this potter who had not learned"
"11123" 1998 "thoroughly! That he took revenge on his pots and creations, however,"
"11124" 1998 "because they turned out badly--that was a sin against GOOD TASTE."
"11125" 1998 ""
"11126" 1998 "There is also good taste in piety: THIS at last said: 'Away with SUCH"
"11127" 1998 "a God! Better to have no God, better to set up destiny on one's own"
"11128" 1998 "account, better to be a fool, better to be God oneself!'\""
"11129" 1998 ""
"11130" 1998 "--\"What do I hear!\" said then the old pope, with intent ears; \"O"
"11131" 1998 "Zarathustra, thou art more pious than thou believest, with such an"
"11132" 1998 "unbelief! Some God in thee hath converted thee to thine ungodliness."
"11133" 1998 ""
"11134" 1998 "Is it not thy piety itself which no longer letteth thee believe in a"
"11135" 1998 "God? And thine over-great honesty will yet lead thee even beyond good"
"11136" 1998 "and evil!"
"11137" 1998 ""
"11138" 1998 "Behold, what hath been reserved for thee? Thou hast eyes and hands and"
"11139" 1998 "mouth, which have been predestined for blessing from eternity. One doth"
"11140" 1998 "not bless with the hand alone."
"11141" 1998 ""
"11142" 1998 "Nigh unto thee, though thou professest to be the ungodliest one, I feel"

"11145" 1998 ""
"11146" 1998 "Let me be thy guest, O Zarathustra, for a single night! Nowhere on earth"

"11148" 1998 ""
"11149" 1998 "\"Amen! So shall it be!\" said Zarathustra, with great astonishment; \"up"

"11151" 1998 ""
"11152" 1998 "Gladly, forsooth, would I conduct thee thither myself, thou venerable"

"11155" 1998 ""
"11156" 1998 "In my domain shall no one come to grief; my cave is a good haven. And"

"11159" 1998 ""

"11163" 1998 ""
"11164" 1998 "For that old God liveth no more: he is indeed dead.\"--"
"11165" 1998 ""
"11166" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra."
"11167" 1998 ""
"11168" 1998 ""
"11169" 1998 ""
"11170" 1998 ""
"11171" 1998 "LXVII. THE UGLIEST MAN."
"11172" 1998 ""
"11173" 1998 "--And again did Zarathustra's feet run through mountains and forests,"
"11174" 1998 "and his eyes sought and sought, but nowhere was he to be seen whom they"
"11175" 1998 "wanted to see--the sorely distressed sufferer and crier. On the whole"
"11176" 1998 "way, however, he rejoiced in his heart and was full of gratitude. \"What"
"11177" 1998 "good things,\" said he, \"hath this day given me, as amends for its bad"
"11178" 1998 "beginning! What strange interlocutors have I found!"
"11179" 1998 ""
"11180" 1998 "At their words will I now chew a long while as at good corn; small"
"11181" 1998 "shall my teeth grind and crush them, until they flow like milk into my"
"11182" 1998 "soul!\"--"
"11183" 1998 ""
"11184" 1998 "When, however, the path again curved round a rock, all at once the"
"11185" 1998 "landscape changed, and Zarathustra entered into a realm of death. Here"
"11186" 1998 "bristled aloft black and red cliffs, without any grass, tree, or bird's"
"11187" 1998 "voice. For it was a valley which all animals avoided, even the beasts of"
"11188" 1998 "prey, except that a species of ugly, thick, green serpent came here to"
"11189" 1998 "die when they became old. Therefore the shepherds called this valley:"
"11190" 1998 "\"Serpent-death.\""
"11191" 1998 ""
"11192" 1998 "Zarathustra, however, became absorbed in dark recollections, for it"
"11193" 1998 "seemed to him as if he had once before stood in this valley. And much"
"11194" 1998 "heaviness settled on his mind, so that he walked slowly and always more"
"11195" 1998 "slowly, and at last stood still. Then, however, when he opened his eyes,"
"11196" 1998 "he saw something sitting by the wayside shaped like a man, and hardly"
"11197" 1998 "like a man, something nondescript. And all at once there came over"
"11198" 1998 "Zarathustra a great shame, because he had gazed on such a thing."
"11199" 1998 "Blushing up to the very roots of his white hair, he turned aside his"
"11200" 1998 "glance, and raised his foot that he might leave this ill-starred place."
"11201" 1998 "Then, however, became the dead wilderness vocal: for from the ground a"
"11202" 1998 "noise welled up, gurgling and rattling, as water gurgleth and rattleth"
"11203" 1998 "at night through stopped-up water-pipes; and at last it turned into"
"11204" 1998 "human voice and human speech:--it sounded thus:"
"11205" 1998 ""
"11206" 1998 "\"Zarathustra! Zarathustra! Read my riddle! Say, say! WHAT IS THE REVENGE"
"11207" 1998 "ON THE WITNESS?"
"11208" 1998 ""
"11209" 1998 "I entice thee back; here is smooth ice! See to it, see to it, that thy"
"11210" 1998 "pride doth not here break its legs!"
"11211" 1998 ""
"11212" 1998 "Thou thinkest thyself wise, thou proud Zarathustra! Read then the"
"11213" 1998 "riddle, thou hard nut-cracker,--the riddle that I am! Say then: who am"
"11214" 1998 "_I_!\""
"11215" 1998 ""
"11216" 1998 "--When however Zarathustra had heard these words,--what think ye then"
"11217" 1998 "took place in his soul? PITY OVERCAME HIM; and he sank down all at"
"11218" 1998 "once, like an oak that hath long withstood many tree-fellers,--heavily,"
"11219" 1998 "suddenly, to the terror even of those who meant to fell it. But"
"11220" 1998 "immediately he got up again from the ground, and his countenance became"
"11221" 1998 "stern."
"11222" 1998 ""
"11223" 1998 "\"I know thee well,\" said he, with a brazen voice, \"THOU ART THE MURDERER"
"11224" 1998 "OF GOD! Let me go."
"11225" 1998 ""
"11226" 1998 "Thou couldst not ENDURE him who beheld THEE,--who ever beheld thee"
"11227" 1998 "through and through, thou ugliest man. Thou tookest revenge on this"
"11228" 1998 "witness!\""
"11229" 1998 ""
"11230" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra and was about to go; but the nondescript grasped"
"11231" 1998 "at a corner of his garment and began anew to gurgle and seek for words."
"11232" 1998 "\"Stay,\" said he at last--"
"11233" 1998 ""
"11234" 1998 "--\"Stay! Do not pass by! I have divined what axe it was that struck thee"
"11235" 1998 "to the ground: hail to thee, O Zarathustra, that thou art again upon thy"
"11236" 1998 "feet!"
"11237" 1998 ""
"11238" 1998 "Thou hast divined, I know it well, how the man feeleth who killed"
"11239" 1998 "him,--the murderer of God. Stay! Sit down here beside me; it is not to"
"11240" 1998 "no purpose."
"11241" 1998 ""
"11242" 1998 "To whom would I go but unto thee? Stay, sit down! Do not however look at"
"11243" 1998 "me! Honour thus--mine ugliness!"
"11244" 1998 ""
"11245" 1998 "They persecute me: now art THOU my last refuge. NOT with their hatred,"
"11246" 1998 "NOT with their bailiffs;--Oh, such persecution would I mock at, and be"
"11247" 1998 "proud and cheerful!"
"11248" 1998 ""
"11249" 1998 "Hath not all success hitherto been with the well-persecuted ones? And"
"11250" 1998 "he who persecuteth well learneth readily to be OBSEQUENT--when once he"
"11251" 1998 "is--put behind! But it is their PITY--"
"11252" 1998 ""
"11253" 1998 "--Their pity is it from which I flee away and flee to thee. O"
"11254" 1998 "Zarathustra, protect me, thou, my last refuge, thou sole one who"
"11255" 1998 "divinedst me:"
"11256" 1998 ""
"11257" 1998 "--Thou hast divined how the man feeleth who killed HIM. Stay! And if"
"11258" 1998 "thou wilt go, thou impatient one, go not the way that I came. THAT way"
"11259" 1998 "is bad."
"11260" 1998 ""
"11261" 1998 "Art thou angry with me because I have already racked language too long?"
"11262" 1998 "Because I have already counselled thee? But know that it is I, the"
"11263" 1998 "ugliest man,"
"11264" 1998 ""
"11265" 1998 "--Who have also the largest, heaviest feet. Where _I_ have gone, the way"
"11266" 1998 "is bad. I tread all paths to death and destruction."
"11267" 1998 ""
"11268" 1998 "But that thou passedst me by in silence, that thou blushedst--I saw it"
"11269" 1998 "well: thereby did I know thee as Zarathustra."
"11270" 1998 ""
"11271" 1998 "Every one else would have thrown to me his alms, his pity, in look and"
"11272" 1998 "speech. But for that--I am not beggar enough: that didst thou divine."
"11273" 1998 ""
"11274" 1998 "For that I am too RICH, rich in what is great, frightful, ugliest, most"
"11275" 1998 "unutterable! Thy shame, O Zarathustra, HONOURED me!"
"11276" 1998 ""
"11277" 1998 "With difficulty did I get out of the crowd of the pitiful,--that I might"
"11278" 1998 "find the only one who at present teacheth that 'pity is obtrusive'--"
"11279" 1998 "thyself, O Zarathustra!"
"11280" 1998 ""
"11281" 1998 "--Whether it be the pity of a God, or whether it be human pity, it is"
"11282" 1998 "offensive to modesty. And unwillingness to help may be nobler than the"
"11283" 1998 "virtue that rusheth to do so."
"11284" 1998 ""
"11285" 1998 "THAT however--namely, pity--is called virtue itself at present by"
"11286" 1998 "all petty people:--they have no reverence for great misfortune, great"
"11287" 1998 "ugliness, great failure."
"11288" 1998 ""
"11289" 1998 "Beyond all these do I look, as a dog looketh over the backs of thronging"
"11290" 1998 "flocks of sheep. They are petty, good-wooled, good-willed, grey people."
"11291" 1998 ""
"11292" 1998 "As the heron looketh contemptuously at shallow pools, with backward-bent"
"11293" 1998 "head, so do I look at the throng of grey little waves and wills and"
"11294" 1998 "souls."
"11295" 1998 ""
"11296" 1998 "Too long have we acknowledged them to be right, those petty people: SO"
"11297" 1998 "we have at last given them power as well;--and now do they teach that"
"11298" 1998 "'good is only what petty people call good.'"
"11299" 1998 ""
"11300" 1998 "And 'truth' is at present what the preacher spake who himself sprang"
"11301" 1998 "from them, that singular saint and advocate of the petty people, who"
"11302" 1998 "testified of himself: 'I--am the truth.'"
"11303" 1998 ""
"11304" 1998 "That immodest one hath long made the petty people greatly puffed up,--he"
"11305" 1998 "who taught no small error when he taught: 'I--am the truth.'"
"11306" 1998 ""
"11307" 1998 "Hath an immodest one ever been answered more courteously?--Thou,"
"11308" 1998 "however, O Zarathustra, passedst him by, and saidst: 'Nay! Nay! Three"
"11309" 1998 "times Nay!'"
"11310" 1998 ""
"11311" 1998 "Thou warnedst against his error; thou warnedst--the first to do"
"11312" 1998 "so--against pity:--not every one, not none, but thyself and thy type."
"11313" 1998 ""
"11314" 1998 "Thou art ashamed of the shame of the great sufferer; and verily when"
"11315" 1998 "thou sayest: 'From pity there cometh a heavy cloud; take heed, ye men!'"
"11316" 1998 ""
"11317" 1998 "--When thou teachest: 'All creators are hard, all great love is beyond"
"11318" 1998 "their pity:' O Zarathustra, how well versed dost thou seem to me in"
"11319" 1998 "weather-signs!"
"11320" 1998 ""
"11321" 1998 "Thou thyself, however,--warn thyself also against THY pity! For many are"
"11322" 1998 "on their way to thee, many suffering, doubting, despairing, drowning,"
"11323" 1998 "freezing ones--"
"11324" 1998 ""
"11325" 1998 "I warn thee also against myself. Thou hast read my best, my worst"
"11326" 1998 "riddle, myself, and what I have done. I know the axe that felleth thee."
"11327" 1998 ""
"11328" 1998 "But he--HAD TO die: he looked with eyes which beheld EVERYTHING,--he"
"11329" 1998 "beheld men's depths and dregs, all his hidden ignominy and ugliness."
"11330" 1998 ""
"11331" 1998 "His pity knew no modesty: he crept into my dirtiest corners. This most"
"11332" 1998 "prying, over-intrusive, over-pitiful one had to die."
"11333" 1998 ""
"11334" 1998 "He ever beheld ME: on such a witness I would have revenge--or not live"
"11335" 1998 "myself."
"11336" 1998 ""
"11337" 1998 "The God who beheld everything, AND ALSO MAN: that God had to die! Man"
"11338" 1998 "cannot ENDURE it that such a witness should live.\""
"11339" 1998 ""
"11340" 1998 "Thus spake the ugliest man. Zarathustra however got up, and prepared to"
"11341" 1998 "go on: for he felt frozen to the very bowels."
"11342" 1998 ""
"11343" 1998 "\"Thou nondescript,\" said he, \"thou warnedst me against thy path. As"
"11344" 1998 "thanks for it I praise mine to thee. Behold, up thither is the cave of"
"11345" 1998 "Zarathustra."
"11346" 1998 ""
"11347" 1998 "My cave is large and deep and hath many corners; there findeth he"
"11348" 1998 "that is most hidden his hiding-place. And close beside it, there are"
"11349" 1998 "a hundred lurking-places and by-places for creeping, fluttering, and"
"11350" 1998 "hopping creatures."
"11351" 1998 ""
"11352" 1998 "Thou outcast, who hast cast thyself out, thou wilt not live amongst men"
"11353" 1998 "and men's pity? Well then, do like me! Thus wilt thou learn also from"
"11354" 1998 "me; only the doer learneth."
"11355" 1998 ""
"11356" 1998 "And talk first and foremost to mine animals! The proudest animal and the"
"11357" 1998 "wisest animal--they might well be the right counsellors for us both!\"--"
"11358" 1998 ""
"11359" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra and went his way, more thoughtfully and slowly"
"11360" 1998 "even than before: for he asked himself many things, and hardly knew what"
"11361" 1998 "to answer."
"11362" 1998 ""
"11363" 1998 "\"How poor indeed is man,\" thought he in his heart, \"how ugly, how"
"11364" 1998 "wheezy, how full of hidden shame!"
"11365" 1998 ""
"11366" 1998 "They tell me that man loveth himself. Ah, how great must that self-love"
"11367" 1998 "be! How much contempt is opposed to it!"
"11368" 1998 ""
"11369" 1998 "Even this man hath loved himself, as he hath despised himself,--a great"
"11370" 1998 "lover methinketh he is, and a great despiser."
"11371" 1998 ""
"11372" 1998 "No one have I yet found who more thoroughly despised himself: even THAT"
"11373" 1998 "is elevation. Alas, was THIS perhaps the higher man whose cry I heard?"
"11374" 1998 ""
"11375" 1998 "I love the great despisers. Man is something that hath to be"
"11376" 1998 "surpassed.\"--"
"11377" 1998 ""
"11378" 1998 ""
"11379" 1998 ""
"11380" 1998 ""
"11381" 1998 "LXVIII. THE VOLUNTARY BEGGAR."
"11382" 1998 ""
"11383" 1998 "When Zarathustra had left the ugliest man, he was chilled and felt"
"11384" 1998 "lonesome: for much coldness and lonesomeness came over his spirit, so"
"11385" 1998 "that even his limbs became colder thereby. When, however, he wandered"
"11386" 1998 "on and on, uphill and down, at times past green meadows, though also"
"11387" 1998 "sometimes over wild stony couches where formerly perhaps an impatient"
"11388" 1998 "brook had made its bed, then he turned all at once warmer and heartier"
"11389" 1998 "again."
"11390" 1998 ""
"11391" 1998 "\"What hath happened unto me?\" he asked himself, \"something warm and"
"11392" 1998 "living quickeneth me; it must be in the neighbourhood."
"11393" 1998 ""
"11394" 1998 "Already am I less alone; unconscious companions and brethren rove around"
"11395" 1998 "me; their warm breath toucheth my soul.\""
"11396" 1998 ""
"11397" 1998 "When, however, he spied about and sought for the comforters of his"
"11398" 1998 "lonesomeness, behold, there were kine there standing together on an"
"11399" 1998 "eminence, whose proximity and smell had warmed his heart. The kine,"
"11400" 1998 "however, seemed to listen eagerly to a speaker, and took no heed of him"
"11401" 1998 "who approached. When, however, Zarathustra was quite nigh unto them,"
"11402" 1998 "then did he hear plainly that a human voice spake in the midst of the"
"11403" 1998 "kine, and apparently all of them had turned their heads towards the"
"11404" 1998 "speaker."
"11405" 1998 ""
"11406" 1998 "Then ran Zarathustra up speedily and drove the animals aside; for he"
"11407" 1998 "feared that some one had here met with harm, which the pity of the"
"11408" 1998 "kine would hardly be able to relieve. But in this he was deceived; for"
"11409" 1998 "behold, there sat a man on the ground who seemed to be persuading"
"11410" 1998 "the animals to have no fear of him, a peaceable man and"
"11411" 1998 "Preacher-on-the-Mount, out of whose eyes kindness itself preached. \"What"
"11412" 1998 "dost thou seek here?\" called out Zarathustra in astonishment."
"11413" 1998 ""
"11414" 1998 "\"What do I here seek?\" answered he: \"the same that thou seekest, thou"
"11415" 1998 "mischief-maker; that is to say, happiness upon earth."
"11416" 1998 ""
"11417" 1998 "To that end, however, I would fain learn of these kine. For I tell thee"
"11418" 1998 "that I have already talked half a morning unto them, and just now were"
"11419" 1998 "they about to give me their answer. Why dost thou disturb them?"
"11420" 1998 ""
"11421" 1998 "Except we be converted and become as kine, we shall in no wise enter"
"11422" 1998 "into the kingdom of heaven. For we ought to learn from them one thing:"
"11423" 1998 "ruminating."
"11424" 1998 ""
"11425" 1998 "And verily, although a man should gain the whole world, and yet not"
"11426" 1998 "learn one thing, ruminating, what would it profit him! He would not be"
"11427" 1998 "rid of his affliction,"
"11428" 1998 ""
"11429" 1998 "--His great affliction: that, however, is at present called DISGUST. Who"
"11430" 1998 "hath not at present his heart, his mouth and his eyes full of disgust?"
"11431" 1998 "Thou also! Thou also! But behold these kine!\"--"
"11432" 1998 ""
"11433" 1998 "Thus spake the Preacher-on-the-Mount, and turned then his own look"
"11434" 1998 "towards Zarathustra--for hitherto it had rested lovingly on the kine--:"
"11435" 1998 "then, however, he put on a different expression. \"Who is this with whom"
"11436" 1998 "I talk?\" he exclaimed frightened, and sprang up from the ground."
"11437" 1998 ""
"11438" 1998 "\"This is the man without disgust, this is Zarathustra himself, the"
"11439" 1998 "surmounter of the great disgust, this is the eye, this is the mouth,"
"11440" 1998 "this is the heart of Zarathustra himself.\""
"11441" 1998 ""
"11442" 1998 "And whilst he thus spake he kissed with o'erflowing eyes the hands"
"11443" 1998 "of him with whom he spake, and behaved altogether like one to whom a"
"11444" 1998 "precious gift and jewel hath fallen unawares from heaven. The kine,"
"11445" 1998 "however, gazed at it all and wondered."
"11446" 1998 ""
"11447" 1998 "\"Speak not of me, thou strange one; thou amiable one!\" said Zarathustra,"
"11448" 1998 "and restrained his affection, \"speak to me firstly of thyself! Art thou"
"11449" 1998 "not the voluntary beggar who once cast away great riches,--"
"11450" 1998 ""
"11451" 1998 "--Who was ashamed of his riches and of the rich, and fled to the poorest"
"11452" 1998 "to bestow upon them his abundance and his heart? But they received him"
"11453" 1998 "not.\""
"11454" 1998 ""
"11455" 1998 "\"But they received me not,\" said the voluntary beggar, \"thou knowest it,"
"11456" 1998 "forsooth. So I went at last to the animals and to those kine.\""
"11457" 1998 ""
"11458" 1998 "\"Then learnedst thou,\" interrupted Zarathustra, \"how much harder it is"
"11459" 1998 "to give properly than to take properly, and that bestowing well is an"
"11460" 1998 "ART--the last, subtlest master-art of kindness.\""
"11461" 1998 ""
"11462" 1998 "\"Especially nowadays,\" answered the voluntary beggar: \"at present, that"
"11463" 1998 "is to say, when everything low hath become rebellious and exclusive and"
"11464" 1998 "haughty in its manner--in the manner of the populace."
"11465" 1998 ""
"11466" 1998 "For the hour hath come, thou knowest it forsooth, for the great, evil,"
"11467" 1998 "long, slow mob-and-slave-insurrection: it extendeth and extendeth!"
"11468" 1998 ""
"11469" 1998 "Now doth it provoke the lower classes, all benevolence and petty giving;"
"11470" 1998 "and the overrich may be on their guard!"
"11471" 1998 ""
"11472" 1998 "Whoever at present drip, like bulgy bottles out of all-too-small"
"11473" 1998 "necks:--of such bottles at present one willingly breaketh the necks."
"11474" 1998 ""
"11475" 1998 "Wanton avidity, bilious envy, careworn revenge, populace-pride: all"
"11476" 1998 "these struck mine eye. It is no longer true that the poor are blessed."
"11477" 1998 "The kingdom of heaven, however, is with the kine.\""
"11478" 1998 ""
"11479" 1998 "\"And why is it not with the rich?\" asked Zarathustra temptingly, while"
"11480" 1998 "he kept back the kine which sniffed familiarly at the peaceful one."
"11481" 1998 ""
"11482" 1998 "\"Why dost thou tempt me?\" answered the other. \"Thou knowest it thyself"
"11483" 1998 "better even than I. What was it drove me to the poorest, O Zarathustra?"
"11484" 1998 "Was it not my disgust at the richest?"
"11485" 1998 ""
"11486" 1998 "--At the culprits of riches, with cold eyes and rank thoughts, who pick"
"11487" 1998 "up profit out of all kinds of rubbish--at this rabble that stinketh to"
"11488" 1998 "heaven,"
"11489" 1998 ""
"11490" 1998 "--At this gilded, falsified populace, whose fathers were pickpockets,"
"11491" 1998 "or carrion-crows, or rag-pickers, with wives compliant, lewd and"
"11492" 1998 "forgetful:--for they are all of them not far different from harlots--"
"11493" 1998 ""
"11494" 1998 "Populace above, populace below! What are 'poor' and 'rich' at present!"
"11495" 1998 "That distinction did I unlearn,--then did I flee away further and ever"
"11496" 1998 "further, until I came to those kine.\""
"11497" 1998 ""
"11498" 1998 "Thus spake the peaceful one, and puffed himself and perspired with"
"11499" 1998 "his words: so that the kine wondered anew. Zarathustra, however, kept"
"11500" 1998 "looking into his face with a smile, all the time the man talked so"
"11501" 1998 "severely--and shook silently his head."
"11502" 1998 ""
"11503" 1998 "\"Thou doest violence to thyself, thou Preacher-on-the-Mount, when thou"
"11504" 1998 "usest such severe words. For such severity neither thy mouth nor thine"
"11505" 1998 "eye have been given thee."
"11506" 1998 ""
"11507" 1998 "Nor, methinketh, hath thy stomach either: unto IT all such rage and"
"11508" 1998 "hatred and foaming-over is repugnant. Thy stomach wanteth softer things:"
"11509" 1998 "thou art not a butcher."
"11510" 1998 ""
"11511" 1998 "Rather seemest thou to me a plant-eater and a root-man. Perhaps thou"
"11512" 1998 "grindest corn. Certainly, however, thou art averse to fleshly joys, and"
"11513" 1998 "thou lovest honey.\""
"11514" 1998 ""
"11515" 1998 "\"Thou hast divined me well,\" answered the voluntary beggar, with"
"11516" 1998 "lightened heart. \"I love honey, I also grind corn; for I have sought out"
"11517" 1998 "what tasteth sweetly and maketh pure breath:"
"11518" 1998 ""
"11519" 1998 "--Also what requireth a long time, a day's-work and a mouth's-work for"
"11520" 1998 "gentle idlers and sluggards."
"11521" 1998 ""
"11522" 1998 "Furthest, to be sure, have those kine carried it: they have devised"
"11523" 1998 "ruminating and lying in the sun. They also abstain from all heavy"
"11524" 1998 "thoughts which inflate the heart.\""
"11525" 1998 ""
"11526" 1998 "--\"Well!\" said Zarathustra, \"thou shouldst also see MINE animals, mine"
"11527" 1998 "eagle and my serpent,--their like do not at present exist on earth."
"11528" 1998 ""
"11529" 1998 "Behold, thither leadeth the way to my cave: be to-night its guest. And"
"11530" 1998 "talk to mine animals of the happiness of animals,--"
"11531" 1998 ""
"11532" 1998 "--Until I myself come home. For now a cry of distress calleth me hastily"
"11533" 1998 "away from thee. Also, shouldst thou find new honey with me, ice-cold,"
"11534" 1998 "golden-comb-honey, eat it!"
"11535" 1998 ""
"11536" 1998 "Now, however, take leave at once of thy kine, thou strange one! thou"
"11537" 1998 "amiable one! though it be hard for thee. For they are thy warmest"
"11538" 1998 "friends and preceptors!\"--"
"11539" 1998 ""
"11540" 1998 "--\"One excepted, whom I hold still dearer,\" answered the voluntary"
"11541" 1998 "beggar. \"Thou thyself art good, O Zarathustra, and better even than a"
"11542" 1998 "cow!\""
"11543" 1998 ""
"11544" 1998 "\"Away, away with thee! thou evil flatterer!\" cried Zarathustra"
"11545" 1998 "mischievously, \"why dost thou spoil me with such praise and"
"11546" 1998 "flattery-honey?"
"11547" 1998 ""
"11548" 1998 "\"Away, away from me!\" cried he once more, and heaved his stick at the"
"11549" 1998 "fond beggar, who, however, ran nimbly away."
"11550" 1998 ""
"11551" 1998 ""
"11552" 1998 ""
"11553" 1998 ""
"11554" 1998 "LXIX. THE SHADOW."
"11555" 1998 ""
"11556" 1998 "Scarcely however was the voluntary beggar gone in haste, and Zarathustra"
"11557" 1998 "again alone, when he heard behind him a new voice which called out:"
"11558" 1998 "\"Stay! Zarathustra! Do wait! It is myself, forsooth, O Zarathustra,"
"11559" 1998 "myself, thy shadow!\" But Zarathustra did not wait; for a sudden"
"11560" 1998 "irritation came over him on account of the crowd and the crowding in his"
"11561" 1998 "mountains. \"Whither hath my lonesomeness gone?\" spake he."
"11562" 1998 ""
"11563" 1998 "\"It is verily becoming too much for me; these mountains swarm; my"
"11564" 1998 "kingdom is no longer of THIS world; I require new mountains."
"11565" 1998 ""
"11566" 1998 "My shadow calleth me? What matter about my shadow! Let it run after me!"
"11567" 1998 "I--run away from it.\""
"11568" 1998 ""
"11569" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra to his heart and ran away. But the one behind"
"11570" 1998 "followed after him, so that immediately there were three runners,"
"11571" 1998 "one after the other--namely, foremost the voluntary beggar, then"
"11572" 1998 "Zarathustra, and thirdly, and hindmost, his shadow. But not long had"
"11573" 1998 "they run thus when Zarathustra became conscious of his folly, and shook"
"11574" 1998 "off with one jerk all his irritation and detestation."
"11575" 1998 ""
"11576" 1998 "\"What!\" said he, \"have not the most ludicrous things always happened to"
"11577" 1998 "us old anchorites and saints?"
"11578" 1998 ""
"11579" 1998 "Verily, my folly hath grown big in the mountains! Now do I hear six old"
"11580" 1998 "fools' legs rattling behind one another!"
"11581" 1998 ""
"11582" 1998 "But doth Zarathustra need to be frightened by his shadow? Also,"
"11583" 1998 "methinketh that after all it hath longer legs than mine.\""
"11584" 1998 ""
"11585" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra, and, laughing with eyes and entrails, he stood"
"11586" 1998 "still and turned round quickly--and behold, he almost thereby threw his"
"11587" 1998 "shadow and follower to the ground, so closely had the latter followed at"
"11588" 1998 "his heels, and so weak was he. For when Zarathustra scrutinised him"
"11589" 1998 "with his glance he was frightened as by a sudden apparition, so slender,"
"11590" 1998 "swarthy, hollow and worn-out did this follower appear."
"11591" 1998 ""
"11592" 1998 "\"Who art thou?\" asked Zarathustra vehemently, \"what doest thou here? And"
"11593" 1998 "why callest thou thyself my shadow? Thou art not pleasing unto me.\""
"11594" 1998 ""
"11595" 1998 "\"Forgive me,\" answered the shadow, \"that it is I; and if I please thee"
"11596" 1998 "not--well, O Zarathustra! therein do I admire thee and thy good taste."
"11597" 1998 ""
"11598" 1998 "A wanderer am I, who have walked long at thy heels; always on the way,"
"11599" 1998 "but without a goal, also without a home: so that verily, I lack little"
"11600" 1998 "of being the eternally Wandering Jew, except that I am not eternal and"
"11601" 1998 "not a Jew."
"11602" 1998 ""
"11603" 1998 "What? Must I ever be on the way? Whirled by every wind, unsettled,"
"11604" 1998 "driven about? O earth, thou hast become too round for me!"
"11605" 1998 ""
"11606" 1998 "On every surface have I already sat, like tired dust have I fallen"
"11607" 1998 "asleep on mirrors and window-panes: everything taketh from me, nothing"
"11608" 1998 "giveth; I become thin--I am almost equal to a shadow."
"11609" 1998 ""
"11610" 1998 "After thee, however, O Zarathustra, did I fly and hie longest; and"
"11611" 1998 "though I hid myself from thee, I was nevertheless thy best shadow:"
"11612" 1998 "wherever thou hast sat, there sat I also."
"11613" 1998 ""
"11614" 1998 "With thee have I wandered about in the remotest, coldest worlds, like a"
"11615" 1998 "phantom that voluntarily haunteth winter roofs and snows."
"11616" 1998 ""
"11617" 1998 "With thee have I pushed into all the forbidden, all the worst and the"
"11618" 1998 "furthest: and if there be anything of virtue in me, it is that I have"
"11619" 1998 "had no fear of any prohibition."
"11620" 1998 ""
"11621" 1998 "With thee have I broken up whatever my heart revered; all"
"11622" 1998 "boundary-stones and statues have I o'erthrown; the most dangerous wishes"
"11623" 1998 "did I pursue,--verily, beyond every crime did I once go."
"11624" 1998 ""
"11625" 1998 "With thee did I unlearn the belief in words and worths and in great"
"11626" 1998 "names. When the devil casteth his skin, doth not his name also fall"
"11627" 1998 "away? It is also skin. The devil himself is perhaps--skin."
"11628" 1998 ""
"11629" 1998 "'Nothing is true, all is permitted': so said I to myself. Into the"
"11630" 1998 "coldest water did I plunge with head and heart. Ah, how oft did I stand"
"11631" 1998 "there naked on that account, like a red crab!"
"11632" 1998 ""
"11633" 1998 "Ah, where have gone all my goodness and all my shame and all my belief"
"11634" 1998 "in the good! Ah, where is the lying innocence which I once possessed,"
"11635" 1998 "the innocence of the good and of their noble lies!"
"11636" 1998 ""
"11637" 1998 "Too oft, verily, did I follow close to the heels of truth: then did it"
"11638" 1998 "kick me on the face. Sometimes I meant to lie, and behold! then only did"
"11639" 1998 "I hit--the truth."
"11640" 1998 ""
"11641" 1998 "Too much hath become clear unto me: now it doth not concern me any more."
"11642" 1998 "Nothing liveth any longer that I love,--how should I still love myself?"
"11643" 1998 ""
"11644" 1998 "'To live as I incline, or not to live at all': so do I wish; so wisheth"
"11645" 1998 "also the holiest. But alas! how have _I_ still--inclination?"
"11646" 1998 ""
"11647" 1998 "Have _I_--still a goal? A haven towards which MY sail is set?"
"11648" 1998 ""
"11649" 1998 "A good wind? Ah, he only who knoweth WHITHER he saileth, knoweth what"
"11650" 1998 "wind is good, and a fair wind for him."
"11651" 1998 ""
"11652" 1998 "What still remaineth to me? A heart weary and flippant; an unstable"
"11653" 1998 "will; fluttering wings; a broken backbone."
"11654" 1998 ""
"11655" 1998 "This seeking for MY home: O Zarathustra, dost thou know that this"
"11656" 1998 "seeking hath been MY home-sickening; it eateth me up."
"11657" 1998 ""
"11658" 1998 "'WHERE is--MY home?' For it do I ask and seek, and have sought, but"
"11659" 1998 "have not found it. O eternal everywhere, O eternal nowhere, O"
"11660" 1998 "eternal--in-vain!\""
"11661" 1998 ""
"11662" 1998 "Thus spake the shadow, and Zarathustra's countenance lengthened at his"
"11663" 1998 "words. \"Thou art my shadow!\" said he at last sadly."
"11664" 1998 ""
"11665" 1998 "\"Thy danger is not small, thou free spirit and wanderer! Thou hast had a"
"11666" 1998 "bad day: see that a still worse evening doth not overtake thee!"
"11667" 1998 ""
"11668" 1998 "To such unsettled ones as thou, seemeth at last even a prisoner blessed."
"11669" 1998 "Didst thou ever see how captured criminals sleep? They sleep quietly,"
"11670" 1998 "they enjoy their new security."
"11671" 1998 ""
"11672" 1998 "Beware lest in the end a narrow faith capture thee, a hard, rigorous"
"11673" 1998 "delusion! For now everything that is narrow and fixed seduceth and"
"11674" 1998 "tempteth thee."
"11675" 1998 ""
"11676" 1998 "Thou hast lost thy goal. Alas, how wilt thou forego and forget that"
"11677" 1998 "loss? Thereby--hast thou also lost thy way!"
"11678" 1998 ""
"11679" 1998 "Thou poor rover and rambler, thou tired butterfly! wilt thou have a rest"
"11680" 1998 "and a home this evening? Then go up to my cave!"
"11681" 1998 ""
"11682" 1998 "Thither leadeth the way to my cave. And now will I run quickly away from"
"11683" 1998 "thee again. Already lieth as it were a shadow upon me."
"11684" 1998 ""

"11686" 1998 "Therefore must I still be a long time merrily upon my legs. In the"
"11687" 1998 "evening, however, there will be--dancing with me!\"--"
"11688" 1998 ""

"11694" 1998 "LXX. NOONTIDE."
"11695" 1998 ""
"11696" 1998 "--And Zarathustra ran and ran, but he found no one else, and was alone"
"11697" 1998 "and ever found himself again; he enjoyed and quaffed his solitude, and"
"11698" 1998 "thought of good things--for hours. About the hour of noontide, however,"
"11699" 1998 "when the sun stood exactly over Zarathustra's head, he passed an old,"
"11700" 1998 "bent and gnarled tree, which was encircled round by the ardent love of"
"11701" 1998 "a vine, and hidden from itself; from this there hung yellow grapes in"
"11702" 1998 "abundance, confronting the wanderer. Then he felt inclined to quench a"
"11703" 1998 "little thirst, and to break off for himself a cluster of grapes. When,"
"11704" 1998 "however, he had already his arm out-stretched for that purpose, he felt"
"11705" 1998 "still more inclined for something else--namely, to lie down beside the"
"11706" 1998 "tree at the hour of perfect noontide and sleep."
"11707" 1998 ""
"11708" 1998 "This Zarathustra did; and no sooner had he laid himself on the ground in"
"11709" 1998 "the stillness and secrecy of the variegated grass, than he had forgotten"
"11710" 1998 "his little thirst, and fell asleep. For as the proverb of Zarathustra"
"11711" 1998 "saith: \"One thing is more necessary than the other.\" Only that his eyes"
"11712" 1998 "remained open:--for they never grew weary of viewing and admiring the"
"11713" 1998 "tree and the love of the vine. In falling asleep, however, Zarathustra"
"11714" 1998 "spake thus to his heart:"
"11715" 1998 ""
"11716" 1998 "\"Hush! Hush! Hath not the world now become perfect? What hath happened"
"11717" 1998 "unto me?"
"11718" 1998 ""
"11719" 1998 "As a delicate wind danceth invisibly upon parqueted seas, light,"
"11720" 1998 "feather-light, so--danceth sleep upon me."
"11721" 1998 ""
"11722" 1998 "No eye doth it close to me, it leaveth my soul awake. Light is it,"
"11723" 1998 "verily, feather-light."
"11724" 1998 ""
"11725" 1998 "It persuadeth me, I know not how, it toucheth me inwardly with a"
"11726" 1998 "caressing hand, it constraineth me. Yea, it constraineth me, so that my"
"11727" 1998 "soul stretcheth itself out:--"
"11728" 1998 ""
"11729" 1998 "--How long and weary it becometh, my strange soul! Hath a seventh-day"
"11730" 1998 "evening come to it precisely at noontide? Hath it already wandered too"
"11731" 1998 "long, blissfully, among good and ripe things?"
"11732" 1998 ""
"11733" 1998 "It stretcheth itself out, long--longer! it lieth still, my strange"
"11734" 1998 "soul. Too many good things hath it already tasted; this golden sadness"
"11735" 1998 "oppresseth it, it distorteth its mouth."
"11736" 1998 ""
"11737" 1998 "--As a ship that putteth into the calmest cove:--it now draweth up to"
"11738" 1998 "the land, weary of long voyages and uncertain seas. Is not the land more"
"11739" 1998 "faithful?"
"11740" 1998 ""
"11741" 1998 "As such a ship huggeth the shore, tuggeth the shore:--then it sufficeth"
"11742" 1998 "for a spider to spin its thread from the ship to the land. No stronger"
"11743" 1998 "ropes are required there."
"11744" 1998 ""
"11745" 1998 "As such a weary ship in the calmest cove, so do I also now repose, nigh"
"11746" 1998 "to the earth, faithful, trusting, waiting, bound to it with the lightest"
"11747" 1998 "threads."
"11748" 1998 ""
"11749" 1998 "O happiness! O happiness! Wilt thou perhaps sing, O my soul? Thou liest"
"11750" 1998 "in the grass. But this is the secret, solemn hour, when no shepherd"
"11751" 1998 "playeth his pipe."
"11752" 1998 ""
"11753" 1998 "Take care! Hot noontide sleepeth on the fields. Do not sing! Hush! The"
"11754" 1998 "world is perfect."
"11755" 1998 ""
"11756" 1998 "Do not sing, thou prairie-bird, my soul! Do not even whisper! Lo--hush!"
"11757" 1998 "The old noontide sleepeth, it moveth its mouth: doth it not just now"
"11758" 1998 "drink a drop of happiness--"
"11759" 1998 ""
"11760" 1998 "--An old brown drop of golden happiness, golden wine? Something whisketh"
"11761" 1998 "over it, its happiness laugheth. Thus--laugheth a God. Hush!--"
"11762" 1998 ""
"11763" 1998 "--'For happiness, how little sufficeth for happiness!' Thus spake I"
"11764" 1998 "once and thought myself wise. But it was a blasphemy: THAT have I now"

"11766" 1998 ""
"11767" 1998 "The least thing precisely, the gentlest thing, the lightest thing, a"
"11768" 1998 "lizard's rustling, a breath, a whisk, an eye-glance--LITTLE maketh up"
"11769" 1998 "the BEST happiness. Hush!"
"11770" 1998 ""
"11771" 1998 "--What hath befallen me: Hark! Hath time flown away? Do I not fall? Have"

"11773" 1998 ""
"11774" 1998 "--What happeneth to me? Hush! It stingeth me--alas--to the heart? To"
"11775" 1998 "the heart! Oh, break up, break up, my heart, after such happiness, after"
"11776" 1998 "such a sting!"
"11777" 1998 ""
"11778" 1998 "--What? Hath not the world just now become perfect? Round and ripe? Oh,"
"11779" 1998 "for the golden round ring--whither doth it fly? Let me run after it!"
"11780" 1998 "Quick!"
"11781" 1998 ""
"11782" 1998 "Hush--\" (and here Zarathustra stretched himself, and felt that he was"

"11811" 1998 ""

"11820" 1998 "LXXI. THE GREETING."
"11821" 1998 ""
"11822" 1998 "It was late in the afternoon only when Zarathustra, after long useless"
"11823" 1998 "searching and strolling about, again came home to his cave. When,"
"11824" 1998 "however, he stood over against it, not more than twenty paces therefrom,"
"11825" 1998 "the thing happened which he now least of all expected: he heard anew the"
"11826" 1998 "great CRY OF DISTRESS. And extraordinary! this time the cry came out"
"11827" 1998 "of his own cave. It was a long, manifold, peculiar cry, and Zarathustra"
"11828" 1998 "plainly distinguished that it was composed of many voices: although"
"11829" 1998 "heard at a distance it might sound like the cry out of a single mouth."
"11830" 1998 ""
"11831" 1998 "Thereupon Zarathustra rushed forward to his cave, and behold! what a"

"11838" 1998 "all ugly ones, to disguise himself and play the handsome person. In the"

"11840" 1998 "ruffled and disquieted, for it had been called upon to answer too much"

"11847" 1998 "from their seats, and waited with reverence for Zarathustra to speak."
"11848" 1998 "Zarathustra however spake thus:"
"11849" 1998 ""

"11853" 1998 ""

"11857" 1998 ""

"11861" 1998 ""

"11871" 1998 "despairing one--every one thinketh himself strong enough to do so."
"11872" 1998 ""
"11873" 1998 "To myself have ye given this power,--a good gift, mine honourable"

"11876" 1998 ""
"11877" 1998 "This is mine empire and my dominion: that which is mine, however, shall"

"11884" 1998 ""

"11892" 1998 ""

"11896" 1998 ""

"11904" 1998 ""

"11908" 1998 ""

"11912" 1998 ""

"11927" 1998 ""

"11968" 1998 ""

"12051" 1998 "LXXII. THE SUPPER."
"12052" 1998 ""
