"12095" 1998 ""
"12096" 1998 "Thus will we have a good repast in a little while. But whoever wish to"
"12097" 1998 "eat with us must also give a hand to the work, even the kings. For with"
"12098" 1998 "Zarathustra even a king may be a cook.\""
"12099" 1998 ""
"12100" 1998 "This proposal appealed to the hearts of all of them, save that the"
"12101" 1998 "voluntary beggar objected to the flesh and wine and spices."
"12102" 1998 ""
"12103" 1998 "\"Just hear this glutton Zarathustra!\" said he jokingly: \"doth one go"
"12104" 1998 "into caves and high mountains to make such repasts?"
"12105" 1998 ""
"12106" 1998 "Now indeed do I understand what he once taught us: Blessed be moderate"
"12107" 1998 "poverty!' And why he wisheth to do away with beggars.\""
"12108" 1998 ""
"12109" 1998 "\"Be of good cheer,\" replied Zarathustra, \"as I am. Abide by thy"
"12110" 1998 "customs, thou excellent one: grind thy corn, drink thy water, praise thy"
"12111" 1998 "cooking,--if only it make thee glad!"
"12112" 1998 ""
"12113" 1998 "I am a law only for mine own; I am not a law for all. He, however, who"
"12114" 1998 "belongeth unto me must be strong of bone and light of foot,--"
"12115" 1998 ""
"12116" 1998 "--Joyous in fight and feast, no sulker, no John o' Dreams, ready for the"
"12117" 1998 "hardest task as for the feast, healthy and hale."
"12118" 1998 ""
"12119" 1998 "The best belongeth unto mine and me; and if it be not given us, then do"
"12120" 1998 "we take it:--the best food, the purest sky, the strongest thoughts, the"
"12121" 1998 "fairest women!\"--"
"12122" 1998 ""
"12123" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra; the king on the right however answered and said:"
"12124" 1998 "\"Strange! Did one ever hear such sensible things out of the mouth of a"
"12125" 1998 "wise man?"
"12126" 1998 ""
"12127" 1998 "And verily, it is the strangest thing in a wise man, if over and above,"
"12128" 1998 "he be still sensible, and not an ass.\""
"12129" 1998 ""
"12130" 1998 "Thus spake the king on the right and wondered; the ass however, with"
"12131" 1998 "ill-will, said YE-A to his remark. This however was the beginning of"
"12132" 1998 "that long repast which is called \"The Supper\" in the history-books. At"
"12133" 1998 "this there was nothing else spoken of but THE HIGHER MAN."
"12134" 1998 ""
"12135" 1998 ""
"12136" 1998 ""
"12137" 1998 ""
"12138" 1998 "LXXIII. THE HIGHER MAN."
"12139" 1998 ""
"12140" 1998 "1."
"12141" 1998 ""
"12142" 1998 "When I came unto men for the first time, then did I commit the anchorite"
"12143" 1998 "folly, the great folly: I appeared on the market-place."
"12144" 1998 ""
"12145" 1998 "And when I spake unto all, I spake unto none. In the evening, however,"
"12146" 1998 "rope-dancers were my companions, and corpses; and I myself almost a"
"12147" 1998 "corpse."
"12148" 1998 ""
"12149" 1998 "With the new morning, however, there came unto me a new truth: then did"
"12150" 1998 "I learn to say: \"Of what account to me are market-place and populace and"
"12151" 1998 "populace-noise and long populace-ears!\""
"12152" 1998 ""
"12153" 1998 "Ye higher men, learn THIS from me: On the market-place no one believeth"
"12154" 1998 "in higher men. But if ye will speak there, very well! The populace,"
"12155" 1998 "however, blinketh: \"We are all equal.\""
"12156" 1998 ""
"12157" 1998 "\"Ye higher men,\"--so blinketh the populace--\"there are no higher men, we"
"12158" 1998 "are all equal; man is man, before God--we are all equal!\""
"12159" 1998 ""
"12160" 1998 "Before God!--Now, however, this God hath died. Before the populace,"
"12161" 1998 "however, we will not be equal. Ye higher men, away from the"
"12162" 1998 "market-place!"
"12163" 1998 ""
"12164" 1998 "2."
"12165" 1998 ""
"12166" 1998 "Before God!--Now however this God hath died! Ye higher men, this God was"
"12167" 1998 "your greatest danger."
"12168" 1998 ""
"12169" 1998 "Only since he lay in the grave have ye again arisen. Now only cometh the"
"12170" 1998 "great noontide, now only doth the higher man become--master!"
"12171" 1998 ""
"12172" 1998 "Have ye understood this word, O my brethren? Ye are frightened: do your"
"12173" 1998 "hearts turn giddy? Doth the abyss here yawn for you? Doth the hell-hound"
"12174" 1998 "here yelp at you?"
"12175" 1998 ""
"12176" 1998 "Well! Take heart! ye higher men! Now only travaileth the mountain of the"
"12177" 1998 "human future. God hath died: now do WE desire--the Superman to live."
"12178" 1998 ""
"12179" 1998 "3."
"12180" 1998 ""
"12181" 1998 "The most careful ask to-day: \"How is man to be maintained?\" Zarathustra"
"12182" 1998 "however asketh, as the first and only one: \"How is man to be SURPASSED?\""
"12183" 1998 ""
"12184" 1998 "The Superman, I have at heart; THAT is the first and only thing to"
"12185" 1998 "me--and NOT man: not the neighbour, not the poorest, not the sorriest,"
"12186" 1998 "not the best.--"
"12187" 1998 ""
"12188" 1998 "O my brethren, what I can love in man is that he is an over-going and a"
"12189" 1998 "down-going. And also in you there is much that maketh me love and hope."
"12190" 1998 ""
"12191" 1998 "In that ye have despised, ye higher men, that maketh me hope. For the"
"12192" 1998 "great despisers are the great reverers."
"12193" 1998 ""
"12194" 1998 "In that ye have despaired, there is much to honour. For ye have not"
"12195" 1998 "learned to submit yourselves, ye have not learned petty policy."
"12196" 1998 ""
"12197" 1998 "For to-day have the petty people become master: they all preach"
"12198" 1998 "submission and humility and policy and diligence and consideration and"
"12199" 1998 "the long et cetera of petty virtues."
"12200" 1998 ""
"12201" 1998 "Whatever is of the effeminate type, whatever originateth from the"
"12202" 1998 "servile type, and especially the populace-mishmash:--THAT wisheth now to"
"12203" 1998 "be master of all human destiny--O disgust! Disgust! Disgust!"
"12204" 1998 ""
"12205" 1998 "THAT asketh and asketh and never tireth: \"How is man to maintain himself"
"12206" 1998 "best, longest, most pleasantly?\" Thereby--are they the masters of"
"12207" 1998 "to-day."
"12208" 1998 ""
"12209" 1998 "These masters of to-day--surpass them, O my brethren--these petty"
"12210" 1998 "people: THEY are the Superman's greatest danger!"
"12211" 1998 ""
"12212" 1998 "Surpass, ye higher men, the petty virtues, the petty policy, the"
"12213" 1998 "sand-grain considerateness, the ant-hill trumpery, the pitiable"
"12214" 1998 "comfortableness, the \"happiness of the greatest number\"--!"
"12215" 1998 ""
"12216" 1998 "And rather despair than submit yourselves. And verily, I love you,"
"12217" 1998 "because ye know not to-day how to live, ye higher men! For thus do YE"
"12218" 1998 "live--best!"
"12219" 1998 ""
"12220" 1998 "4."
"12221" 1998 ""
"12222" 1998 "Have ye courage, O my brethren? Are ye stout-hearted? NOT the courage"
"12223" 1998 "before witnesses, but anchorite and eagle courage, which not even a God"
"12224" 1998 "any longer beholdeth?"
"12225" 1998 ""
"12226" 1998 "Cold souls, mules, the blind and the drunken, I do not call"
"12227" 1998 "stout-hearted. He hath heart who knoweth fear, but VANQUISHETH it; who"
"12228" 1998 "seeth the abyss, but with PRIDE."
"12229" 1998 ""
"12230" 1998 "He who seeth the abyss, but with eagle's eyes,--he who with eagle's"
"12231" 1998 "talons GRASPETH the abyss: he hath courage.--"
"12232" 1998 ""
"12233" 1998 "5."
"12234" 1998 ""
"12235" 1998 "\"Man is evil\"--so said to me for consolation, all the wisest ones. Ah,"
"12236" 1998 "if only it be still true to-day! For the evil is man's best force."
"12237" 1998 ""
"12238" 1998 "\"Man must become better and eviler\"--so do _I_ teach. The evilest is"
"12239" 1998 "necessary for the Superman's best."
"12240" 1998 ""
"12241" 1998 "It may have been well for the preacher of the petty people to suffer and"
"12242" 1998 "be burdened by men's sin. I, however, rejoice in great sin as my great"
"12243" 1998 "CONSOLATION.--"
"12244" 1998 ""
"12245" 1998 "Such things, however, are not said for long ears. Every word, also,"
"12246" 1998 "is not suited for every mouth. These are fine far-away things: at them"
"12247" 1998 "sheep's claws shall not grasp!"
"12248" 1998 ""
"12249" 1998 "6."
"12250" 1998 ""
"12251" 1998 "Ye higher men, think ye that I am here to put right what ye have put"
"12252" 1998 "wrong?"
"12253" 1998 ""
"12254" 1998 "Or that I wished henceforth to make snugger couches for you sufferers?"
"12255" 1998 "Or show you restless, miswandering, misclimbing ones, new and easier"
"12256" 1998 "footpaths?"
"12257" 1998 ""
"12258" 1998 "Nay! Nay! Three times Nay! Always more, always better ones of your"
"12259" 1998 "type shall succumb,--for ye shall always have it worse and harder. Thus"
"12260" 1998 "only--"
"12261" 1998 ""
"12262" 1998 "--Thus only groweth man aloft to the height where the lightning striketh"
"12263" 1998 "and shattereth him: high enough for the lightning!"
"12264" 1998 ""
"12265" 1998 "Towards the few, the long, the remote go forth my soul and my seeking:"
"12266" 1998 "of what account to me are your many little, short miseries!"
"12267" 1998 ""
"12268" 1998 "Ye do not yet suffer enough for me! For ye suffer from yourselves, ye"
"12269" 1998 "have not yet suffered FROM MAN. Ye would lie if ye spake otherwise! None"
"12270" 1998 "of you suffereth from what _I_ have suffered.--"
"12271" 1998 ""
"12272" 1998 "7."
"12273" 1998 ""
"12274" 1998 "It is not enough for me that the lightning no longer doeth harm. I do"
"12275" 1998 "not wish to conduct it away: it shall learn--to work for ME.--"
"12276" 1998 ""
"12277" 1998 "My wisdom hath accumulated long like a cloud, it becometh stiller and"
"12278" 1998 "darker. So doeth all wisdom which shall one day bear LIGHTNINGS.--"
"12279" 1998 ""
"12280" 1998 "Unto these men of to-day will I not be LIGHT, nor be called light."
"12281" 1998 "THEM--will I blind: lightning of my wisdom! put out their eyes!"
"12282" 1998 ""
"12283" 1998 "8."
"12284" 1998 ""
"12285" 1998 "Do not will anything beyond your power: there is a bad falseness in"
"12286" 1998 "those who will beyond their power."
"12287" 1998 ""
"12288" 1998 "Especially when they will great things! For they awaken distrust in"
"12289" 1998 "great things, these subtle false-coiners and stage-players:--"
"12290" 1998 ""
"12291" 1998 "--Until at last they are false towards themselves, squint-eyed, whited"
"12292" 1998 "cankers, glossed over with strong words, parade virtues and brilliant"
"12293" 1998 "false deeds."
"12294" 1998 ""
"12295" 1998 "Take good care there, ye higher men! For nothing is more precious to me,"
"12296" 1998 "and rarer, than honesty."
"12297" 1998 ""
"12298" 1998 "Is this to-day not that of the populace? The populace however knoweth"
"12299" 1998 "not what is great and what is small, what is straight and what is"
"12300" 1998 "honest: it is innocently crooked, it ever lieth."
"12301" 1998 ""
"12302" 1998 "9."
"12303" 1998 ""
"12304" 1998 "Have a good distrust to-day ye, higher men, ye enheartened ones! Ye"
"12305" 1998 "open-hearted ones! And keep your reasons secret! For this to-day is that"
"12306" 1998 "of the populace."
"12307" 1998 ""
"12308" 1998 "What the populace once learned to believe without reasons, who could--"
"12309" 1998 "refute it to them by means of reasons?"
"12310" 1998 ""
"12311" 1998 "And on the market-place one convinceth with gestures. But reasons make"
"12312" 1998 "the populace distrustful."
"12313" 1998 ""
"12314" 1998 "And when truth hath once triumphed there, then ask yourselves with good"
"12315" 1998 "distrust: \"What strong error hath fought for it?\""
"12316" 1998 ""
"12317" 1998 "Be on your guard also against the learned! They hate you, because they"
"12318" 1998 "are unproductive! They have cold, withered eyes before which every bird"
"12319" 1998 "is unplumed."
"12320" 1998 ""
"12321" 1998 "Such persons vaunt about not lying: but inability to lie is still far"
"12322" 1998 "from being love to truth. Be on your guard!"
"12323" 1998 ""
"12324" 1998 "Freedom from fever is still far from being knowledge! Refrigerated"
"12325" 1998 "spirits I do not believe in. He who cannot lie, doth not know what truth"
"12326" 1998 "is."
"12327" 1998 ""
"12328" 1998 "10."
"12329" 1998 ""
"12330" 1998 "If ye would go up high, then use your own legs! Do not get yourselves"
"12331" 1998 "CARRIED aloft; do not seat yourselves on other people's backs and heads!"
"12332" 1998 ""
"12333" 1998 "Thou hast mounted, however, on horseback? Thou now ridest briskly up"
"12334" 1998 "to thy goal? Well, my friend! But thy lame foot is also with thee on"
"12335" 1998 "horseback!"
"12336" 1998 ""
"12337" 1998 "When thou reachest thy goal, when thou alightest from thy horse:"
"12338" 1998 "precisely on thy HEIGHT, thou higher man,--then wilt thou stumble!"
"12339" 1998 ""
"12340" 1998 "11."
"12341" 1998 ""
"12342" 1998 "Ye creating ones, ye higher men! One is only pregnant with one's own"
"12343" 1998 "child."
"12344" 1998 ""
"12345" 1998 "Do not let yourselves be imposed upon or put upon! Who then is YOUR"
"12346" 1998 "neighbour? Even if ye act \"for your neighbour\"--ye still do not create"
"12347" 1998 "for him!"
"12348" 1998 ""
"12349" 1998 "Unlearn, I pray you, this \"for,\" ye creating ones: your very virtue"
"12350" 1998 "wisheth you to have naught to do with \"for\" and \"on account of\" and"
"12351" 1998 "\"because.\" Against these false little words shall ye stop your ears."
"12352" 1998 ""
"12353" 1998 "\"For one's neighbour,\" is the virtue only of the petty people: there it"
"12354" 1998 "is said \"like and like,\" and \"hand washeth hand\":--they have neither the"
"12355" 1998 "right nor the power for YOUR self-seeking!"
"12356" 1998 ""
"12357" 1998 "In your self-seeking, ye creating ones, there is the foresight and"
"12358" 1998 "foreseeing of the pregnant! What no one's eye hath yet seen, namely, the"
"12359" 1998 "fruit--this, sheltereth and saveth and nourisheth your entire love."
"12360" 1998 ""
"12361" 1998 "Where your entire love is, namely, with your child, there is also your"
"12362" 1998 "entire virtue! Your work, your will is YOUR \"neighbour\": let no false"
"12363" 1998 "values impose upon you!"
"12364" 1998 ""
"12365" 1998 "12."
"12366" 1998 ""
"12367" 1998 "Ye creating ones, ye higher men! Whoever hath to give birth is sick;"
"12368" 1998 "whoever hath given birth, however, is unclean."
"12369" 1998 ""
"12370" 1998 "Ask women: one giveth birth, not because it giveth pleasure. The pain"
"12371" 1998 "maketh hens and poets cackle."
"12372" 1998 ""
"12373" 1998 "Ye creating ones, in you there is much uncleanliness. That is because ye"
"12374" 1998 "have had to be mothers."
"12375" 1998 ""
"12376" 1998 "A new child: oh, how much new filth hath also come into the world! Go"
"12377" 1998 "apart! He who hath given birth shall wash his soul!"
"12378" 1998 ""
"12379" 1998 "13."
"12380" 1998 ""
"12381" 1998 "Be not virtuous beyond your powers! And seek nothing from yourselves"
"12382" 1998 "opposed to probability!"
"12383" 1998 ""
"12384" 1998 "Walk in the footsteps in which your fathers' virtue hath already walked!"
"12385" 1998 "How would ye rise high, if your fathers' will should not rise with you?"
"12386" 1998 ""
"12387" 1998 "He, however, who would be a firstling, let him take care lest he also"
"12388" 1998 "become a lastling! And where the vices of your fathers are, there should"
"12389" 1998 "ye not set up as saints!"
"12390" 1998 ""
"12391" 1998 "He whose fathers were inclined for women, and for strong wine and flesh"
"12392" 1998 "of wildboar swine; what would it be if he demanded chastity of himself?"
"12393" 1998 ""
"12394" 1998 "A folly would it be! Much, verily, doth it seem to me for such a one, if"
"12395" 1998 "he should be the husband of one or of two or of three women."
"12396" 1998 ""
"12397" 1998 "And if he founded monasteries, and inscribed over their portals: \"The"
"12398" 1998 "way to holiness,\"--I should still say: What good is it! it is a new"
"12399" 1998 "folly!"
"12400" 1998 ""
"12401" 1998 "He hath founded for himself a penance-house and refuge-house: much good"
"12402" 1998 "may it do! But I do not believe in it."
"12403" 1998 ""
"12404" 1998 "In solitude there groweth what any one bringeth into it--also the brute"
"12405" 1998 "in one's nature. Thus is solitude inadvisable unto many."
"12406" 1998 ""
"12407" 1998 "Hath there ever been anything filthier on earth than the saints of"
"12408" 1998 "the wilderness? AROUND THEM was not only the devil loose--but also the"
"12409" 1998 "swine."
"12410" 1998 ""
"12411" 1998 "14."
"12412" 1998 ""
"12413" 1998 "Shy, ashamed, awkward, like the tiger whose spring hath failed--thus, ye"
"12414" 1998 "higher men, have I often seen you slink aside. A CAST which ye made had"
"12415" 1998 "failed."
"12416" 1998 ""
"12417" 1998 "But what doth it matter, ye dice-players! Ye had not learned to play and"
"12418" 1998 "mock, as one must play and mock! Do we not ever sit at a great table of"
"12419" 1998 "mocking and playing?"
"12420" 1998 ""
"12421" 1998 "And if great things have been a failure with you, have ye yourselves"
"12422" 1998 "therefore--been a failure? And if ye yourselves have been a failure,"
"12423" 1998 "hath man therefore--been a failure? If man, however, hath been a"
"12424" 1998 "failure: well then! never mind!"
"12425" 1998 ""
"12426" 1998 "15."
"12427" 1998 ""
"12428" 1998 "The higher its type, always the seldomer doth a thing succeed. Ye higher"
"12429" 1998 "men here, have ye not all--been failures?"
"12430" 1998 ""
"12431" 1998 "Be of good cheer; what doth it matter? How much is still possible! Learn"
"12432" 1998 "to laugh at yourselves, as ye ought to laugh!"
"12433" 1998 ""
"12434" 1998 "What wonder even that ye have failed and only half-succeeded, ye"
"12435" 1998 "half-shattered ones! Doth not--man's FUTURE strive and struggle in you?"
"12436" 1998 ""
"12437" 1998 "Man's furthest, profoundest, star-highest issues, his prodigious"
"12438" 1998 "powers--do not all these foam through one another in your vessel?"
"12439" 1998 ""
"12440" 1998 "What wonder that many a vessel shattereth! Learn to laugh at yourselves,"
"12441" 1998 "as ye ought to laugh! Ye higher men, O, how much is still possible!"
"12442" 1998 ""
"12443" 1998 "And verily, how much hath already succeeded! How rich is this earth in"
"12444" 1998 "small, good, perfect things, in well-constituted things!"
"12445" 1998 ""
"12446" 1998 "Set around you small, good, perfect things, ye higher men. Their golden"
"12447" 1998 "maturity healeth the heart. The perfect teacheth one to hope."
"12448" 1998 ""
"12449" 1998 "16."
"12450" 1998 ""
"12451" 1998 "What hath hitherto been the greatest sin here on earth? Was it not the"
"12452" 1998 "word of him who said: \"Woe unto them that laugh now!\""
"12453" 1998 ""
"12454" 1998 "Did he himself find no cause for laughter on the earth? Then he sought"
"12455" 1998 "badly. A child even findeth cause for it."
"12456" 1998 ""
"12457" 1998 "He--did not love sufficiently: otherwise would he also have loved"
"12458" 1998 "us, the laughing ones! But he hated and hooted us; wailing and"
"12459" 1998 "teeth-gnashing did he promise us."
"12460" 1998 ""
"12461" 1998 "Must one then curse immediately, when one doth not love? That--seemeth"
"12462" 1998 "to me bad taste. Thus did he, however, this absolute one. He sprang from"
"12463" 1998 "the populace."
"12464" 1998 ""
"12465" 1998 "And he himself just did not love sufficiently; otherwise would he have"
"12466" 1998 "raged less because people did not love him. All great love doth not SEEK"
"12467" 1998 "love:--it seeketh more."
"12468" 1998 ""
"12469" 1998 "Go out of the way of all such absolute ones! They are a poor sickly"
"12470" 1998 "type, a populace-type: they look at this life with ill-will, they have"
"12471" 1998 "an evil eye for this earth."
"12472" 1998 ""
"12473" 1998 "Go out of the way of all such absolute ones! They have heavy feet and"
"12474" 1998 "sultry hearts:--they do not know how to dance. How could the earth be"
"12475" 1998 "light to such ones!"
"12476" 1998 ""
"12477" 1998 "17."
"12478" 1998 ""
"12479" 1998 "Tortuously do all good things come nigh to their goal. Like cats"
"12480" 1998 "they curve their backs, they purr inwardly with their approaching"
"12481" 1998 "happiness,--all good things laugh."
"12482" 1998 ""
"12483" 1998 "His step betrayeth whether a person already walketh on HIS OWN path:"
"12484" 1998 "just see me walk! He, however, who cometh nigh to his goal, danceth."
"12485" 1998 ""
"12486" 1998 "And verily, a statue have I not become, not yet do I stand there stiff,"
"12487" 1998 "stupid and stony, like a pillar; I love fast racing."
"12488" 1998 ""
"12489" 1998 "And though there be on earth fens and dense afflictions, he who hath"
"12490" 1998 "light feet runneth even across the mud, and danceth, as upon well-swept"
"12491" 1998 "ice."
"12492" 1998 ""
"12493" 1998 "Lift up your hearts, my brethren, high, higher! And do not forget your"
"12494" 1998 "legs! Lift up also your legs, ye good dancers, and better still, if ye"
"12495" 1998 "stand upon your heads!"
"12496" 1998 ""
"12497" 1998 "18."
"12498" 1998 ""
"12499" 1998 "This crown of the laughter, this rose-garland crown: I myself have put"
"12500" 1998 "on this crown, I myself have consecrated my laughter. No one else have I"
"12501" 1998 "found to-day potent enough for this."
"12502" 1998 ""
"12503" 1998 "Zarathustra the dancer, Zarathustra the light one, who beckoneth with"
"12504" 1998 "his pinions, one ready for flight, beckoning unto all birds, ready and"
"12505" 1998 "prepared, a blissfully light-spirited one:--"
"12506" 1998 ""
"12507" 1998 "Zarathustra the soothsayer, Zarathustra the sooth-laugher, no impatient"

"12509" 1998 "put on this crown!"
"12510" 1998 ""
"12511" 1998 "19."
"12512" 1998 ""
"12513" 1998 "Lift up your hearts, my brethren, high, higher! And do not forget your"
"12514" 1998 "legs! Lift up also your legs, ye good dancers, and better still if ye"
"12515" 1998 "stand upon your heads!"
"12516" 1998 ""
"12517" 1998 "There are also heavy animals in a state of happiness, there are"
"12518" 1998 "club-footed ones from the beginning. Curiously do they exert themselves,"
"12519" 1998 "like an elephant which endeavoureth to stand upon its head."
"12520" 1998 ""
"12521" 1998 "Better, however, to be foolish with happiness than foolish with"
"12522" 1998 "misfortune, better to dance awkwardly than walk lamely. So learn, I"
"12523" 1998 "pray you, my wisdom, ye higher men: even the worst thing hath two good"
"12524" 1998 "reverse sides,--"
"12525" 1998 ""
"12526" 1998 "--Even the worst thing hath good dancing-legs: so learn, I pray you, ye"

"12528" 1998 ""
"12529" 1998 "So unlearn, I pray you, the sorrow-sighing, and all the"
"12530" 1998 "populace-sadness! Oh, how sad the buffoons of the populace seem to me"
"12531" 1998 "to-day! This to-day, however, is that of the populace."
"12532" 1998 ""
"12533" 1998 "20."
"12534" 1998 ""
"12535" 1998 "Do like unto the wind when it rusheth forth from its mountain-caves:"
"12536" 1998 "unto its own piping will it dance; the seas tremble and leap under its"
"12537" 1998 "footsteps."
"12538" 1998 ""
"12539" 1998 "That which giveth wings to asses, that which milketh the lionesses:--"
"12540" 1998 "praised be that good, unruly spirit, which cometh like a hurricane unto"
"12541" 1998 "all the present and unto all the populace,--"
"12542" 1998 ""
"12543" 1998 "--Which is hostile to thistle-heads and puzzle-heads, and to all"

"12545" 1998 "the storm, which danceth upon fens and afflictions, as upon meadows!"
"12546" 1998 ""
"12547" 1998 "Which hateth the consumptive populace-dogs, and all the ill-constituted,"
"12548" 1998 "sullen brood:--praised be this spirit of all free spirits, the laughing"

"12634" 1998 "3."
"12635" 1998 ""
"12636" 1998 "     In evening's limpid air,"
"12637" 1998 "     What time the dew's soothings"
"12638" 1998 "     Unto the earth downpour,"
"12639" 1998 "     Invisibly and unheard--"
"12640" 1998 "     For tender shoe-gear wear"

"12642" 1998 "     Bethinkst thou then, bethinkst thou, burning heart,"

"12646" 1998 "     What time on yellow grass-pathways"

"12655" 1998 "     That wittingly, wilfully, aye must lie:"
"12656" 1998 "     For booty lusting,"
"12657" 1998 "     Motley masked,"
"12658" 1998 "     Self-hidden, shrouded,"

"12661" 1998 "     Nay!  Mere fool!  Mere poet!"
"12662" 1998 "     Just motley speaking,"
"12663" 1998 "     From mask of fool confusedly shouting,"
"12664" 1998 "     Circumambling on fabricated word-bridges,"

"12673" 1998 "     Become an image,"
"12674" 1998 "     A godlike statue,"
"12675" 1998 "     Set up in front of temples,"

"12678" 1998 "     In every desert homelier than at temples,"

"12680" 1998 "     Through every window leaping"
"12681" 1998 "     Quickly into chances,"
"12682" 1998 "     Every wild forest a-sniffing,"

"12685" 1998 "     'Mong the motley-speckled fierce creatures,"

"12704" 1998 "     Fierce 'gainst all lamb-spirits,"

"12723" 1998 ""

"12750" 1998 "LXXV. SCIENCE."
"12751" 1998 ""

"12758" 1998 ""

"12762" 1998 ""

"12936" 1998 "     --Ha!"

"13000" 1998 "     --ROUNDSPHINXED, that into one word"
"13001" 1998 "     I may crowd much feeling:"
"13002" 1998 "     (Forgive me, O God,"
"13003" 1998 "     All such speech-sinning!)"
"13004" 1998 "     --Sit I here the best of air sniffling,"
"13005" 1998 "     Paradisal air, truly,"
"13006" 1998 "     Bright and buoyant air, golden-mottled,"
"13007" 1998 "     As goodly air as ever"
"13008" 1998 "     From lunar orb downfell--"
"13009" 1998 "     Be it by hazard,"
"13010" 1998 "     Or supervened it by arrogancy?"
"13011" 1998 "     As the ancient poets relate it."
"13012" 1998 "     But doubter, I'm now calling it"
"13013" 1998 "     In question:  with this do I come indeed"
"13014" 1998 "     Out of Europe,"
"13015" 1998 "     That doubt'th more eagerly than doth any"
"13016" 1998 "     Elderly married woman."
"13017" 1998 "     May the Lord improve it!"
"13018" 1998 "     Amen."
"13019" 1998 ""
"13020" 1998 "     This the finest air drinking,"
"13021" 1998 "     With nostrils out-swelled like goblets,"
"13022" 1998 "     Lacking future, lacking remembrances"
"13023" 1998 "     Thus do I sit here, ye"
"13024" 1998 "     Friendly damsels dearly loved,"
"13025" 1998 "     And look at the palm-tree there,"
"13026" 1998 "     How it, to a dance-girl, like,"
"13027" 1998 "     Doth bow and bend and on its haunches bob,"
"13028" 1998 "     --One doth it too, when one view'th it long!--"
"13029" 1998 "     To a dance-girl like, who as it seem'th to me,"
"13030" 1998 "     Too long, and dangerously persistent,"
"13031" 1998 "     Always, always, just on SINGLE leg hath stood?"
"13032" 1998 "     --Then forgot she thereby, as it seem'th to me,"
"13033" 1998 "     The OTHER leg?"
"13034" 1998 "     For vainly I, at least,"
"13035" 1998 "     Did search for the amissing"
"13036" 1998 "     Fellow-jewel"
"13037" 1998 "     --Namely, the other leg--"
"13038" 1998 "     In the sanctified precincts,"
"13039" 1998 "     Nigh her very dearest, very tenderest,"
"13040" 1998 "     Flapping and fluttering and flickering skirting."
"13041" 1998 "     Yea, if ye should, ye beauteous friendly ones,"
"13042" 1998 "     Quite take my word:"
"13043" 1998 "     She hath, alas! LOST it!"
"13044" 1998 "     Hu!  Hu!  Hu!  Hu!  Hu!"
"13045" 1998 "     It is away!"
"13046" 1998 "     For ever away!"
"13047" 1998 "     The other leg!"
"13048" 1998 "     Oh, pity for that loveliest other leg!"
"13049" 1998 "     Where may it now tarry, all-forsaken weeping?"
"13050" 1998 "     The lonesomest leg?"
"13051" 1998 "     In fear perhaps before a"
"13052" 1998 "     Furious, yellow, blond and curled"
"13053" 1998 "     Leonine monster?  Or perhaps even"
"13054" 1998 "     Gnawed away, nibbled badly--"
"13055" 1998 "     Most wretched, woeful! woeful! nibbled badly!  Selah."
"13056" 1998 ""
"13057" 1998 "     Oh, weep ye not,"
"13058" 1998 "     Gentle spirits!"
"13059" 1998 "     Weep ye not, ye"
"13060" 1998 "     Date-fruit spirits!  Milk-bosoms!"
"13061" 1998 "     Ye sweetwood-heart"
"13062" 1998 "     Purselets!"
"13063" 1998 "     Weep ye no more,"
"13064" 1998 "     Pallid Dudu!"
"13065" 1998 "     Be a man, Suleika!  Bold!  Bold!"
"13066" 1998 "     --Or else should there perhaps"
"13067" 1998 "     Something strengthening, heart-strengthening,"
"13068" 1998 "     Here most proper be?"
"13069" 1998 "     Some inspiring text?"
"13070" 1998 "     Some solemn exhortation?--"
"13071" 1998 "     Ha!  Up now! honour!"
"13072" 1998 "     Moral honour!  European honour!"
"13073" 1998 "     Blow again, continue,"
"13074" 1998 "     Bellows-box of virtue!"
"13075" 1998 "     Ha!"
"13076" 1998 "     Once more thy roaring,"
"13077" 1998 "     Thy moral roaring!"
"13078" 1998 "     As a virtuous lion"
"13079" 1998 "     Nigh the daughters of deserts roaring!"
"13080" 1998 "     --For virtue's out-howl,"
"13081" 1998 "     Ye very dearest maidens,"
"13082" 1998 "     Is more than every"
"13083" 1998 "     European fervour, European hot-hunger!"
"13084" 1998 "     And now do I stand here,"
"13085" 1998 "     As European,"
"13086" 1998 "     I can't be different, God's help to me!"
"13087" 1998 "     Amen!"

"13094" 1998 "LXXVII. THE AWAKENING."
"13095" 1998 ""
"13096" 1998 "1."
"13097" 1998 ""
"13098" 1998 "After the song of the wanderer and shadow, the cave became all at once"

"13105" 1998 ""

"13109" 1998 ""

"13117" 1998 ""

"13121" 1998 ""

"13125" 1998 ""

"13129" 1998 ""

"13136" 1998 ""

"13140" 1998 ""

"13155" 1998 ""

"13165" 1998 ""
"13166" 1998 "2."

"13194" 1998 ""

"13401" 1998 "1."

"13441" 1998 "then, as some narrators suppose, full of sweet wine, he was certainly"
"13442" 1998 "still fuller of sweet life, and had renounced all weariness. There are"
"13443" 1998 "even those who narrate that the ass then danced: for not in vain had the"
"13444" 1998 "ugliest man previously given it wine to drink. That may be the case, or"
"13445" 1998 "it may be otherwise; and if in truth the ass did not dance that evening,"
"13446" 1998 "there nevertheless happened then greater and rarer wonders than"
"13447" 1998 "the dancing of an ass would have been. In short, as the proverb of"
"13448" 1998 "Zarathustra saith: \"What doth it matter!\""
"13449" 1998 ""
"13450" 1998 "2."
"13451" 1998 ""
"13452" 1998 "When, however, this took place with the ugliest man, Zarathustra stood"
"13453" 1998 "there like one drunken: his glance dulled, his tongue faltered and his"
"13454" 1998 "feet staggered. And who could divine what thoughts then passed through"
"13455" 1998 "Zarathustra's soul? Apparently, however, his spirit retreated and fled"
"13456" 1998 "in advance and was in remote distances, and as it were \"wandering on"
"13457" 1998 "high mountain-ridges,\" as it standeth written, \"'twixt two seas,"
"13458" 1998 ""
"13459" 1998 "--Wandering 'twixt the past and the future as a heavy cloud.\" Gradually,"
"13460" 1998 "however, while the higher men held him in their arms, he came back to"
"13461" 1998 "himself a little, and resisted with his hands the crowd of the honouring"
"13462" 1998 "and caring ones; but he did not speak. All at once, however, he turned"
"13463" 1998 "his head quickly, for he seemed to hear something: then laid he his"
"13464" 1998 "finger on his mouth and said: \"COME!\""
"13465" 1998 ""
"13466" 1998 "And immediately it became still and mysterious round about; from"
"13467" 1998 "the depth however there came up slowly the sound of a clock-bell."
"13468" 1998 "Zarathustra listened thereto, like the higher men; then, however, laid"
"13469" 1998 "he his finger on his mouth the second time, and said again: \"COME! COME!"
"13470" 1998 "IT IS GETTING ON TO MIDNIGHT!\"--and his voice had changed. But still"
"13471" 1998 "he had not moved from the spot. Then it became yet stiller and more"
"13472" 1998 "mysterious, and everything hearkened, even the ass, and Zarathustra's"
"13473" 1998 "noble animals, the eagle and the serpent,--likewise the cave of"
"13474" 1998 "Zarathustra and the big cool moon, and the night itself. Zarathustra,"
"13475" 1998 "however, laid his hand upon his mouth for the third time, and said:"
"13476" 1998 ""
"13477" 1998 "COME! COME! COME! LET US NOW WANDER! IT IS THE HOUR: LET US WANDER INTO"
"13478" 1998 "THE NIGHT!"
"13479" 1998 ""
"13480" 1998 "3."
"13481" 1998 ""
"13482" 1998 "Ye higher men, it is getting on to midnight: then will I say something"
"13483" 1998 "into your ears, as that old clock-bell saith it into mine ear,--"
"13484" 1998 ""
"13485" 1998 "--As mysteriously, as frightfully, and as cordially as that midnight"
"13486" 1998 "clock-bell speaketh it to me, which hath experienced more than one man:"
"13487" 1998 ""
"13488" 1998 "--Which hath already counted the smarting throbbings of your fathers'"
"13489" 1998 "hearts--ah! ah! how it sigheth! how it laugheth in its dream! the old,"
"13490" 1998 "deep, deep midnight!"
"13491" 1998 ""

"13495" 1998 ""

"13499" 1998 ""

"13502" 1998 ""
"13503" 1998 "O MAN, TAKE HEED!"

"13507" 1998 "Woe to me! Whither hath time gone? Have I not sunk into deep wells? The"

"13509" 1998 ""

"13512" 1998 ""

"13515" 1998 ""

"13524" 1998 ""

"13526" 1998 ""
"13527" 1998 "It carrieth me away, my soul danceth. Day's-work! Day's-work! Who is to"

"13529" 1998 ""

"13547" 1998 ""

"13551" 1998 ""

"13568" 1998 ""
"13569" 1998 "7."
"13570" 1998 ""
"13571" 1998 "Leave me alone! Leave me alone! I am too pure for thee. Touch me not!"
"13572" 1998 "Hath not my world just now become perfect?"
"13573" 1998 ""
"13574" 1998 "My skin is too pure for thy hands. Leave me alone, thou dull, doltish,"
"13575" 1998 "stupid day! Is not the midnight brighter?"
"13576" 1998 ""
"13577" 1998 "The purest are to be masters of the world, the least known, the"
"13578" 1998 "strongest, the midnight-souls, who are brighter and deeper than any day."
"13579" 1998 ""

"13611" 1998 "--Her woe doth she ruminate over, in a dream, the old, deep"

"13614" 1998 ""

"13616" 1998 ""
"13617" 1998 "Thou grape-vine! Why dost thou praise me? Have I not cut thee! I am"

"13619" 1998 ""

"13623" 1998 ""

"13634" 1998 ""

"13638" 1998 ""
"13639" 1998 "10."
"13640" 1998 ""
"13641" 1998 "Ye higher men, what think ye? Am I a soothsayer? Or a dreamer? Or a"

"13643" 1998 ""

"13647" 1998 ""

"13650" 1998 ""

"13653" 1998 ""

"13659" 1998 ""

"13662" 1998 ""

"13664" 1998 ""

"13700" 1998 ""
"13701" 1998 "     O man!  Take heed!"

"13716" 1998 "LXXX. THE SIGN."
"13717" 1998 ""
"13718" 1998 "In the morning, however, after this night, Zarathustra jumped up from"
"13719" 1998 "his couch, and, having girded his loins, he came out of his cave glowing"
"13720" 1998 "and strong, like a morning sun coming out of gloomy mountains."
"13721" 1998 ""
"13722" 1998 "\"Thou great star,\" spake he, as he had spoken once before, \"thou deep"
"13723" 1998 "eye of happiness, what would be all thy happiness if thou hadst not"
"13724" 1998 "THOSE for whom thou shinest!"
"13725" 1998 ""
"13726" 1998 "And if they remained in their chambers whilst thou art already awake,"
"13727" 1998 "and comest and bestowest and distributest, how would thy proud modesty"
"13728" 1998 "upbraid for it!"
"13729" 1998 ""
"13730" 1998 "Well! they still sleep, these higher men, whilst _I_ am awake: THEY are"
"13731" 1998 "not my proper companions! Not for them do I wait here in my mountains."
"13732" 1998 ""
"13733" 1998 "At my work I want to be, at my day: but they understand not what are the"
"13734" 1998 "signs of my morning, my step--is not for them the awakening-call."
"13735" 1998 ""
"13736" 1998 "They still sleep in my cave; their dream still drinketh at my drunken"
"13737" 1998 "songs. The audient ear for ME--the OBEDIENT ear, is yet lacking in their"
"13738" 1998 "limbs.\""
"13739" 1998 ""
"13740" 1998 "--This had Zarathustra spoken to his heart when the sun arose: then"
"13741" 1998 "looked he inquiringly aloft, for he heard above him the sharp call of"
"13742" 1998 "his eagle. \"Well!\" called he upwards, \"thus is it pleasing and proper to"
"13743" 1998 "me. Mine animals are awake, for I am awake."
"13744" 1998 ""
"13745" 1998 "Mine eagle is awake, and like me honoureth the sun. With eagle-talons"
"13746" 1998 "doth it grasp at the new light. Ye are my proper animals; I love you."
"13747" 1998 ""
"13748" 1998 "But still do I lack my proper men!\"--"
"13749" 1998 ""
"13750" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra; then, however, it happened that all on a sudden"
"13751" 1998 "he became aware that he was flocked around and fluttered around, as if"
"13752" 1998 "by innumerable birds,--the whizzing of so many wings, however, and the"
"13753" 1998 "crowding around his head was so great that he shut his eyes. And verily,"
"13754" 1998 "there came down upon him as it were a cloud, like a cloud of arrows"
"13755" 1998 "which poureth upon a new enemy. But behold, here it was a cloud of love,"
"13756" 1998 "and showered upon a new friend."
"13757" 1998 ""
"13758" 1998 "\"What happeneth unto me?\" thought Zarathustra in his astonished heart,"
"13759" 1998 "and slowly seated himself on the big stone which lay close to the exit"
"13760" 1998 "from his cave. But while he grasped about with his hands, around him,"
"13761" 1998 "above him and below him, and repelled the tender birds, behold, there"
"13762" 1998 "then happened to him something still stranger: for he grasped thereby"
"13763" 1998 "unawares into a mass of thick, warm, shaggy hair; at the same time,"
"13764" 1998 "however, there sounded before him a roar,--a long, soft lion-roar."
"13765" 1998 ""
"13766" 1998 "\"THE SIGN COMETH,\" said Zarathustra, and a change came over his heart."
"13767" 1998 "And in truth, when it turned clear before him, there lay a yellow,"
"13768" 1998 "powerful animal at his feet, resting its head on his knee,--unwilling to"
"13769" 1998 "leave him out of love, and doing like a dog which again findeth its old"
"13770" 1998 "master. The doves, however, were no less eager with their love than the"
"13771" 1998 "lion; and whenever a dove whisked over its nose, the lion shook its head"
"13772" 1998 "and wondered and laughed."
"13773" 1998 ""
"13774" 1998 "When all this went on Zarathustra spake only a word: \"MY CHILDREN ARE"
"13775" 1998 "NIGH, MY CHILDREN\"--, then he became quite mute. His heart, however,"
"13776" 1998 "was loosed, and from his eyes there dropped down tears and fell upon"
"13777" 1998 "his hands. And he took no further notice of anything, but sat there"
"13778" 1998 "motionless, without repelling the animals further. Then flew the doves"
"13779" 1998 "to and fro, and perched on his shoulder, and caressed his white hair,"
"13780" 1998 "and did not tire of their tenderness and joyousness. The strong lion,"
"13781" 1998 "however, licked always the tears that fell on Zarathustra's hands, and"
"13782" 1998 "roared and growled shyly. Thus did these animals do.--"
"13783" 1998 ""
"13784" 1998 "All this went on for a long time, or a short time: for properly"
"13785" 1998 "speaking, there is NO time on earth for such things--. Meanwhile,"
"13786" 1998 "however, the higher men had awakened in Zarathustra's cave, and"
"13787" 1998 "marshalled themselves for a procession to go to meet Zarathustra, and"
"13788" 1998 "give him their morning greeting: for they had found when they awakened"
"13789" 1998 "that he no longer tarried with them. When, however, they reached the"
"13790" 1998 "door of the cave and the noise of their steps had preceded them, the"
"13791" 1998 "lion started violently; it turned away all at once from Zarathustra, and"
"13792" 1998 "roaring wildly, sprang towards the cave. The higher men, however, when"
"13793" 1998 "they heard the lion roaring, cried all aloud as with one voice, fled"
"13794" 1998 "back and vanished in an instant."
"13795" 1998 ""
"13796" 1998 "Zarathustra himself, however, stunned and strange, rose from his seat,"
"13797" 1998 "looked around him, stood there astonished, inquired of his heart,"
"13798" 1998 "bethought himself, and remained alone. \"What did I hear?\" said he at"
"13799" 1998 "last, slowly, \"what happened unto me just now?\""
"13800" 1998 ""
"13801" 1998 "But soon there came to him his recollection, and he took in at a glance"
"13802" 1998 "all that had taken place between yesterday and to-day. \"Here is indeed"
"13803" 1998 "the stone,\" said he, and stroked his beard, \"on IT sat I yester-morn;"
"13804" 1998 "and here came the soothsayer unto me, and here heard I first the cry"
"13805" 1998 "which I heard just now, the great cry of distress."
"13806" 1998 ""
"13807" 1998 "O ye higher men, YOUR distress was it that the old soothsayer foretold"
"13808" 1998 "to me yester-morn,--"
"13809" 1998 ""
"13810" 1998 "--Unto your distress did he want to seduce and tempt me: 'O"
"13811" 1998 "Zarathustra,' said he to me, 'I come to seduce thee to thy last sin.'"
"13812" 1998 ""
"13813" 1998 "To my last sin?\" cried Zarathustra, and laughed angrily at his own"
"13814" 1998 "words: \"WHAT hath been reserved for me as my last sin?\""
"13815" 1998 ""
"13816" 1998 "--And once more Zarathustra became absorbed in himself, and sat down"
"13817" 1998 "again on the big stone and meditated. Suddenly he sprang up,--"
"13818" 1998 ""
"13819" 1998 "\"FELLOW-SUFFERING! FELLOW-SUFFERING WITH THE HIGHER MEN!\" he cried out,"
"13820" 1998 "and his countenance changed into brass. \"Well! THAT--hath had its time!"
"13821" 1998 ""
"13822" 1998 "My suffering and my fellow-suffering--what matter about them! Do I then"
"13823" 1998 "strive after HAPPINESS? I strive after my WORK!"
"13824" 1998 ""
"13825" 1998 "Well! The lion hath come, my children are nigh, Zarathustra hath grown"
"13826" 1998 "ripe, mine hour hath come:--"
"13827" 1998 ""
"13828" 1998 "This is MY morning, MY day beginneth: ARISE NOW, ARISE, THOU GREAT"
"13829" 1998 "NOONTIDE!\"--"
"13830" 1998 ""
"13831" 1998 "Thus spake Zarathustra and left his cave, glowing and strong, like a"
"13832" 1998 "morning sun coming out of gloomy mountains."
"13833" 1998 ""
"13834" 1998 ""
"13835" 1998 ""
"13836" 1998 ""
"13837" 1998 "APPENDIX."
"13838" 1998 ""
"13839" 1998 "NOTES ON \"THUS SPAKE ZARATHUSTRA\" BY ANTHONY M. LUDOVICI."
"13840" 1998 ""
"13841" 1998 "I have had some opportunities of studying the conditions under which"
"13842" 1998 "Nietzsche is read in Germany, France, and England, and I have found"
"13843" 1998 "that, in each of these countries, students of his philosophy, as if"
"13844" 1998 "actuated by precisely similar motives and desires, and misled by the"
"13845" 1998 "same mistaken tactics on the part of most publishers, all proceed in the"
"13846" 1998 "same happy-go-lucky style when \"taking him up.\" They have had it said to"
"13847" 1998 "them that he wrote without any system, and they very naturally conclude"
"13848" 1998 "that it does not matter in the least whether they begin with his first,"
"13849" 1998 "third, or last book, provided they can obtain a few vague ideas as to"
"13850" 1998 "what his leading and most sensational principles were."
"13851" 1998 ""
"13852" 1998 "Now, it is clear that the book with the most mysterious, startling, or"
"13853" 1998 "suggestive title, will always stand the best chance of being purchased"
"13854" 1998 "by those who have no other criteria to guide them in their choice"
"13855" 1998 "than the aspect of a title-page; and this explains why \"Thus Spake"
"13856" 1998 "Zarathustra\" is almost always the first and often the only one of"
"13857" 1998 "Nietzsche's books that falls into the hands of the uninitiated."
"13858" 1998 ""
"13859" 1998 "The title suggests all kinds of mysteries; a glance at the"
"13860" 1998 "chapter-headings quickly confirms the suspicions already aroused,"
"13861" 1998 "and the sub-title: \"A Book for All and None\", generally succeeds in"
"13862" 1998 "dissipating the last doubts the prospective purchaser may entertain"
"13863" 1998 "concerning his fitness for the book or its fitness for him. And what"
"13864" 1998 "happens?"
"13865" 1998 ""
"13866" 1998 "\"Thus Spake Zarathustra\" is taken home; the reader, who perchance may"
"13867" 1998 "know no more concerning Nietzsche than a magazine article has told him,"
"13868" 1998 "tries to read it and, understanding less than half he reads, probably"
"13869" 1998 "never gets further than the second or third part,--and then only to feel"
"13870" 1998 "convinced that Nietzsche himself was \"rather hazy\" as to what he was"
"13871" 1998 "talking about. Such chapters as \"The Child with the Mirror\", \"In the"
"13872" 1998 "Happy Isles\", \"The Grave-Song,\" \"Immaculate Perception,\" \"The Stillest"
"13873" 1998 "Hour\", \"The Seven Seals\", and many others, are almost utterly devoid of"
"13874" 1998 "meaning to all those who do not know something of Nietzsche's life, his"
"13875" 1998 "aims and his friendships."
"13876" 1998 ""
"13877" 1998 "As a matter of fact, \"Thus Spake Zarathustra\", though it is"
"13878" 1998 "unquestionably Nietzsche's opus magnum, is by no means the first of"
"13879" 1998 "Nietzsche's works that the beginner ought to undertake to read. The"
"13880" 1998 "author himself refers to it as the deepest work ever offered to the"
"13881" 1998 "German public, and elsewhere speaks of his other writings as being"
"13882" 1998 "necessary for the understanding of it. But when it is remembered that"
"13883" 1998 "in Zarathustra we not only have the history of his most intimate"
"13884" 1998 "experiences, friendships, feuds, disappointments, triumphs and the like,"
"13885" 1998 "but that the very form in which they are narrated is one which tends"
"13886" 1998 "rather to obscure than to throw light upon them, the difficulties which"
"13887" 1998 "meet the reader who starts quite unprepared will be seen to be really"
"13888" 1998 "formidable."
"13889" 1998 ""
"13890" 1998 "Zarathustra, then,--this shadowy, allegorical personality, speaking in"
"13891" 1998 "allegories and parables, and at times not even refraining from relating"
"13892" 1998 "his own dreams--is a figure we can understand but very imperfectly if we"
"13893" 1998 "have no knowledge of his creator and counterpart, Friedrich Nietzsche;"
"13894" 1998 "and it were therefore well, previous to our study of the more abstruse"
"13895" 1998 "parts of this book, if we were to turn to some authoritative book on"
"13896" 1998 "Nietzsche's life and works and to read all that is there said on the"
"13897" 1998 "subject. Those who can read German will find an excellent guide, in this"
"13898" 1998 "respect, in Frau Foerster-Nietzsche's exhaustive and highly interesting"
"13899" 1998 "biography of her brother: \"Das Leben Friedrich Nietzsche's\" (published"
"13900" 1998 "by Naumann); while the works of Deussen, Raoul Richter, and Baroness"
"13901" 1998 "Isabelle von Unger-Sternberg, will be found to throw useful and"
"13902" 1998 "necessary light upon many questions which it would be difficult for a"
"13903" 1998 "sister to touch upon."
"13904" 1998 ""
"13905" 1998 "In regard to the actual philosophical views expounded in this work,"
"13906" 1998 "there is an excellent way of clearing up any difficulties they may"
"13907" 1998 "present, and that is by an appeal to Nietzsche's other works. Again and"
"13908" 1998 "again, of course, he will be found to express himself so clearly that"
"13909" 1998 "all reference to his other writings may be dispensed with; but where"
"13910" 1998 "this is not the case, the advice he himself gives is after all the best"
"13911" 1998 "to be followed here, viz.:--to regard such works as: \"Joyful Science\","
"13912" 1998 "\"Beyond Good and Evil\", \"The Genealogy of Morals\", \"The Twilight of"
"13913" 1998 "the Idols\", \"The Antichrist\", \"The Will to Power\", etc., etc., as the"
"13914" 1998 "necessary preparation for \"Thus Spake Zarathustra\"."
"13915" 1998 ""
"13916" 1998 "These directions, though they are by no means simple to carry out, seem"
"13917" 1998 "at least to possess the quality of definiteness and straightforwardness."
"13918" 1998 "\"Follow them and all will be clear,\" I seem to imply. But I regret to"
"13919" 1998 "say that this is not really the case. For my experience tells me that"
"13920" 1998 "even after the above directions have been followed with the greatest"
"13921" 1998 "possible zeal, the student will still halt in perplexity before certain"
"13922" 1998 "passages in the book before us, and wonder what they mean. Now, it is"
"13923" 1998 "with the view of giving a little additional help to all those who find"
"13924" 1998 "themselves in this position that I proceed to put forth my own personal"
"13925" 1998 "interpretation of the more abstruse passages in this work."
"13926" 1998 ""
"13927" 1998 "In offering this little commentary to the Nietzsche student, I should"
"13928" 1998 "like it to be understood that I make no claim as to its infallibility or"
"13929" 1998 "indispensability. It represents but an attempt on my part--a very feeble"
"13930" 1998 "one perhaps--to give the reader what little help I can in surmounting"
"13931" 1998 "difficulties which a long study of Nietzsche's life and works has"
"13932" 1998 "enabled me, partially I hope, to overcome."
"13933" 1998 ""
"13934" 1998 "..."
"13935" 1998 ""
"13936" 1998 "Perhaps it would be as well to start out with a broad and rapid sketch"
"13937" 1998 "of Nietzsche as a writer on Morals, Evolution, and Sociology, so that"
"13938" 1998 "the reader may be prepared to pick out for himself, so to speak, all"
"13939" 1998 "passages in this work bearing in any way upon Nietzsche's views in those"
"13940" 1998 "three important branches of knowledge."
"13941" 1998 ""
"13942" 1998 "(A.) Nietzsche and Morality."
"13943" 1998 ""
"13944" 1998 "In morality, Nietzsche starts out by adopting the position of the"
"13945" 1998 "relativist. He says there are no absolute values \"good\" and \"evil\";"
"13946" 1998 "these are mere means adopted by all in order to acquire power to"
"13947" 1998 "maintain their place in the world, or to become supreme. It is the"
"13948" 1998 "lion's good to devour an antelope. It is the dead-leaf butterfly's"
"13949" 1998 "good to tell a foe a falsehood. For when the dead-leaf butterfly is in"
"13950" 1998 "danger, it clings to the side of a twig, and what it says to its foe is"
"13951" 1998 "practically this: \"I am not a butterfly, I am a dead leaf, and can be"
"13952" 1998 "of no use to thee.\" This is a lie which is good to the butterfly, for"
"13953" 1998 "it preserves it. In nature every species of organic being instinctively"
"13954" 1998 "adopts and practises those acts which most conduce to the prevalence"
"13955" 1998 "or supremacy of its kind. Once the most favourable order of conduct is"
"13956" 1998 "found, proved efficient and established, it becomes the ruling morality"
"13957" 1998 "of the species that adopts it and bears them along to victory. All"
"13958" 1998 "species must not and cannot value alike, for what is the lion's good is"
"13959" 1998 "the antelope's evil and vice versa."
"13960" 1998 ""
"13961" 1998 "Concepts of good and evil are therefore, in their origin, merely a means"
"13962" 1998 "to an end, they are expedients for acquiring power."
"13963" 1998 ""
"13964" 1998 "Applying this principle to mankind, Nietzsche attacked Christian"
"13965" 1998 "moral values. He declared them to be, like all other morals, merely"
"13966" 1998 "an expedient for protecting a certain type of man. In the case of"
"13967" 1998 "Christianity this type was, according to Nietzsche, a low one."
"13968" 1998 ""
"13969" 1998 "Conflicting moral codes have been no more than the conflicting weapons"
"13970" 1998 "of different classes of men; for in mankind there is a continual war"
"13971" 1998 "between the powerful, the noble, the strong, and the well-constituted"
"13972" 1998 "on the one side, and the impotent, the mean, the weak, and the"
"13973" 1998 "ill-constituted on the other. The war is a war of moral principles."
"13974" 1998 "The morality of the powerful class, Nietzsche calls NOBLE- or"
"13975" 1998 "MASTER-MORALITY; that of the weak and subordinate class he calls"
"13976" 1998 "SLAVE-MORALITY. In the first morality it is the eagle which, looking"
"13977" 1998 "down upon a browsing lamb, contends that \"eating lamb is good.\" In the"
"13978" 1998 "second, the slave-morality, it is the lamb which, looking up from the"
"13979" 1998 "sward, bleats dissentingly: \"Eating lamb is evil.\""
"13980" 1998 ""
"13981" 1998 "(B.) The Master- and Slave-Morality Compared."
"13982" 1998 ""
"13983" 1998 "The first morality is active, creative, Dionysian. The second is"
"13984" 1998 "passive, defensive,--to it belongs the \"struggle for existence.\""
"13985" 1998 ""
"13986" 1998 "Where attempts have not been made to reconcile the two moralities, they"
"13987" 1998 "may be described as follows:--All is GOOD in the noble morality which"
"13988" 1998 "proceeds from strength, power, health, well-constitutedness, happiness,"
"13989" 1998 "and awfulness; for, the motive force behind the people practising it is"
"13990" 1998 "\"the struggle for power.\" The antithesis \"good and bad\" to this"
"13991" 1998 "first class means the same as \"noble\" and \"despicable.\" \"Bad\" in the"
"13992" 1998 "master-morality must be applied to the coward, to all acts that spring"
"13993" 1998 "from weakness, to the man with \"an eye to the main chance,\" who would"
"13994" 1998 "forsake everything in order to live."
"13995" 1998 ""
"13996" 1998 "With the second, the slave-morality, the case is different. There,"
"13997" 1998 "inasmuch as the community is an oppressed, suffering, unemancipated, and"
"13998" 1998 "weary one, all THAT will be held to be good which alleviates the"
"13999" 1998 "state of suffering. Pity, the obliging hand, the warm heart, patience,"
"14000" 1998 "industry, and humility--these are unquestionably the qualities we shall"
"14001" 1998 "here find flooded with the light of approval and admiration; because"
"14002" 1998 "they are the most USEFUL qualities--; they make life endurable, they are"
"14003" 1998 "of assistance in the \"struggle for existence\" which is the motive force"
"14004" 1998 "behind the people practising this morality. To this class, all that is"
"14005" 1998 "AWFUL is bad, in fact it is THE evil par excellence. Strength, health,"
"14006" 1998 "superabundance of animal spirits and power, are regarded with hate,"
"14007" 1998 "suspicion, and fear by the subordinate class."
"14008" 1998 ""
"14009" 1998 "Now Nietzsche believed that the first or the noble-morality conduced to"
"14010" 1998 "an ascent in the line of life; because it was creative and active. On"
"14011" 1998 "the other hand, he believed that the second or slave-morality, where"
"14012" 1998 "it became paramount, led to degeneration, because it was passive and"
"14013" 1998 "defensive, wanting merely to keep those who practised it alive. Hence"
"14014" 1998 "his earnest advocacy of noble-morality."
"14015" 1998 ""
"14016" 1998 "(C.) Nietzsche and Evolution."
"14017" 1998 ""
"14018" 1998 "Nietzsche as an evolutionist I shall have occasion to define and discuss"
"14019" 1998 "in the course of these notes (see Notes on Chapter LVI., par.10, and on"
"14020" 1998 "Chapter LVII.). For the present let it suffice for us to know that he"
"14021" 1998 "accepted the \"Development Hypothesis\" as an explanation of the origin of"
"14022" 1998 "species: but he did not halt where most naturalists have halted. He"
"14023" 1998 "by no means regarded man as the highest possible being which evolution"
"14024" 1998 "could arrive at; for though his physical development may have reached"
"14025" 1998 "its limit, this is not the case with his mental or spiritual attributes."
"14026" 1998 "If the process be a fact; if things have BECOME what they are, then, he"
"14027" 1998 "contends, we may describe no limit to man's aspirations. If he struggled"
"14028" 1998 "up from barbarism, and still more remotely from the lower Primates,"
"14029" 1998 "his ideal should be to surpass man himself and reach Superman (see"
"14030" 1998 "especially the Prologue)."
"14031" 1998 ""
"14032" 1998 "(D.) Nietzsche and Sociology."
"14033" 1998 ""
"14034" 1998 "Nietzsche as a sociologist aims at an aristocratic arrangement of"
"14035" 1998 "society. He would have us rear an ideal race. Honest and truthful in"
"14036" 1998 "intellectual matters, he could not even think that men are equal. \"With"
"14037" 1998 "these preachers of equality will I not be mixed up and confounded. For"
"14038" 1998 "thus speaketh justice unto ME: 'Men are not equal.'\" He sees precisely"
"14039" 1998 "in this inequality a purpose to be served, a condition to be exploited."
"14040" 1998 "\"Every elevation of the type 'man,'\" he writes in \"Beyond Good and"
"14041" 1998 "Evil\", \"has hitherto been the work of an aristocratic society--and so"
"14042" 1998 "will it always be--a society believing in a long scale of gradations of"
"14043" 1998 "rank and differences of worth among human beings.\""
"14044" 1998 ""
"14045" 1998 "Those who are sufficiently interested to desire to read his own detailed"
"14046" 1998 "account of the society he would fain establish, will find an excellent"
"14047" 1998 "passage in Aphorism 57 of \"The Antichrist\"."
"14048" 1998 ""
"14049" 1998 "..."
"14050" 1998 ""
"14051" 1998 "PART I. THE PROLOGUE."
"14052" 1998 ""
"14053" 1998 "In Part I. including the Prologue, no very great difficulties will"
"14054" 1998 "appear. Zarathustra's habit of designating a whole class of men or a"
"14055" 1998 "whole school of thought by a single fitting nickname may perhaps lead to"
"14056" 1998 "a little confusion at first; but, as a rule, when the general drift"
"14057" 1998 "of his arguments is grasped, it requires but a slight effort of the"
"14058" 1998 "imagination to discover whom he is referring to. In the ninth paragraph"
"14059" 1998 "of the Prologue, for instance, it is quite obvious that \"Herdsmen\" in"
"14060" 1998 "the verse \"Herdsmen, I say, etc., etc.,\" stands for all those to-day"
"14061" 1998 "who are the advocates of gregariousness--of the ant-hill. And when our"
"14062" 1998 "author says: \"A robber shall Zarathustra be called by the herdsmen,\" it"
"14063" 1998 "is clear that these words may be taken almost literally from one whose"
"14064" 1998 "ideal was the rearing of a higher aristocracy. Again, \"the good and"
"14065" 1998 "just,\" throughout the book, is the expression used in referring to the"
"14066" 1998 "self-righteous of modern times,--those who are quite sure that they"
"14067" 1998 "know all that is to be known concerning good and evil, and are satisfied"
"14068" 1998 "that the values their little world of tradition has handed down to them,"
"14069" 1998 "are destined to rule mankind as long as it lasts."
"14070" 1998 ""
"14071" 1998 "In the last paragraph of the Prologue, verse 7, Zarathustra gives us a"
"14072" 1998 "foretaste of his teaching concerning the big and the little sagacities,"
"14073" 1998 "expounded subsequently. He says he would he were as wise as his serpent;"
"14074" 1998 "this desire will be found explained in the discourse entitled \"The"
"14075" 1998 "Despisers of the Body\", which I shall have occasion to refer to later."
"14076" 1998 ""
"14077" 1998 "..."
"14078" 1998 ""
"14079" 1998 "THE DISCOURSES."
"14080" 1998 ""
"14081" 1998 "Chapter I. The Three Metamorphoses."
"14082" 1998 ""
"14083" 1998 "This opening discourse is a parable in which Zarathustra discloses the"
"14084" 1998 "mental development of all creators of new values. It is the story of"
"14085" 1998 "a life which reaches its consummation in attaining to a second"
"14086" 1998 "ingenuousness or in returning to childhood. Nietzsche, the supposed"
"14087" 1998 "anarchist, here plainly disclaims all relationship whatever to anarchy,"
"14088" 1998 "for he shows us that only by bearing the burdens of the existing law and"
"14089" 1998 "submitting to it patiently, as the camel submits to being laden, does"
"14090" 1998 "the free spirit acquire that ascendancy over tradition which enables him"
"14091" 1998 "to meet and master the dragon \"Thou shalt,\"--the dragon with the values"
"14092" 1998 "of a thousand years glittering on its scales. There are two lessons in"
"14093" 1998 "this discourse: first, that in order to create one must be as a little"
"14094" 1998 "child; secondly, that it is only through existing law and order that"
"14095" 1998 "one attains to that height from which new law and new order may be"
"14096" 1998 "promulgated."
"14097" 1998 ""
"14098" 1998 "Chapter II. The Academic Chairs of Virtue."
"14099" 1998 ""
"14100" 1998 "Almost the whole of this is quite comprehensible. It is a discourse"
"14101" 1998 "against all those who confound virtue with tameness and smug ease, and"
"14102" 1998 "who regard as virtuous only that which promotes security and tends to"
"14103" 1998 "deepen sleep."
"14104" 1998 ""
"14105" 1998 "Chapter IV. The Despisers of the Body."
"14106" 1998 ""
"14107" 1998 "Here Zarathustra gives names to the intellect and the instincts; he"
"14108" 1998 "calls the one \"the little sagacity\" and the latter \"the big sagacity.\""
"14109" 1998 "Schopenhauer's teaching concerning the intellect is fully endorsed here."
"14110" 1998 "\"An instrument of thy body is also thy little sagacity, my brother,"
"14111" 1998 "which thou callest 'spirit,'\" says Zarathustra. From beginning to end it"
"14112" 1998 "is a warning to those who would think too lightly of the instincts"
"14113" 1998 "and unduly exalt the intellect and its derivatives: Reason and"
"14114" 1998 "Understanding."
"14115" 1998 ""
"14116" 1998 "Chapter IX. The Preachers of Death."
"14117" 1998 ""
"14118" 1998 "This is an analysis of the psychology of all those who have the \"evil"
"14119" 1998 "eye\" and are pessimists by virtue of their constitutions."
"14120" 1998 ""
"14121" 1998 "Chapter XV. The Thousand and One Goals."
"14122" 1998 ""
"14123" 1998 "In this discourse Zarathustra opens his exposition of the doctrine of"
"14124" 1998 "relativity in morality, and declares all morality to be a mere means"
"14125" 1998 "to power. Needless to say that verses 9, 10, 11, and 12 refer to the"
"14126" 1998 "Greeks, the Persians, the Jews, and the Germans respectively. In the"
"14127" 1998 "penultimate verse he makes known his discovery concerning the root of"
"14128" 1998 "modern Nihilism and indifference,--i.e., that modern man has no goal, no"
"14129" 1998 "aim, no ideals (see Note A)."
"14130" 1998 ""
"14131" 1998 "Chapter XVIII. Old and Young Women."
"14132" 1998 ""
"14133" 1998 "Nietzsche's views on women have either to be loved at first sight"
"14134" 1998 "or they become perhaps the greatest obstacle in the way of those who"
"14135" 1998 "otherwise would be inclined to accept his philosophy. Women especially,"
"14136" 1998 "of course, have been taught to dislike them, because it has been"
"14137" 1998 "rumoured that his views are unfriendly to themselves. Now, to my mind,"
"14138" 1998 "all this is pure misunderstanding and error."
"14139" 1998 ""
"14140" 1998 "German philosophers, thanks to Schopenhauer, have earned rather a bad"
"14141" 1998 "name for their views on women. It is almost impossible for one of them"
"14142" 1998 "to write a line on the subject, however kindly he may do so, without"
"14143" 1998 "being suspected of wishing to open a crusade against the fair sex."
"14144" 1998 "Despite the fact, therefore, that all Nietzsche's views in this respect"
"14145" 1998 "were dictated to him by the profoundest love; despite Zarathustra's"
"14146" 1998 "reservation in this discourse, that \"with women nothing (that can be"
"14147" 1998 "said) is impossible,\" and in the face of other overwhelming evidence"
"14148" 1998 "to the contrary, Nietzsche is universally reported to have mis son"
"14149" 1998 "pied dans le plat, where the female sex is concerned. And what is the"
"14150" 1998 "fundamental doctrine which has given rise to so much bitterness and"
"14151" 1998 "aversion?--Merely this: that the sexes are at bottom ANTAGONISTIC--that"
"14152" 1998 "is to say, as different as blue is from yellow, and that the best"
"14153" 1998 "possible means of rearing anything approaching a desirable race is to"
"14154" 1998 "preserve and to foster this profound hostility. What Nietzsche strives"
"14155" 1998 "to combat and to overthrow is the modern democratic tendency which is"
"14156" 1998 "slowly labouring to level all things--even the sexes. His quarrel is not"
"14157" 1998 "with women--what indeed could be more undignified?--it is with those who"
"14158" 1998 "would destroy the natural relationship between the sexes, by modifying"
"14159" 1998 "either the one or the other with a view to making them more alike. The"
"14160" 1998 "human world is just as dependent upon women's powers as upon men's. It"
"14161" 1998 "is women's strongest and most valuable instincts which help to determine"
"14162" 1998 "who are to be the fathers of the next generation. By destroying these"
"14163" 1998 "particular instincts, that is to say by attempting to masculinise woman,"
"14164" 1998 "and to feminise men, we jeopardise the future of our people. The general"
"14165" 1998 "democratic movement of modern times, in its frantic struggle to mitigate"
"14166" 1998 "all differences, is now invading even the world of sex. It is against"
"14167" 1998 "this movement that Nietzsche raises his voice; he would have woman"
"14168" 1998 "become ever more woman and man become ever more man. Only thus, and"
"14169" 1998 "he is undoubtedly right, can their combined instincts lead to the"
"14170" 1998 "excellence of humanity. Regarded in this light, all his views on woman"
"14171" 1998 "appear not only necessary but just (see Note on Chapter LVI., par. 21.)"
"14172" 1998 ""
"14173" 1998 "It is interesting to observe that the last line of the discourse, which"
"14174" 1998 "has so frequently been used by women as a weapon against Nietzsche's"
"14175" 1998 "views concerning them, was suggested to Nietzsche by a woman (see \"Das"
"14176" 1998 "Leben F. Nietzsche's\")."
"14177" 1998 ""
"14178" 1998 "Chapter XXI. Voluntary Death."
"14179" 1998 ""
"14180" 1998 "In regard to this discourse, I should only like to point out that"
"14181" 1998 "Nietzsche had a particular aversion to the word \"suicide\"--self-murder."
"14182" 1998 "He disliked the evil it suggested, and in rechristening the act"
"14183" 1998 "Voluntary Death, i.e., the death that comes from no other hand than"
"14184" 1998 "one's own, he was desirous of elevating it to the position it held in"
"14185" 1998 "classical antiquity (see Aphorism 36 in \"The Twilight of the Idols\")."
"14186" 1998 ""
"14187" 1998 "Chapter XXII. The Bestowing Virtue."
"14188" 1998 ""
"14189" 1998 "An important aspect of Nietzsche's philosophy is brought to light in"
"14190" 1998 "this discourse. His teaching, as is well known, places the Aristotelian"
"14191" 1998 "man of spirit, above all others in the natural divisions of man. The"
"14192" 1998 "man with overflowing strength, both of mind and body, who must discharge"
"14193" 1998 "this strength or perish, is the Nietzschean ideal. To such a man, giving"
"14194" 1998 "from his overflow becomes a necessity; bestowing develops into a means"
"14195" 1998 "of existence, and this is the only giving, the only charity, that"
"14196" 1998 "Nietzsche recognises. In paragraph 3 of the discourse, we read"
"14197" 1998 "Zarathustra's healthy exhortation to his disciples to become independent"
"14198" 1998 "thinkers and to find themselves before they learn any more from him (see"
"14199" 1998 "Notes on Chapters LVI., par. 5, and LXXIII., pars. 10, 11)."
"14200" 1998 ""
"14201" 1998 "..."
"14202" 1998 ""
"14203" 1998 "PART II."
"14204" 1998 ""
"14205" 1998 "Chapter XXIII. The Child with the Mirror."
"14206" 1998 ""
"14207" 1998 "Nietzsche tells us here, in a poetical form, how deeply grieved he was"
"14208" 1998 "by the manifold misinterpretations and misunderstandings which were"
"14209" 1998 "becoming rife concerning his publications. He does not recognise"
"14210" 1998 "himself in the mirror of public opinion, and recoils terrified from the"
"14211" 1998 "distorted reflection of his features. In verse 20 he gives us a"
"14212" 1998 "hint which it were well not to pass over too lightly; for, in the"
"14213" 1998 "introduction to \"The Genealogy of Morals\" (written in 1887) he finds it"
"14214" 1998 "necessary to refer to the matter again and with greater precision. The"
"14215" 1998 "point is this, that a creator of new values meets with his surest and"
"14216" 1998 "strongest obstacles in the very spirit of the language which is at his"
"14217" 1998 "disposal. Words, like all other manifestations of an evolving race, are"
"14218" 1998 "stamped with the values that have long been paramount in that race."
"14219" 1998 "Now, the original thinker who finds himself compelled to use the current"
"14220" 1998 "speech of his country in order to impart new and hitherto untried views"
"14221" 1998 "to his fellows, imposes a task upon the natural means of communication"
"14222" 1998 "which it is totally unfitted to perform,--hence the obscurities and"
"14223" 1998 "prolixities which are so frequently met with in the writings of original"
"14224" 1998 "thinkers. In the \"Dawn of Day\", Nietzsche actually cautions young"
"14225" 1998 "writers against THE DANGER OF ALLOWING THEIR THOUGHTS TO BE MOULDED BY"
"14226" 1998 "THE WORDS AT THEIR DISPOSAL."
"14227" 1998 ""
"14228" 1998 "Chapter XXIV. In the Happy Isles."
"14229" 1998 ""
"14230" 1998 "While writing this, Nietzsche is supposed to have been thinking of the"
"14231" 1998 "island of Ischia which was ultimately destroyed by an earthquake. His"
"14232" 1998 "teaching here is quite clear. He was among the first thinkers of Europe"
"14233" 1998 "to overcome the pessimism which godlessness generally brings in its"
"14234" 1998 "wake. He points to creating as the surest salvation from the suffering"
"14235" 1998 "which is a concomitant of all higher life. \"What would there be to"
"14236" 1998 "create,\" he asks, \"if there were--Gods?\" His ideal, the Superman, lends"
"14237" 1998 "him the cheerfulness necessary to the overcoming of that despair usually"
"14238" 1998 "attendant upon godlessness and upon the apparent aimlessness of a world"
"14239" 1998 "without a god."
"14240" 1998 ""
"14241" 1998 "Chapter XXIX. The Tarantulas."
"14242" 1998 ""
"14243" 1998 "The tarantulas are the Socialists and Democrats. This discourse offers"
"14244" 1998 "us an analysis of their mental attitude. Nietzsche refuses to be"
"14245" 1998 "confounded with those resentful and revengeful ones who condemn society"
"14246" 1998 "FROM BELOW, and whose criticism is only suppressed envy. \"There are"
"14247" 1998 "those who preach my doctrine of life,\" he says of the Nietzschean"
"14248" 1998 "Socialists, \"and are at the same time preachers of equality and"
"14249" 1998 "tarantulas\" (see Notes on Chapter XL. and Chapter LI.)."
"14250" 1998 ""
"14251" 1998 "Chapter XXX. The Famous Wise Ones."
"14252" 1998 ""
"14253" 1998 "This refers to all those philosophers hitherto, who have run in the"
"14254" 1998 "harness of established values and have not risked their reputation with"
"14255" 1998 "the people in pursuit of truth. The philosopher, however, as Nietzsche"
"14256" 1998 "understood him, is a man who creates new values, and thus leads mankind"
"14257" 1998 "in a new direction."
"14258" 1998 ""
"14259" 1998 "Chapter XXXIII. The Grave-Song."
"14260" 1998 ""
"14261" 1998 "Here Zarathustra sings about the ideals and friendships of his youth."
"14262" 1998 "Verses 27 to 31 undoubtedly refer to Richard Wagner (see Note on Chapter"
"14263" 1998 "LXV.)."
"14264" 1998 ""
"14265" 1998 "Chapter XXXIV. Self-Surpassing."
"14266" 1998 ""
"14267" 1998 "In this discourse we get the best exposition in the whole book of"
"14268" 1998 "Nietzsche's doctrine of the Will to Power. I go into this question"
"14269" 1998 "thoroughly in the Note on Chapter LVII."
"14270" 1998 ""
"14271" 1998 "Nietzsche was not an iconoclast from choice. Those who hastily class him"
"14272" 1998 "with the anarchists (or the Progressivists of the last century) fail"
"14273" 1998 "to understand the high esteem in which he always held both law and"
"14274" 1998 "discipline. In verse 41 of this most decisive discourse he truly"
"14275" 1998 "explains his position when he says: \"...he who hath to be a creator in"
"14276" 1998 "good and evil--verily he hath first to be a destroyer, and break values"
"14277" 1998 "in pieces.\" This teaching in regard to self-control is evidence enough"
"14278" 1998 "of his reverence for law."
"14279" 1998 ""
"14280" 1998 "Chapter XXXV. The Sublime Ones."
"14281" 1998 ""
"14282" 1998 "These belong to a type which Nietzsche did not altogether dislike, but"
"14283" 1998 "which he would fain have rendered more subtle and plastic. It is the"
"14284" 1998 "type that takes life and itself too seriously, that never surmounts the"
"14285" 1998 "camel-stage mentioned in the first discourse, and that is obdurately"
"14286" 1998 "sublime and earnest. To be able to smile while speaking of lofty things"
"14287" 1998 "and NOT TO BE OPPRESSED by them, is the secret of real greatness. He"
"14288" 1998 "whose hand trembles when it lays hold of a beautiful thing, has the"
"14289" 1998 "quality of reverence, without the artist's unembarrassed friendship"
"14290" 1998 "with the beautiful. Hence the mistakes which have arisen in regard to"
"14291" 1998 "confounding Nietzsche with his extreme opposites the anarchists and"
"14292" 1998 "agitators. For what they dare to touch and break with the impudence"
"14293" 1998 "and irreverence of the unappreciative, he seems likewise to touch and"
"14294" 1998 "break,--but with other fingers--with the fingers of the loving and"
"14295" 1998 "unembarrassed artist who is on good terms with the beautiful and who"
"14296" 1998 "feels able to create it and to enhance it with his touch. The question"
"14297" 1998 "of taste plays an important part in Nietzsche's philosophy, and verses"
"14298" 1998 "9, 10 of this discourse exactly state Nietzsche's ultimate views on the"
"14299" 1998 "subject. In the \"Spirit of Gravity\", he actually cries:--\"Neither a good"
"14300" 1998 "nor a bad taste, but MY taste, of which I have no longer either shame or"
"14301" 1998 "secrecy.\""
"14302" 1998 ""
"14303" 1998 "Chapter XXXVI. The Land of Culture."
"14304" 1998 ""
"14305" 1998 "This is a poetical epitome of some of the scathing criticism of"
"14306" 1998 "scholars which appears in the first of the \"Thoughts out of Season\"--the"
"14307" 1998 "polemical pamphlet (written in 1873) against David Strauss and his"
"14308" 1998 "school. He reproaches his former colleagues with being sterile and"
"14309" 1998 "shows them that their sterility is the result of their not believing"
"14310" 1998 "in anything. \"He who had to create, had always his presaging dreams and"
"14311" 1998 "astral premonitions--and believed in believing!\" (See Note on Chapter"
"14312" 1998 "LXXVII.) In the last two verses he reveals the nature of his altruism."
"14313" 1998 "How far it differs from that of Christianity we have already read in the"
"14314" 1998 "discourse \"Neighbour-Love\", but here he tells us definitely the nature"
"14315" 1998 "of his love to mankind; he explains why he was compelled to assail the"
"14316" 1998 "Christian values of pity and excessive love of the neighbour, not only"
"14317" 1998 "because they are slave-values and therefore tend to promote degeneration"
"14318" 1998 "(see Note B.), but because he could only love his children's land, the"
"14319" 1998 "undiscovered land in a remote sea; because he would fain retrieve the"
"14320" 1998 "errors of his fathers in his children."
"14321" 1998 ""
"14322" 1998 "Chapter XXXVII. Immaculate Perception."
"14323" 1998 ""
"14324" 1998 "An important feature of Nietzsche's interpretation of Life is disclosed"
"14325" 1998 "in this discourse. As Buckle suggests in his \"Influence of Women on the"
"14326" 1998 "Progress of Knowledge\", the scientific spirit of the investigator is"
"14327" 1998 "both helped and supplemented by the latter's emotions and personality,"
"14328" 1998 "and the divorce of all emotionalism and individual temperament from"
"14329" 1998 "science is a fatal step towards sterility. Zarathustra abjures all those"
"14330" 1998 "who would fain turn an IMPERSONAL eye upon nature and contemplate her"
"14331" 1998 "phenomena with that pure objectivity to which the scientific idealists"
"14332" 1998 "of to-day would so much like to attain. He accuses such idealists of"
"14333" 1998 "hypocrisy and guile; he says they lack innocence in their desires and"
"14334" 1998 "therefore slander all desiring."
"14335" 1998 ""
"14336" 1998 "Chapter XXXVIII. Scholars."
"14337" 1998 ""
"14338" 1998 "This is a record of Nietzsche's final breach with his former"
"14339" 1998 "colleagues--the scholars of Germany. Already after the publication of"
"14340" 1998 "the \"Birth of Tragedy\", numbers of German philologists and professional"
"14341" 1998 "philosophers had denounced him as one who had strayed too far from"
"14342" 1998 "their flock, and his lectures at the University of Bale were deserted"
"14343" 1998 "in consequence; but it was not until 1879, when he finally severed all"
"14344" 1998 "connection with University work, that he may be said to have attained to"
"14345" 1998 "the freedom and independence which stamp this discourse."
"14346" 1998 ""
"14347" 1998 "Chapter XXXIX. Poets."
"14348" 1998 ""
"14349" 1998 "People have sometimes said that Nietzsche had no sense of humour. I"
"14350" 1998 "have no intention of defending him here against such foolish critics; I"
"14351" 1998 "should only like to point out to the reader that we have him here at"
"14352" 1998 "his best, poking fun at himself, and at his fellow-poets (see Note on"
"14353" 1998 "Chapter LXIII., pars. 16, 17, 18, 19, 20)."
"14354" 1998 ""
"14355" 1998 "Chapter XL. Great Events."
"14356" 1998 ""
"14357" 1998 "Here we seem to have a puzzle. Zarathustra himself, while relating"
"14358" 1998 "his experience with the fire-dog to his disciples, fails to get them"
"14359" 1998 "interested in his narrative, and we also may be only too ready to turn"
"14360" 1998 "over these pages under the impression that they are little more than"
"14361" 1998 "a mere phantasy or poetical flight. Zarathustra's interview with the"
"14362" 1998 "fire-dog is, however, of great importance. In it we find Nietzsche"
"14363" 1998 "face to face with the creature he most sincerely loathes--the spirit"
"14364" 1998 "of revolution, and we obtain fresh hints concerning his hatred of the"
"14365" 1998 "anarchist and rebel. \"'Freedom' ye all roar most eagerly,\" he says to"
"14366" 1998 "the fire-dog, \"but I have unlearned the belief in 'Great Events' when"
"14367" 1998 "there is much roaring and smoke about them. Not around the inventors"
"14368" 1998 "of new noise, but around the inventors of new values, doth the world"
"14369" 1998 "revolve; INAUDIBLY it revolveth.\""
"14370" 1998 ""
"14371" 1998 "Chapter XLI. The Soothsayer."
"14372" 1998 ""
"14373" 1998 "This refers, of course, to Schopenhauer. Nietzsche, as is well known,"
"14374" 1998 "was at one time an ardent follower of Schopenhauer. He overcame"
"14375" 1998 "Pessimism by discovering an object in existence; he saw the possibility"
"14376" 1998 "of raising society to a higher level and preached the profoundest"
"14377" 1998 "Optimism in consequence."
"14378" 1998 ""
"14379" 1998 "Chapter XLII. Redemption."
"14380" 1998 ""
