"14381" 1998 "Zarathustra here addresses cripples. He tells them of other"
"14382" 1998 "cripples--the GREAT MEN in this world who have one organ or faculty"
"14383" 1998 "inordinately developed at the cost of their other faculties. This is"
"14384" 1998 "doubtless a reference to a fact which is too often noticeable in the"
"14385" 1998 "case of so many of the world's giants in art, science, or religion. In"
"14386" 1998 "verse 19 we are told what Nietzsche called Redemption--that is to say,"
"14387" 1998 "the ability to say of all that is past: \"Thus would I have it.\" The"
"14388" 1998 "in ability to say this, and the resentment which results therefrom,"
"14389" 1998 "he regards as the source of all our feelings of revenge, and all our"
"14390" 1998 "desires to punish--punishment meaning to him merely a euphemism for the"
"14391" 1998 "word revenge, invented in order to still our consciences. He who can be"
"14392" 1998 "proud of his enemies, who can be grateful to them for the obstacles they"
"14393" 1998 "have put in his way; he who can regard his worst calamity as but the"
"14394" 1998 "extra strain on the bow of his life, which is to send the arrow of"
"14395" 1998 "his longing even further than he could have hoped;--this man knows no"
"14396" 1998 "revenge, neither does he know despair, he truly has found redemption and"
"14397" 1998 "can turn on the worst in his life and even in himself, and call it his"
"14398" 1998 "best (see Notes on Chapter LVII.)."
"14399" 1998 ""
"14400" 1998 "Chapter XLIII. Manly Prudence."
"14401" 1998 ""
"14402" 1998 "This discourse is very important. In \"Beyond Good and Evil\" we hear"
"14403" 1998 "often enough that the select and superior man must wear a mask, and"
"14404" 1998 "here we find this injunction explained. \"And he who would not languish"
"14405" 1998 "amongst men, must learn to drink out of all glasses: and he who would"
"14406" 1998 "keep clean amongst men, must know how to wash himself even with dirty"
"14407" 1998 "water.\" This, I venture to suggest, requires some explanation. At a time"
"14408" 1998 "when individuality is supposed to be shown most tellingly by putting"
"14409" 1998 "boots on one's hands and gloves on one's feet, it is somewhat refreshing"
"14410" 1998 "to come across a true individualist who feels the chasm between himself"
"14411" 1998 "and others so deeply, that he must perforce adapt himself to them"
"14412" 1998 "outwardly, at least, in all respects, so that the inner difference"
"14413" 1998 "should be overlooked. Nietzsche practically tells us here that it is not"
"14414" 1998 "he who intentionally wears eccentric clothes or does eccentric things"
"14415" 1998 "who is truly the individualist. The profound man, who is by nature"
"14416" 1998 "differentiated from his fellows, feels this difference too keenly to"
"14417" 1998 "call attention to it by any outward show. He is shamefast and bashful"
"14418" 1998 "with those who surround him and wishes not to be discovered by them,"
"14419" 1998 "just as one instinctively avoids all lavish display of comfort or wealth"
"14420" 1998 "in the presence of a poor friend."
"14421" 1998 ""
"14422" 1998 "Chapter XLIV. The Stillest Hour."
"14423" 1998 ""
"14424" 1998 "This seems to me to give an account of the great struggle which must"
"14425" 1998 "have taken place in Nietzsche's soul before he finally resolved to make"
"14426" 1998 "known the more esoteric portions of his teaching. Our deepest feelings"
"14427" 1998 "crave silence. There is a certain self-respect in the serious man which"
"14428" 1998 "makes him hold his profoundest feelings sacred. Before they are uttered"
"14429" 1998 "they are full of the modesty of a virgin, and often the oldest sage will"
"14430" 1998 "blush like a girl when this virginity is violated by an indiscretion"
"14431" 1998 "which forces him to reveal his deepest thoughts."
"14432" 1998 ""
"14433" 1998 "..."
"14434" 1998 ""
"14435" 1998 "PART III."
"14436" 1998 ""
"14437" 1998 "This is perhaps the most important of all the four parts. If it"
"14438" 1998 "contained only \"The Vision and the Enigma\" and \"The Old and New Tables\""
"14439" 1998 "I should still be of this opinion; for in the former of these discourses"
"14440" 1998 "we meet with what Nietzsche regarded as the crowning doctrine of his"
"14441" 1998 "philosophy and in \"The Old and New Tables\" we have a valuable epitome of"
"14442" 1998 "practically all his leading principles."
"14443" 1998 ""
"14444" 1998 "Chapter XLVI. The Vision and the Enigma."
"14445" 1998 ""
"14446" 1998 "\"The Vision and the Enigma\" is perhaps an example of Nietzsche in his"
"14447" 1998 "most obscure vein. We must know how persistently he inveighed against"
"14448" 1998 "the oppressing and depressing influence of man's sense of guilt and"
"14449" 1998 "consciousness of sin in order fully to grasp the significance of this"
"14450" 1998 "discourse. Slowly but surely, he thought the values of Christianity and"
"14451" 1998 "Judaic traditions had done their work in the minds of men. What were"
"14452" 1998 "once but expedients devised for the discipline of a certain portion of"
"14453" 1998 "humanity, had now passed into man's blood and had become instincts. This"
"14454" 1998 "oppressive and paralysing sense of guilt and of sin is what Nietzsche"
"14455" 1998 "refers to when he speaks of \"the spirit of gravity.\" This creature"
"14456" 1998 "half-dwarf, half-mole, whom he bears with him a certain distance on his"
"14457" 1998 "climb and finally defies, and whom he calls his devil and arch-enemy, is"
"14458" 1998 "nothing more than the heavy millstone \"guilty conscience,\" together with"
"14459" 1998 "the concept of sin which at present hangs round the neck of men. To rise"
"14460" 1998 "above it--to soar--is the most difficult of all things to-day. Nietzsche"
"14461" 1998 "is able to think cheerfully and optimistically of the possibility of"
"14462" 1998 "life in this world recurring again and again, when he has once cast the"
"14463" 1998 "dwarf from his shoulders, and he announces his doctrine of the Eternal"
"14464" 1998 "Recurrence of all things great and small to his arch-enemy and in"
"14465" 1998 "defiance of him."
"14466" 1998 ""
"14467" 1998 "That there is much to be said for Nietzsche's hypothesis of the Eternal"
"14468" 1998 "Recurrence of all things great and small, nobody who has read the"
"14469" 1998 "literature on the subject will doubt for an instant; but it remains a"
"14470" 1998 "very daring conjecture notwithstanding and even in its ultimate effect,"
"14471" 1998 "as a dogma, on the minds of men, I venture to doubt whether Nietzsche"
"14472" 1998 "ever properly estimated its worth (see Note on Chapter LVII.)."
"14473" 1998 ""
"14474" 1998 "What follows is clear enough. Zarathustra sees a young shepherd"
"14475" 1998 "struggling on the ground with a snake holding fast to the back of his"
"14476" 1998 "throat. The sage, assuming that the snake must have crawled into the"
"14477" 1998 "young man's mouth while he lay sleeping, runs to his help and pulls"
"14478" 1998 "at the loathsome reptile with all his might, but in vain. At last, in"
"14479" 1998 "despair, Zarathustra appeals to the young man's will. Knowing full well"
"14480" 1998 "what a ghastly operation he is recommending, he nevertheless cries,"
"14481" 1998 "\"Bite! Bite! Its head off! Bite!\" as the only possible solution of the"
"14482" 1998 "difficulty. The young shepherd bites, and far away he spits the"
"14483" 1998 "snake's head, whereupon he rises, \"No longer shepherd, no longer man--a"
"14484" 1998 "transfigured being, a light-surrounded being, that LAUGHED! Never on"
"14485" 1998 "earth laughed a man as he laughed!\""
"14486" 1998 ""
"14487" 1998 "In this parable the young shepherd is obviously the man of to-day; the"
"14488" 1998 "snake that chokes him represents the stultifying and paralysing social"
"14489" 1998 "values that threaten to shatter humanity, and the advice \"Bite! Bite!\""
"14490" 1998 "is but Nietzsche's exasperated cry to mankind to alter their values"
"14491" 1998 "before it is too late."
"14492" 1998 ""
"14493" 1998 "Chapter XLVII. Involuntary Bliss."
"14494" 1998 ""
"14495" 1998 "This, like \"The Wanderer\", is one of the many introspective passages"
"14496" 1998 "in the work, and is full of innuendos and hints as to the Nietzschean"
"14497" 1998 "outlook on life."
"14498" 1998 ""
"14499" 1998 "Chapter XLVIII. Before Sunrise."
"14500" 1998 ""
"14501" 1998 "Here we have a record of Zarathustra's avowal of optimism, as also the"
"14502" 1998 "important statement concerning \"Chance\" or \"Accident\" (verse 27). Those"
"14503" 1998 "who are familiar with Nietzsche's philosophy will not require to be told"
"14504" 1998 "what an important role his doctrine of chance plays in his teaching."
"14505" 1998 "The Giant Chance has hitherto played with the puppet \"man,\"--this is"
"14506" 1998 "the fact he cannot contemplate with equanimity. Man shall now exploit"
"14507" 1998 "chance, he says again and again, and make it fall on its knees before"
"14508" 1998 "him! (See verse 33 in \"On the Olive Mount\", and verses 9-10 in \"The"
"14509" 1998 "Bedwarfing Virtue\")."
"14510" 1998 ""
"14511" 1998 "Chapter XLIX. The Bedwarfing Virtue."
"14512" 1998 ""
"14513" 1998 "This requires scarcely any comment. It is a satire on modern man and"
"14514" 1998 "his belittling virtues. In verses 23 and 24 of the second part of the"
"14515" 1998 "discourse we are reminded of Nietzsche's powerful indictment of the"
"14516" 1998 "great of to-day, in the Antichrist (Aphorism 43):--\"At present"
"14517" 1998 "nobody has any longer the courage for separate rights, for rights of"
"14518" 1998 "domination, for a feeling of reverence for himself and his equals,--FOR"
"14519" 1998 "PATHOS OF DISTANCE...Our politics are MORBID from this want of"
"14520" 1998 "courage!--The aristocracy of character has been undermined most craftily"
"14521" 1998 "by the lie of the equality of souls; and if the belief in the 'privilege"
"14522" 1998 "of the many,' makes revolutions and WILL CONTINUE TO MAKE them, it is"
"14523" 1998 "Christianity, let us not doubt it, it is CHRISTIAN valuations, which"
"14524" 1998 "translate every revolution merely into blood and crime!\" (see also"
"14525" 1998 "\"Beyond Good and Evil\", pages 120, 121). Nietzsche thought it was a"
"14526" 1998 "bad sign of the times that even rulers have lost the courage of"
"14527" 1998 "their positions, and that a man of Frederick the Great's power and"
"14528" 1998 "distinguished gifts should have been able to say: \"Ich bin der erste"
"14529" 1998 "Diener des Staates\" (I am the first servant of the State.) To this"
"14530" 1998 "utterance of the great sovereign, verse 24 undoubtedly refers."
"14531" 1998 "\"Cowardice\" and \"Mediocrity,\" are the names with which he labels modern"
"14532" 1998 "notions of virtue and moderation."
"14533" 1998 ""
"14534" 1998 "In Part III., we get the sentiments of the discourse \"In the Happy"
"14535" 1998 "Isles\", but perhaps in stronger terms. Once again we find Nietzsche"
"14536" 1998 "thoroughly at ease, if not cheerful, as an atheist, and speaking with"
"14537" 1998 "vertiginous daring of making chance go on its knees to him. In verse"
"14538" 1998 "20, Zarathustra makes yet another attempt at defining his entirely"
"14539" 1998 "anti-anarchical attitude, and unless such passages have been completely"
"14540" 1998 "overlooked or deliberately ignored hitherto by those who will persist in"
"14541" 1998 "laying anarchy at his door, it is impossible to understand how he ever"
"14542" 1998 "became associated with that foul political party."
"14543" 1998 ""
"14544" 1998 "The last verse introduces the expression, \"THE GREAT NOONTIDE!\" In the"
"14545" 1998 "poem to be found at the end of \"Beyond Good and Evil\", we meet with"
"14546" 1998 "the expression again, and we shall find it occurring time and again in"
"14547" 1998 "Nietzsche's works. It will be found fully elucidated in the fifth part"
"14548" 1998 "of \"The Twilight of the Idols\"; but for those who cannot refer to"
"14549" 1998 "this book, it were well to point out that Nietzsche called the present"
"14550" 1998 "period--our period--the noon of man's history. Dawn is behind us. The"
"14551" 1998 "childhood of mankind is over. Now we KNOW; there is now no longer any"
"14552" 1998 "excuse for mistakes which will tend to botch and disfigure the type man."
"14553" 1998 "\"With respect to what is past,\" he says, \"I have, like all discerning"
"14554" 1998 "ones, great toleration, that is to say, GENEROUS self-control...But my"
"14555" 1998 "feeling changes suddenly, and breaks out as soon as I enter the modern"
"14556" 1998 "period, OUR period. Our age KNOWS...\" (See Note on Chapter LXX.)."
"14557" 1998 ""
"14558" 1998 "Chapter LI. On Passing-by."
"14559" 1998 ""
"14560" 1998 "Here we find Nietzsche confronted with his extreme opposite, with"
"14561" 1998 "him therefore for whom he is most frequently mistaken by the unwary."
"14562" 1998 "\"Zarathustra's ape\" he is called in the discourse. He is one of those"
"14563" 1998 "at whose hands Nietzsche had to suffer most during his life-time, and"
"14564" 1998 "at whose hands his philosophy has suffered most since his death. In this"
"14565" 1998 "respect it may seem a little trivial to speak of extremes meeting; but"
"14566" 1998 "it is wonderfully apt. Many have adopted Nietzsche's mannerisms and"
"14567" 1998 "word-coinages, who had nothing in common with him beyond the ideas and"
"14568" 1998 "\"business\" they plagiarised; but the superficial observer and a large"
"14569" 1998 "portion of the public, not knowing of these things,--not knowing perhaps"
"14570" 1998 "that there are iconoclasts who destroy out of love and are therefore"
"14571" 1998 "creators, and that there are others who destroy out of resentment and"
"14572" 1998 "revengefulness and who are therefore revolutionists and anarchists,--are"
"14573" 1998 "prone to confound the two, to the detriment of the nobler type."
"14574" 1998 ""
"14575" 1998 "If we now read what the fool says to Zarathustra, and note the tricks of"
"14576" 1998 "speech he has borrowed from him: if we carefully follow the attitude"
"14577" 1998 "he assumes, we shall understand why Zarathustra finally interrupts him."
"14578" 1998 "\"Stop this at once,\" Zarathustra cries, \"long have thy speech and"
"14579" 1998 "thy species disgusted me...Out of love alone shall my contempt and my"
"14580" 1998 "warning bird take wing; BUT NOT OUT OF THE SWAMP!\" It were well if"
"14581" 1998 "this discourse were taken to heart by all those who are too ready to"
"14582" 1998 "associate Nietzsche with lesser and noiser men,--with mountebanks and"
"14583" 1998 "mummers."
"14584" 1998 ""
"14585" 1998 "Chapter LII. The Apostates."
"14586" 1998 ""
"14587" 1998 "It is clear that this applies to all those breathless and hasty \"tasters"
"14588" 1998 "of everything,\" who plunge too rashly into the sea of independent"
"14589" 1998 "thought and \"heresy,\" and who, having miscalculated their strength, find"
"14590" 1998 "it impossible to keep their head above water. \"A little older, a little"
"14591" 1998 "colder,\" says Nietzsche. They soon clamber back to the conventions of"
"14592" 1998 "the age they intended reforming. The French then say \"le diable se fait"
"14593" 1998 "hermite,\" but these men, as a rule, have never been devils, neither"
"14594" 1998 "do they become angels; for, in order to be really good or evil, some"
"14595" 1998 "strength and deep breathing is required. Those who are more interested"
"14596" 1998 "in supporting orthodoxy than in being over nice concerning the kind of"
"14597" 1998 "support they give it, often refer to these people as evidence in favour"
"14598" 1998 "of the true faith."
"14599" 1998 ""
"14600" 1998 "Chapter LIII. The Return Home."
"14601" 1998 ""
"14602" 1998 "This is an example of a class of writing which may be passed over too"
"14603" 1998 "lightly by those whom poetasters have made distrustful of poetry. From"
"14604" 1998 "first to last it is extremely valuable as an autobiographical note. The"
"14605" 1998 "inevitable superficiality of the rabble is contrasted with the peaceful"
"14606" 1998 "and profound depths of the anchorite. Here we first get a direct hint"
"14607" 1998 "concerning Nietzsche's fundamental passion--the main force behind all"
"14608" 1998 "his new values and scathing criticism of existing values. In verse 30"
"14609" 1998 "we are told that pity was his greatest danger. The broad altruism of the"
"14610" 1998 "law-giver, thinking over vast eras of time, was continually being pitted"
"14611" 1998 "by Nietzsche, in himself, against that transient and meaner sympathy for"
"14612" 1998 "the neighbour which he more perhaps than any of his contemporaries had"
"14613" 1998 "suffered from, but which he was certain involved enormous dangers not"
"14614" 1998 "only for himself but also to the next and subsequent generations (see"
"14615" 1998 "Note B., where \"pity\" is mentioned among the degenerate virtues). Later"
"14616" 1998 "in the book we shall see how his profound compassion leads him into"
"14617" 1998 "temptation, and how frantically he struggles against it. In verses 31"
"14618" 1998 "and 32, he tells us to what extent he had to modify himself in order"
"14619" 1998 "to be endured by his fellows whom he loved (see also verse 12 in \"Manly"
"14620" 1998 "Prudence\"). Nietzsche's great love for his fellows, which he confesses"
"14621" 1998 "in the Prologue, and which is at the root of all his teaching, seems"
"14622" 1998 "rather to elude the discerning powers of the average philanthropist and"
"14623" 1998 "modern man. He cannot see the wood for the trees. A philanthropy that"
"14624" 1998 "sacrifices the minority of the present-day for the majority constituting"
"14625" 1998 "posterity, completely evades his mental grasp, and Nietzsche's"
"14626" 1998 "philosophy, because it declares Christian values to be a danger to the"
"14627" 1998 "future of our kind, is therefore shelved as brutal, cold, and hard (see"
"14628" 1998 "Note on Chapter XXXVI.). Nietzsche tried to be all things to all men;"
"14629" 1998 "he was sufficiently fond of his fellows for that: in the Return Home he"
"14630" 1998 "describes how he ultimately returns to loneliness in order to recover"
"14631" 1998 "from the effects of his experiment."
"14632" 1998 ""
"14633" 1998 "Chapter LIV. The Three Evil Things."
"14634" 1998 ""
"14635" 1998 "Nietzsche is here completely in his element. Three things hitherto"
"14636" 1998 "best-cursed and most calumniated on earth, are brought forward to be"
"14637" 1998 "weighed. Voluptuousness, thirst of power, and selfishness,--the three"
"14638" 1998 "forces in humanity which Christianity has done most to garble and"
"14639" 1998 "besmirch,--Nietzsche endeavours to reinstate in their former places of"
"14640" 1998 "honour. Voluptuousness, or sensual pleasure, is a dangerous thing to"
"14641" 1998 "discuss nowadays. If we mention it with favour we may be regarded,"
"14642" 1998 "however unjustly, as the advocate of savages, satyrs, and pure"
"14643" 1998 "sensuality. If we condemn it, we either go over to the Puritans or we"
"14644" 1998 "join those who are wont to come to table with no edge to their appetites"
"14645" 1998 "and who therefore grumble at all good fare. There can be no doubt that"
"14646" 1998 "the value of healthy innocent voluptuousness, like the value of health"
"14647" 1998 "itself, must have been greatly discounted by all those who, resenting"
"14648" 1998 "their inability to partake of this world's goods, cried like St Paul:"
"14649" 1998 "\"I would that all men were even as I myself.\" Now Nietzsche's philosophy"
"14650" 1998 "might be called an attempt at giving back to healthy and normal men"
"14651" 1998 "innocence and a clean conscience in their desires--NOT to applaud the"
"14652" 1998 "vulgar sensualists who respond to every stimulus and whose passions are"
"14653" 1998 "out of hand; not to tell the mean, selfish individual, whose selfishness"
"14654" 1998 "is a pollution (see Aphorism 33, \"Twilight of the Idols\"), that he is"
"14655" 1998 "right, nor to assure the weak, the sick, and the crippled, that the"
"14656" 1998 "thirst of power, which they gratify by exploiting the happier and"
"14657" 1998 "healthier individuals, is justified;--but to save the clean healthy man"
"14658" 1998 "from the values of those around him, who look at everything through the"
"14659" 1998 "mud that is in their own bodies,--to give him, and him alone, a clean"
"14660" 1998 "conscience in his manhood and the desires of his manhood. \"Do I counsel"
"14661" 1998 "you to slay your instincts? I counsel to innocence in your instincts.\""
"14662" 1998 "In verse 7 of the second paragraph (as in verse I of paragraph 19 in"
"14663" 1998 "\"The Old and New Tables\") Nietzsche gives us a reason for his occasional"
"14664" 1998 "obscurity (see also verses 3 to 7 of \"Poets\"). As I have already pointed"
"14665" 1998 "out, his philosophy is quite esoteric. It can serve no purpose with the"
"14666" 1998 "ordinary, mediocre type of man. I, personally, can no longer have any"
"14667" 1998 "doubt that Nietzsche's only object, in that part of his philosophy where"
"14668" 1998 "he bids his friends stand \"Beyond Good and Evil\" with him, was to save"
"14669" 1998 "higher men, whose growth and scope might be limited by the too"
"14670" 1998 "strict observance of modern values from foundering on the rocks of a"
"14671" 1998 "\"Compromise\" between their own genius and traditional conventions. The"
"14672" 1998 "only possible way in which the great man can achieve greatness is"
"14673" 1998 "by means of exceptional freedom--the freedom which assists him in"
"14674" 1998 "experiencing HIMSELF. Verses 20 to 30 afford an excellent supplement to"
"14675" 1998 "Nietzsche's description of the attitude of the noble type towards the"
"14676" 1998 "slaves in Aphorism 260 of the work \"Beyond Good and Evil\" (see also Note"
"14677" 1998 "B.)"
"14678" 1998 ""
"14679" 1998 "Chapter LV. The Spirit of Gravity."
"14680" 1998 ""
"14681" 1998 "(See Note on Chapter XLVI.) In Part II. of this discourse we meet with"
"14682" 1998 "a doctrine not touched upon hitherto, save indirectly;--I refer to the"
"14683" 1998 "doctrine of self-love. We should try to understand this perfectly before"
"14684" 1998 "proceeding; for it is precisely views of this sort which, after having"
"14685" 1998 "been cut out of the original context, are repeated far and wide as"
"14686" 1998 "internal evidence proving the general unsoundness of Nietzsche's"
"14687" 1998 "philosophy. Already in the last of the \"Thoughts out of Season\""
"14688" 1998 "Nietzsche speaks as follows about modern men: \"...these modern creatures"
"14689" 1998 "wish rather to be hunted down, wounded and torn to shreds, than to"
"14690" 1998 "live alone with themselves in solitary calm. Alone with oneself!--this"
"14691" 1998 "thought terrifies the modern soul; it is his one anxiety, his one"
"14692" 1998 "ghastly fear\" (English Edition, page 141). In his feverish scurry to"
"14693" 1998 "find entertainment and diversion, whether in a novel, a newspaper, or a"
"14694" 1998 "play, the modern man condemns his own age utterly; for he shows that in"
"14695" 1998 "his heart of hearts he despises himself. One cannot change a condition"
"14696" 1998 "of this sort in a day; to become endurable to oneself an inner"
"14697" 1998 "transformation is necessary. Too long have we lost ourselves in our"
"14698" 1998 "friends and entertainments to be able to find ourselves so soon at"
"14699" 1998 "another's bidding. \"And verily, it is no commandment for to-day and"
"14700" 1998 "to-morrow to LEARN to love oneself. Rather is it of all arts the finest,"
"14701" 1998 "subtlest, last, and patientest.\""
"14702" 1998 ""
"14703" 1998 "In the last verse Nietzsche challenges us to show that our way is"
"14704" 1998 "the right way. In his teaching he does not coerce us, nor does he"
"14705" 1998 "overpersuade; he simply says: \"I am a law only for mine own, I am not a"
"14706" 1998 "law for all. This--is now MY way,--where is yours?\""
"14707" 1998 ""
"14708" 1998 "Chapter LVI. Old and New Tables. Par. 2."
"14709" 1998 ""
"14710" 1998 "Nietzsche himself declares this to be the most decisive portion of"
"14711" 1998 "the whole of \"Thus Spake Zarathustra\". It is a sort of epitome of his"
"14712" 1998 "leading doctrines. In verse 12 of the second paragraph, we learn how he"
"14713" 1998 "himself would fain have abandoned the poetical method of expression had"
"14714" 1998 "he not known only too well that the only chance a new doctrine has of"
"14715" 1998 "surviving, nowadays, depends upon its being given to the world in some"
"14716" 1998 "kind of art-form. Just as prophets, centuries ago, often had to have"
"14717" 1998 "recourse to the mask of madness in order to mitigate the hatred of those"
"14718" 1998 "who did not and could not see as they did; so, to-day, the struggle for"
"14719" 1998 "existence among opinions and values is so great, that an art-form"
"14720" 1998 "is practically the only garb in which a new philosophy can dare to"
"14721" 1998 "introduce itself to us."
"14722" 1998 ""
"14723" 1998 "Pars. 3 and 4."
"14724" 1998 ""
"14725" 1998 "Many of the paragraphs will be found to be merely reminiscent of former"
"14726" 1998 "discourses. For instance, par. 3 recalls \"Redemption\". The last verse"
"14727" 1998 "of par. 4 is important. Freedom which, as I have pointed out before,"
"14728" 1998 "Nietzsche considered a dangerous acquisition in inexperienced or"
"14729" 1998 "unworthy hands, here receives its death-blow as a general desideratum."
"14730" 1998 "In the first Part we read under \"The Way of the Creating One\", that"
"14731" 1998 "freedom as an end in itself does not concern Zarathustra at all. He says"
"14732" 1998 "there: \"Free from what? What doth that matter to Zarathustra? Clearly,"
"14733" 1998 "however, shall thine eye answer me: free FOR WHAT?\" And in \"The"
"14734" 1998 "Bedwarfing Virtue\": \"Ah that ye understood my word: 'Do ever what ye"
"14735" 1998 "will--but first be such as CAN WILL.'\""
"14736" 1998 ""
"14737" 1998 "Par. 5."
"14738" 1998 ""
"14739" 1998 "Here we have a description of the kind of altruism Nietzsche exacted"
"14740" 1998 "from higher men. It is really a comment upon \"The Bestowing Virtue\" (see"
"14741" 1998 "Note on Chapter XXII.)."
"14742" 1998 ""
"14743" 1998 "Par. 6."
"14744" 1998 ""
"14745" 1998 "This refers, of course, to the reception pioneers of Nietzsche's stamp"
"14746" 1998 "meet with at the hands of their contemporaries."
"14747" 1998 ""
"14748" 1998 "Par. 8."
"14749" 1998 ""
"14750" 1998 "Nietzsche teaches that nothing is stable,--not even values,--not"
"14751" 1998 "even the concepts good and evil. He likens life unto a stream. But"
"14752" 1998 "foot-bridges and railings span the stream, and they seem to stand"
"14753" 1998 "firm. Many will be reminded of good and evil when they look upon these"
"14754" 1998 "structures; for thus these same values stand over the stream of life,"
"14755" 1998 "and life flows on beneath them and leaves them standing. When, however,"
"14756" 1998 "winter comes and the stream gets frozen, many inquire: \"Should not"
"14757" 1998 "everything--STAND STILL? Fundamentally everything standeth still.\" But"
"14758" 1998 "soon the spring cometh and with it the thaw-wind. It breaks the ice, and"
"14759" 1998 "the ice breaks down the foot-bridges and railings, whereupon everything"
"14760" 1998 "is swept away. This state of affairs, according to Nietzsche, has now"
"14761" 1998 "been reached. \"Oh, my brethren, is not everything AT PRESENT IN FLUX?"
"14762" 1998 "Have not all railings and foot-bridges fallen into the water? Who would"
"14763" 1998 "still HOLD ON to 'good' and 'evil'?\""
"14764" 1998 ""
"14765" 1998 "Par. 9."
"14766" 1998 ""
"14767" 1998 "This is complementary to the first three verses of par. 2."
"14768" 1998 ""
"14769" 1998 "Par. 10."
"14770" 1998 ""
"14771" 1998 "So far, this is perhaps the most important paragraph. It is a protest"
"14772" 1998 "against reading a moral order of things in life. \"Life is something"
"14773" 1998 "essentially immoral!\" Nietzsche tells us in the introduction to the"
"14774" 1998 "\"Birth of Tragedy\". Even to call life \"activity,\" or to define it"
"14775" 1998 "further as \"the continuous adjustment of internal relations to external"
"14776" 1998 "relations,\" as Spencer has it, Nietzsche characterises as a \"democratic"
"14777" 1998 "idiosyncracy.\" He says to define it in this way, \"is to mistake the"
"14778" 1998 "true nature and function of life, which is Will to Power...Life is"
"14779" 1998 "ESSENTIALLY appropriation, injury, conquest of the strange and weak,"
"14780" 1998 "suppression, severity, obtrusion of its own forms, incorporation and"
"14781" 1998 "at least, putting it mildest, exploitation.\" Adaptation is merely a"
"14782" 1998 "secondary activity, a mere re-activity (see Note on Chapter LVII.)."
"14783" 1998 ""
"14784" 1998 "Pars. 11, 12."
"14785" 1998 ""
"14786" 1998 "These deal with Nietzsche's principle of the desirability of rearing a"
"14787" 1998 "select race. The biological and historical grounds for his insistence"
"14788" 1998 "upon this principle are, of course, manifold. Gobineau in his great"
"14789" 1998 "work, \"L'Inegalite des Races Humaines\", lays strong emphasis upon the"
"14790" 1998 "evils which arise from promiscuous and inter-social marriages. He alone"
"14791" 1998 "would suffice to carry Nietzsche's point against all those who are"
"14792" 1998 "opposed to the other conditions, to the conditions which would have"
"14793" 1998 "saved Rome, which have maintained the strength of the Jewish race, and"
"14794" 1998 "which are strictly maintained by every breeder of animals throughout the"
"14795" 1998 "world. Darwin in his remarks relative to the degeneration of CULTIVATED"
"14796" 1998 "types of animals through the action of promiscuous breeding, brings"
"14797" 1998 "Gobineau support from the realm of biology."
"14798" 1998 ""
"14799" 1998 "The last two verses of par. 12 were discussed in the Notes on Chapters"
"14800" 1998 "XXXVI. and LIII."
"14801" 1998 ""
"14802" 1998 "Par. 13."
"14803" 1998 ""
"14804" 1998 "This, like the first part of \"The Soothsayer\", is obviously a reference"
"14805" 1998 "to the Schopenhauerian Pessimism."
"14806" 1998 ""
"14807" 1998 "Pars. 14, 15, 16, 17."
"14808" 1998 ""
"14809" 1998 "These are supplementary to the discourse \"Backworld's-men\"."
"14810" 1998 ""
"14811" 1998 "Par. 18."
"14812" 1998 ""
"14813" 1998 "We must be careful to separate this paragraph, in sense, from the"
"14814" 1998 "previous four paragraphs. Nietzsche is still dealing with Pessimism"
"14815" 1998 "here; but it is the pessimism of the hero--the man most susceptible of"
"14816" 1998 "all to desperate views of life, owing to the obstacles that are arrayed"
"14817" 1998 "against him in a world where men of his kind are very rare and are"
"14818" 1998 "continually being sacrificed. It was to save this man that Nietzsche"
"14819" 1998 "wrote. Heroism foiled, thwarted, and wrecked, hoping and fighting until"
"14820" 1998 "the last, is at length overtaken by despair, and renounces all struggle"
"14821" 1998 "for sleep. This is not the natural or constitutional pessimism which"
"14822" 1998 "proceeds from an unhealthy body--the dyspeptic's lack of appetite; it"
"14823" 1998 "is rather the desperation of the netted lion that ultimately stops all"
"14824" 1998 "movement, because the more it moves the more involved it becomes."
"14825" 1998 ""
"14826" 1998 "Par. 20."
"14827" 1998 ""
"14828" 1998 "\"All that increases power is good, all that springs from weakness is"
"14829" 1998 "bad. The weak and ill-constituted shall perish: first principle of our"
"14830" 1998 "charity. And one shall also help them thereto.\" Nietzsche partly divined"
"14831" 1998 "the kind of reception moral values of this stamp would meet with at"
"14832" 1998 "the hands of the effeminate manhood of Europe. Here we see that he had"
"14833" 1998 "anticipated the most likely form their criticism would take (see also"
"14834" 1998 "the last two verses of par. 17)."
"14835" 1998 ""
"14836" 1998 "Par. 21."
"14837" 1998 ""
"14838" 1998 "The first ten verses, here, are reminiscent of \"War and Warriors\" and"
"14839" 1998 "of \"The Flies in the Market-place.\" Verses 11 and 12, however, are"
"14840" 1998 "particularly important. There is a strong argument in favour of the"
"14841" 1998 "sharp differentiation of castes and of races (and even of sexes; see"
"14842" 1998 "Note on Chapter XVIII.) running all through Nietzsche's writings."
"14843" 1998 "But sharp differentiation also implies antagonism in some form or"
"14844" 1998 "other--hence Nietzsche's fears for modern men. What modern men desire"
"14845" 1998 "above all, is peace and the cessation of pain. But neither great races"
"14846" 1998 "nor great castes have ever been built up in this way. \"Who still wanteth"
"14847" 1998 "to rule?\" Zarathustra asks in the \"Prologue\". \"Who still wanteth to"
"14848" 1998 "obey? Both are too burdensome.\" This is rapidly becoming everybody's"
"14849" 1998 "attitude to-day. The tame moral reading of the face of nature, together"
"14850" 1998 "with such democratic interpretations of life as those suggested by"
"14851" 1998 "Herbert Spencer, are signs of a physiological condition which is the"
"14852" 1998 "reverse of that bounding and irresponsible healthiness in which harder"
"14853" 1998 "and more tragic values rule."
"14854" 1998 ""
"14855" 1998 "Par. 24."
"14856" 1998 ""
"14857" 1998 "This should be read in conjunction with \"Child and Marriage\". In the"
"14858" 1998 "fifth verse we shall recognise our old friend \"Marriage on the ten-years"
"14859" 1998 "system,\" which George Meredith suggested some years ago. This, however,"
"14860" 1998 "must not be taken too literally. I do not think Nietzsche's profoundest"
"14861" 1998 "views on marriage were ever intended to be given over to the public at"
"14862" 1998 "all, at least not for the present. They appear in the biography by his"
"14863" 1998 "sister, and although their wisdom is unquestionable, the nature of the"
"14864" 1998 "reforms he suggests render it impossible for them to become popular just"
"14865" 1998 "now."
"14866" 1998 ""
"14867" 1998 "Pars. 26, 27."
"14868" 1998 ""
"14869" 1998 "See Note on \"The Prologue\"."
"14870" 1998 ""
"14871" 1998 "Par. 28."
"14872" 1998 ""
"14873" 1998 "Nietzsche was not an iconoclast from predilection. No bitterness or"
"14874" 1998 "empty hate dictated his vituperations against existing values and"
"14875" 1998 "against the dogmas of his parents and forefathers. He knew too well what"
"14876" 1998 "these things meant to the millions who profess them, to approach the"
"14877" 1998 "task of uprooting them with levity or even with haste. He saw what"
"14878" 1998 "modern anarchists and revolutionists do NOT see--namely, that man is in"
"14879" 1998 "danger of actual destruction when his customs and values are broken."
"14880" 1998 "I need hardly point out, therefore, how deeply he was conscious of"
"14881" 1998 "the responsibility he threw upon our shoulders when he invited us to"
"14882" 1998 "reconsider our position. The lines in this paragraph are evidence enough"
"14883" 1998 "of his earnestness."
"14884" 1998 ""
"14885" 1998 "Chapter LVII. The Convalescent."
"14886" 1998 ""
"14887" 1998 "We meet with several puzzles here. Zarathustra calls himself the"
"14888" 1998 "advocate of the circle (the Eternal Recurrence of all things), and he"
"14889" 1998 "calls this doctrine his abysmal thought. In the last verse of the"
"14890" 1998 "first paragraph, however, after hailing his deepest thought, he cries:"
"14891" 1998 "\"Disgust, disgust, disgust!\" We know Nietzsche's ideal man was that"
"14892" 1998 "\"world-approving, exuberant, and vivacious creature, who has not only"
"14893" 1998 "learnt to compromise and arrange with that which was and is, but wishes"
"14894" 1998 "to have it again, AS IT WAS AND IS, for all eternity insatiably calling"
"14895" 1998 "out da capo, not only to himself, but to the whole piece and play\" (see"
"14896" 1998 "Note on Chapter XLII.). But if one ask oneself what the conditions to"
"14897" 1998 "such an attitude are, one will realise immediately how utterly different"
"14898" 1998 "Nietzsche was from his ideal. The man who insatiably cries da capo to"
"14899" 1998 "himself and to the whole of his mise-en-scene, must be in a position to"
"14900" 1998 "desire every incident in his life to be repeated, not once, but"
"14901" 1998 "again and again eternally. Now, Nietzsche's life had been too full of"
"14902" 1998 "disappointments, illness, unsuccessful struggles, and snubs, to allow of"
"14903" 1998 "his thinking of the Eternal Recurrence without loathing--hence probably"
"14904" 1998 "the words of the last verse."
"14905" 1998 ""
"14906" 1998 "In verses 15 and 16, we have Nietzsche declaring himself an evolutionist"
"14907" 1998 "in the broadest sense--that is to say, that he believes in the"
"14908" 1998 "Development Hypothesis as the description of the process by which"
"14909" 1998 "species have originated. Now, to understand his position correctly"
"14910" 1998 "we must show his relationship to the two greatest of modern"
"14911" 1998 "evolutionists--Darwin and Spencer. As a philosopher, however, Nietzsche"
"14912" 1998 "does not stand or fall by his objections to the Darwinian or Spencerian"
"14913" 1998 "cosmogony. He never laid claim to a very profound knowledge of biology,"
"14914" 1998 "and his criticism is far more valuable as the attitude of a fresh mind"
"14915" 1998 "than as that of a specialist towards the question. Moreover, in his"
"14916" 1998 "objections many difficulties are raised which are not settled by an"
"14917" 1998 "appeal to either of the men above mentioned. We have given Nietzsche's"
"14918" 1998 "definition of life in the Note on Chapter LVI., par. 10. Still, there"
"14919" 1998 "remains a hope that Darwin and Nietzsche may some day become reconciled"
"14920" 1998 "by a new description of the processes by which varieties occur. The"
"14921" 1998 "appearance of varieties among animals and of \"sporting plants\" in"
"14922" 1998 "the vegetable kingdom, is still shrouded in mystery, and the question"
"14923" 1998 "whether this is not precisely the ground on which Darwin and Nietzsche"
"14924" 1998 "will meet, is an interesting one. The former says in his \"Origin of"
"14925" 1998 "Species\", concerning the causes of variability: \"...there are two"
"14926" 1998 "factors, namely, the nature of the organism, and the nature of the"
"14927" 1998 "conditions. THE FORMER SEEMS TO BE MUCH THE MORE IMPORTANT (The italics"
"14928" 1998 "are mine.), for nearly similar variations sometimes arise under, as"
"14929" 1998 "far as we can judge, dissimilar conditions; and on the other hand,"
"14930" 1998 "dissimilar variations arise under conditions which appear to be"
"14931" 1998 "nearly uniform.\" Nietzsche, recognising this same truth, would ascribe"
"14932" 1998 "practically all the importance to the \"highest functionaries in the"
"14933" 1998 "organism, in which the life-will appears as an active and formative"
"14934" 1998 "principle,\" and except in certain cases (where passive organisms alone"
"14935" 1998 "are concerned) would not give such a prominent place to the influence"
"14936" 1998 "of environment. Adaptation, according to him, is merely a secondary"
"14937" 1998 "activity, a mere re-activity, and he is therefore quite opposed to"
"14938" 1998 "Spencer's definition: \"Life is the continuous adjustment of internal"
"14939" 1998 "relations to external relations.\" Again in the motive force behind"
"14940" 1998 "animal and plant life, Nietzsche disagrees with Darwin. He"
"14941" 1998 "transforms the \"Struggle for Existence\"--the passive and involuntary"
"14942" 1998 "condition--into the \"Struggle for Power,\" which is active and creative,"
"14943" 1998 "and much more in harmony with Darwin's own view, given above, concerning"
"14944" 1998 "the importance of the organism itself. The change is one of such"
"14945" 1998 "far-reaching importance that we cannot dispose of it in a breath, as a"
"14946" 1998 "mere play upon words. \"Much is reckoned higher than life itself by the"
"14947" 1998 "living one.\" Nietzsche says that to speak of the activity of life as a"
"14948" 1998 "\"struggle for existence,\" is to state the case inadequately. He warns us"
"14949" 1998 "not to confound Malthus with nature. There is something more than"
"14950" 1998 "this struggle between the organic beings on this earth; want, which is"
"14951" 1998 "supposed to bring this struggle about, is not so common as is supposed;"
"14952" 1998 "some other force must be operative. The Will to Power is this force,"
"14953" 1998 "\"the instinct of self-preservation is only one of the indirect and most"
"14954" 1998 "frequent results thereof.\" A certain lack of acumen in psychological"
"14955" 1998 "questions and the condition of affairs in England at the time Darwin"
"14956" 1998 "wrote, may both, according to Nietzsche, have induced the renowned"
"14957" 1998 "naturalist to describe the forces of nature as he did in his \"Origin of"
"14958" 1998 "Species\"."
"14959" 1998 ""
"14960" 1998 "In verses 28, 29, and 30 of the second portion of this discourse we meet"
"14961" 1998 "with a doctrine which, at first sight, seems to be merely \"le manoir"
"14962" 1998 "a l'envers,\" indeed one English critic has actually said of Nietzsche,"
"14963" 1998 "that \"Thus Spake Zarathustra\" is no more than a compendium of modern"
"14964" 1998 "views and maxims turned upside down. Examining these heterodox"
"14965" 1998 "pronouncements a little more closely, however, we may possibly perceive"
"14966" 1998 "their truth. Regarding good and evil as purely relative values, it"
"14967" 1998 "stands to reason that what may be bad or evil in a given man, relative"
"14968" 1998 "to a certain environment, may actually be good if not highly virtuous"
"14969" 1998 "in him relative to a certain other environment. If this hypothetical man"
"14970" 1998 "represent the ascending line of life--that is to say, if he promise all"
"14971" 1998 "that which is highest in a Graeco-Roman sense, then it is likely that"
"14972" 1998 "he will be condemned as wicked if introduced into the society of men"
"14973" 1998 "representing the opposite and descending line of life."
"14974" 1998 ""
"14975" 1998 "By depriving a man of his wickedness--more particularly nowadays--"
"14976" 1998 "therefore, one may unwittingly be doing violence to the greatest in him."
"14977" 1998 "It may be an outrage against his wholeness, just as the lopping-off of a"
"14978" 1998 "leg would be. Fortunately, the natural so-called \"wickedness\" of higher"
"14979" 1998 "men has in a certain measure been able to resist this lopping process"
"14980" 1998 "which successive slave-moralities have practised; but signs are not"
"14981" 1998 "wanting which show that the noblest wickedness is fast vanishing from"
"14982" 1998 "society--the wickedness of courage and determination--and that Nietzsche"
"14983" 1998 "had good reasons for crying: \"Ah, that (man's) baddest is so very small!"
"14984" 1998 "Ah, that his best is so very small. What is good? To be brave is good!"
"14985" 1998 "It is the good war which halloweth every cause!\" (see also par. 5,"
"14986" 1998 "\"Higher Man\")."
"14987" 1998 ""
"14988" 1998 "Chapter LX. The Seven Seals."
"14989" 1998 ""
"14990" 1998 "This is a final paean which Zarathustra sings to Eternity and the"
"14991" 1998 "marriage-ring of rings, the ring of the Eternal Recurrence."
"14992" 1998 ""
"14993" 1998 "..."
"14994" 1998 ""
"14995" 1998 "PART IV."
"14996" 1998 ""
"14997" 1998 "In my opinion this part is Nietzsche's open avowal that all his"
"14998" 1998 "philosophy, together with all his hopes, enthusiastic outbursts,"
"14999" 1998 "blasphemies, prolixities, and obscurities, were merely so many gifts"
"15000" 1998 "laid at the feet of higher men. He had no desire to save the world. What"
"15001" 1998 "he wished to determine was: Who is to be master of the world? This is"
"15002" 1998 "a very different thing. He came to save higher men;--to give them that"
"15003" 1998 "freedom by which, alone, they can develop and reach their zenith (see"
"15004" 1998 "Note on Chapter LIV., end). It has been argued, and with considerable"
"15005" 1998 "force, that no such philosophy is required by higher men, that, as a"
"15006" 1998 "matter of fact, higher men, by virtue of their constitutions always, do"
"15007" 1998 "stand Beyond Good and Evil, and never allow anything to stand in the"
"15008" 1998 "way of their complete growth. Nietzsche, however, was evidently not so"
"15009" 1998 "confident about this. He would probably have argued that we only see the"
"15010" 1998 "successful cases. Being a great man himself, he was well aware of the"
"15011" 1998 "dangers threatening greatness in our age. In \"Beyond Good and Evil\" he"
"15012" 1998 "writes: \"There are few pains so grievous as to have seen, divined,"
"15013" 1998 "or experienced how an exceptional man has missed his way and"
"15014" 1998 "deteriorated...\" He knew \"from his painfullest recollections on what"
"15015" 1998 "wretched obstacles promising developments of the highest rank have"
"15016" 1998 "hitherto usually gone to pieces, broken down, sunk, and become"
"15017" 1998 "contemptible.\" Now in Part IV. we shall find that his strongest"
"15018" 1998 "temptation to descend to the feeling of \"pity\" for his contemporaries,"
"15019" 1998 "is the \"cry for help\" which he hears from the lips of the higher men"
"15020" 1998 "exposed to the dreadful danger of their modern environment."
"15021" 1998 ""
"15022" 1998 "Chapter LXI. The Honey Sacrifice."
"15023" 1998 ""
"15024" 1998 "In the fourteenth verse of this discourse Nietzsche defines the solemn"
"15025" 1998 "duty he imposed upon himself: \"Become what thou art.\" Surely the"
"15026" 1998 "criticism which has been directed against this maxim must all fall to"
"15027" 1998 "the ground when it is remembered, once and for all, that Nietzsche's"
"15028" 1998 "teaching was never intended to be other than an esoteric one. \"I am a"
"15029" 1998 "law only for mine own,\" he says emphatically, \"I am not a law for"
"15030" 1998 "all.\" It is of the greatest importance to humanity that its highest"
"15031" 1998 "individuals should be allowed to attain to their full development; for,"
"15032" 1998 "only by means of its heroes can the human race be led forward step by"
"15033" 1998 "step to higher and yet higher levels. \"Become what thou art\" applied"
"15034" 1998 "to all, of course, becomes a vicious maxim; it is to be hoped, however,"
"15035" 1998 "that we may learn in time that the same action performed by a given"
"15036" 1998 "number of men, loses its identity precisely that same number of"
"15037" 1998 "times.--\"Quod licet Jovi, non licet bovi.\""
"15038" 1998 ""
"15039" 1998 "At the last eight verses many readers may be tempted to laugh. In"
"15040" 1998 "England we almost always laugh when a man takes himself seriously at"
"15041" 1998 "anything save sport. And there is of course no reason why the reader"
"15042" 1998 "should not be hilarious.--A certain greatness is requisite, both in"
"15043" 1998 "order to be sublime and to have reverence for the sublime. Nietzsche"
"15044" 1998 "earnestly believed that the Zarathustra-kingdom--his dynasty of a"
"15045" 1998 "thousand years--would one day come; if he had not believed it so"
"15046" 1998 "earnestly, if every artist in fact had not believed so earnestly in"
"15047" 1998 "his Hazar, whether of ten, fifteen, a hundred, or a thousand years, we"
"15048" 1998 "should have lost all our higher men; they would have become pessimists,"
"15049" 1998 "suicides, or merchants. If the minor poet and philosopher has made us"
"15050" 1998 "shy of the prophetic seriousness which characterized an Isaiah or a"
"15051" 1998 "Jeremiah, it is surely our loss and the minor poet's gain."
"15052" 1998 ""
"15053" 1998 "Chapter LXII. The Cry of Distress."
"15054" 1998 ""
"15055" 1998 "We now meet with Zarathustra in extraordinary circumstances. He is"
"15056" 1998 "confronted with Schopenhauer and tempted by the old Soothsayer to commit"
"15057" 1998 "the sin of pity. \"I have come that I may seduce thee to thy last sin!\""
"15058" 1998 "says the Soothsayer to Zarathustra. It will be remembered that in"
"15059" 1998 "Schopenhauer's ethics, pity is elevated to the highest place among the"
"15060" 1998 "virtues, and very consistently too, seeing that the Weltanschauung is"
"15061" 1998 "a pessimistic one. Schopenhauer appeals to Nietzsche's deepest and"
"15062" 1998 "strongest sentiment--his sympathy for higher men. \"Why dost thou conceal"
"15063" 1998 "thyself?\" he cries. \"It is THE HIGHER MAN that calleth for thee!\""
"15064" 1998 "Zarathustra is almost overcome by the Soothsayer's pleading, as he"
"15065" 1998 "had been once already in the past, but he resists him step by step. At"
"15066" 1998 "length he can withstand him no longer, and, on the plea that the higher"
"15067" 1998 "man is on his ground and therefore under his protection, Zarathustra"
"15068" 1998 "departs in search of him, leaving Schopenhauer--a higher man in"
"15069" 1998 "Nietzsche's opinion--in the cave as a guest."
"15070" 1998 ""
"15071" 1998 "Chapter LXIII. Talk with the Kings."
"15072" 1998 ""
"15073" 1998 "On his way Zarathustra meets two more higher men of his time; two"
"15074" 1998 "kings cross his path. They are above the average modern type; for their"
"15075" 1998 "instincts tell them what real ruling is, and they despise the mockery"
"15076" 1998 "which they have been taught to call \"Reigning.\" \"We ARE NOT the first"
"15077" 1998 "men,\" they say, \"and have nevertheless to STAND FOR them: of this"
"15078" 1998 "imposture have we at last become weary and disgusted.\" It is the kings"
"15079" 1998 "who tell Zarathustra: \"There is no sorer misfortune in all human destiny"
"15080" 1998 "than when the mighty of the earth are not also the first men. There"
"15081" 1998 "everything becometh false and distorted and monstrous.\" The kings are"
"15082" 1998 "also asked by Zarathustra to accept the shelter of his cave, whereupon"
"15083" 1998 "he proceeds on his way."
"15084" 1998 ""
"15085" 1998 "Chapter LXIV. The Leech."
"15086" 1998 ""
"15087" 1998 "Among the higher men whom Zarathustra wishes to save, is also the"
"15088" 1998 "scientific specialist--the man who honestly and scrupulously pursues his"
"15089" 1998 "investigations, as Darwin did, in one department of knowledge. \"I love"
"15090" 1998 "him who liveth in order to know, and seeketh to know in order that the"
"15091" 1998 "Superman may hereafter live. Thus seeketh he his own down-going.\""
"15092" 1998 "\"The spiritually conscientious one,\" he is called in this discourse."
"15093" 1998 "Zarathustra steps on him unawares, and the slave of science, bleeding"
"15094" 1998 "from the violence he has done to himself by his self-imposed task,"
"15095" 1998 "speaks proudly of his little sphere of knowledge--his little hand's"
"15096" 1998 "breadth of ground on Zarathustra's territory, philosophy. \"Where mine"
"15097" 1998 "honesty ceaseth,\" says the true scientific specialist, \"there am I blind"
"15098" 1998 "and want also to be blind. Where I want to know, however, there want"
"15099" 1998 "I also to be honest--namely, severe, rigorous, restricted, cruel, and"
"15100" 1998 "inexorable.\" Zarathustra greatly respecting this man, invites him too to"
"15101" 1998 "the cave, and then vanishes in answer to another cry for help."
"15102" 1998 ""
"15103" 1998 "Chapter LXV. The Magician."
"15104" 1998 ""
"15105" 1998 "The Magician is of course an artist, and Nietzsche's intimate knowledge"
"15106" 1998 "of perhaps the greatest artist of his age rendered the selection of"
"15107" 1998 "Wagner, as the type in this discourse, almost inevitable. Most readers"
"15108" 1998 "will be acquainted with the facts relating to Nietzsche's and Wagner's"
"15109" 1998 "friendship and ultimate separation. As a boy and a youth Nietzsche had"
"15110" 1998 "shown such a remarkable gift for music that it had been a question at"
"15111" 1998 "one time whether he should not perhaps give up everything else in order"
"15112" 1998 "to develop this gift, but he became a scholar notwithstanding, although"
"15113" 1998 "he never entirely gave up composing, and playing the piano. While"
"15114" 1998 "still in his teens, he became acquainted with Wagner's music and"
"15115" 1998 "grew passionately fond of it. Long before he met Wagner he must have"
"15116" 1998 "idealised him in his mind to an extent which only a profoundly artistic"
"15117" 1998 "nature could have been capable of. Nietzsche always had high ideals for"
"15118" 1998 "humanity. If one were asked whether, throughout his many changes, there"
"15119" 1998 "was yet one aim, one direction, and one hope to which he held fast,"
"15120" 1998 "one would be forced to reply in the affirmative and declare that aim,"
"15121" 1998 "direction, and hope to have been \"the elevation of the type man.\""
"15122" 1998 "Now, when Nietzsche met Wagner he was actually casting about for an"
"15123" 1998 "incarnation of his dreams for the German people, and we have only to"
"15124" 1998 "remember his youth (he was twenty-one when he was introduced to Wagner),"
"15125" 1998 "his love of Wagner's music, and the undoubted power of the great"
"15126" 1998 "musician's personality, in order to realise how very uncritical his"
"15127" 1998 "attitude must have been in the first flood of his enthusiasm. Again,"
"15128" 1998 "when the friendship ripened, we cannot well imagine Nietzsche, the"
"15129" 1998 "younger man, being anything less than intoxicated by his senior's"
"15130" 1998 "attention and love, and we are therefore not surprised to find him"
"15131" 1998 "pressing Wagner forward as the great Reformer and Saviour of mankind."
"15132" 1998 "\"Wagner in Bayreuth\" (English Edition, 1909) gives us the best proof"
"15133" 1998 "of Nietzsche's infatuation, and although signs are not wanting in this"
"15134" 1998 "essay which show how clearly and even cruelly he was sub-consciously"
"15135" 1998 "\"taking stock\" of his friend--even then, the work is a record of what"
"15136" 1998 "great love and admiration can do in the way of endowing the object"
"15137" 1998 "of one's affection with all the qualities and ideals that a fertile"
"15138" 1998 "imagination can conceive."
"15139" 1998 ""
"15140" 1998 "When the blow came it was therefore all the more severe. Nietzsche"
"15141" 1998 "at length realised that the friend of his fancy and the real Richard"
"15142" 1998 "Wagner--the composer of Parsifal--were not one; the fact dawned"
"15143" 1998 "upon him slowly; disappointment upon disappointment, revelation after"
"15144" 1998 "revelation, ultimately brought it home to him, and though his best"
"15145" 1998 "instincts were naturally opposed to it at first, the revulsion of"
"15146" 1998 "feeling at last became too strong to be ignored, and Nietzsche was"
"15147" 1998 "plunged into the blackest despair. Years after his break with Wagner,"
"15148" 1998 "he wrote \"The Case of Wagner\", and \"Nietzsche contra Wagner\", and these"
"15149" 1998 "works are with us to prove the sincerity and depth of his views on the"
"15150" 1998 "man who was the greatest event of his life."
"15151" 1998 ""
"15152" 1998 "The poem in this discourse is, of course, reminiscent of Wagner's own"
"15153" 1998 "poetical manner, and it must be remembered that the whole was written"
"15154" 1998 "subsequent to Nietzsche's final break with his friend. The dialogue"
"15155" 1998 "between Zarathustra and the Magician reveals pretty fully what it"
"15156" 1998 "was that Nietzsche grew to loathe so intensely in Wagner,--viz., his"
"15157" 1998 "pronounced histrionic tendencies, his dissembling powers, his inordinate"
"15158" 1998 "vanity, his equivocalness, his falseness. \"It honoureth thee,\" says"
"15159" 1998 "Zarathustra, \"that thou soughtest for greatness, but it betrayeth thee"
"15160" 1998 "also. Thou art not great.\" The Magician is nevertheless sent as a guest"
"15161" 1998 "to Zarathustra's cave; for, in his heart, Zarathustra believed until the"
"15162" 1998 "end that the Magician was a higher man broken by modern values."
"15163" 1998 ""
"15164" 1998 "Chapter LXVI. Out of Service."
"15165" 1998 ""
"15166" 1998 "Zarathustra now meets the last pope, and, in a poetical form, we get"
"15167" 1998 "Nietzsche's description of the course Judaism and Christianity pursued"
"15168" 1998 "before they reached their final break-up in Atheism, Agnosticism, and"
"15169" 1998 "the like. The God of a strong, warlike race--the God of Israel--is a"
"15170" 1998 "jealous, revengeful God. He is a power that can be pictured and endured"
"15171" 1998 "only by a hardy and courageous race, a race rich enough to sacrifice and"
"15172" 1998 "to lose in sacrifice. The image of this God degenerates with the people"
"15173" 1998 "that appropriate it, and gradually He becomes a God of love--\"soft and"
"15174" 1998 "mellow,\" a lower middle-class deity, who is \"pitiful.\" He can no longer"
"15175" 1998 "be a God who requires sacrifice, for we ourselves are no longer rich"
"15176" 1998 "enough for that. The tables are therefore turned upon Him; HE must"
"15177" 1998 "sacrifice to us. His pity becomes so great that he actually does"
"15178" 1998 "sacrifice something to us--His only begotten Son. Such a process"
"15179" 1998 "carried to its logical conclusions must ultimately end in His own"
"15180" 1998 "destruction, and thus we find the pope declaring that God was one day"
"15181" 1998 "suffocated by His all-too-great pity. What follows is clear enough."
"15182" 1998 "Zarathustra recognises another higher man in the ex-pope and sends him"
"15183" 1998 "too as a guest to the cave."
"15184" 1998 ""
"15185" 1998 "Chapter LXVII. The Ugliest Man."
"15186" 1998 ""
"15187" 1998 "This discourse contains perhaps the boldest of Nietzsche's suggestions"
"15188" 1998 "concerning Atheism, as well as some extremely penetrating remarks upon"
"15189" 1998 "the sentiment of pity. Zarathustra comes across the repulsive creature"
"15190" 1998 "sitting on the wayside, and what does he do? He manifests the only"
"15191" 1998 "correct feelings that can be manifested in the presence of any great"
"15192" 1998 "misery--that is to say, shame, reverence, embarrassment. Nietzsche"
"15193" 1998 "detested the obtrusive and gushing pity that goes up to misery without"
"15194" 1998 "a blush either on its cheek or in its heart--the pity which is only"
"15195" 1998 "another form of self-glorification. \"Thank God that I am not like"
"15196" 1998 "thee!\"--only this self-glorifying sentiment can lend a well-constituted"
"15197" 1998 "man the impudence to SHOW his pity for the cripple and the"
"15198" 1998 "ill-constituted. In the presence of the ugliest man Nietzsche"
"15199" 1998 "blushes,--he blushes for his race; his own particular kind of"
"15200" 1998 "altruism--the altruism that might have prevented the existence of this"
"15201" 1998 "man--strikes him with all its force. He will have the world otherwise."
"15202" 1998 "He will have a world where one need not blush for one's fellows--hence"
"15203" 1998 "his appeal to us to love only our children's land, the land undiscovered"
"15204" 1998 "in the remotest sea."
"15205" 1998 ""
"15206" 1998 "Zarathustra calls the ugliest man the murderer of God! Certainly, this"
"15207" 1998 "is one aspect of a certain kind of Atheism--the Atheism of the man who"
"15208" 1998 "reveres beauty to such an extent that his own ugliness, which outrages"
"15209" 1998 "him, must be concealed from every eye lest it should not be respected as"
"15210" 1998 "Zarathustra respected it. If there be a God, He too must be evaded. His"
"15211" 1998 "pity must be foiled. But God is ubiquitous and omniscient. Therefore,"
"15212" 1998 "for the really GREAT ugly man, He must not exist. \"Their pity IS it from"
"15213" 1998 "which I flee away,\" he says--that is to say: \"It is from their want of"
"15214" 1998 "reverence and lack of shame in presence of my great misery!\" The ugliest"
"15215" 1998 "man despises himself; but Zarathustra said in his Prologue: \"I love"
"15216" 1998 "the great despisers because they are the great adorers, and arrows of"
"15217" 1998 "longing for the other shore.\" He therefore honours the ugliest man: sees"
"15218" 1998 "height in his self-contempt, and invites him to join the other higher"
"15219" 1998 "men in the cave."
"15220" 1998 ""
"15221" 1998 "Chapter LXVIII. The Voluntary Beggar."
"15222" 1998 ""
"15223" 1998 "In this discourse, we undoubtedly have the ideal Buddhist, if not"
"15224" 1998 "Gautama Buddha himself. Nietzsche had the greatest respect for Buddhism,"
"15225" 1998 "and almost wherever he refers to it in his works, it is in terms of"
"15226" 1998 "praise. He recognised that though Buddhism is undoubtedly a religion for"
"15227" 1998 "decadents, its decadent values emanate from the higher and not, as in"
"15228" 1998 "Christianity, from the lower grades of society. In Aphorism 20 of \"The"
"15229" 1998 "Antichrist\", he compares it exhaustively with Christianity, and"
"15230" 1998 "the result of his investigation is very much in favour of the older"
"15231" 1998 "religion. Still, he recognised a most decided Buddhistic influence"
"15232" 1998 "in Christ's teaching, and the words in verses 29, 30, and 31 are very"
"15233" 1998 "reminiscent of his views in regard to the Christian Savior."
"15234" 1998 ""
"15235" 1998 "The figure of Christ has been introduced often enough into fiction, and"
"15236" 1998 "many scholars have undertaken to write His life according to their own"
"15237" 1998 "lights, but few perhaps have ever attempted to present Him to us bereft"
"15238" 1998 "of all those characteristics which a lack of the sense of harmony has"
"15239" 1998 "attached to His person through the ages in which His doctrines have been"
"15240" 1998 "taught. Now Nietzsche disagreed entirely with Renan's view, that Christ"
"15241" 1998 "was \"le grand maitre en ironie\"; in Aphorism 31 of \"The Antichrist\","
"15242" 1998 "he says that he (Nietzsche) always purged his picture of the Humble"
"15243" 1998 "Nazarene of all those bitter and spiteful outbursts which, in view of"
"15244" 1998 "the struggle the first Christians went through, may very well have been"
"15245" 1998 "added to the original character by Apologists and Sectarians who, at"
"15246" 1998 "that time, could ill afford to consider nice psychological points,"
"15247" 1998 "seeing that what they needed, above all, was a wrangling and abusive"
"15248" 1998 "deity. These two conflicting halves in the character of the Christ of"
"15249" 1998 "the Gospels, which no sound psychology can ever reconcile, Nietzsche"
"15250" 1998 "always kept distinct in his own mind; he could not credit the same man"
"15251" 1998 "with sentiments sometimes so noble and at other times so vulgar, and"
"15252" 1998 "in presenting us with this new portrait of the Saviour, purged of all"
"15253" 1998 "impurities, Nietzsche rendered military honours to a foe, which far"
"15254" 1998 "exceed in worth all that His most ardent disciples have ever claimed for"
"15255" 1998 "Him. In verse 26 we are vividly reminded of Herbert Spencer's words \"'Le"
"15256" 1998 "mariage de convenance' is legalised prostitution.\""
"15257" 1998 ""
"15258" 1998 "Chapter LXIX. The Shadow."
"15259" 1998 ""
"15260" 1998 "Here we have a description of that courageous and wayward spirit that"
"15261" 1998 "literally haunts the footsteps of every great thinker and every great"
"15262" 1998 "leader; sometimes with the result that it loses all aims, all hopes,"
"15263" 1998 "and all trust in a definite goal. It is the case of the bravest and"
"15264" 1998 "most broad-minded men of to-day. These literally shadow the most daring"
"15265" 1998 "movements in the science and art of their generation; they completely"
"15266" 1998 "lose their bearings and actually find themselves, in the end, without a"
"15267" 1998 "way, a goal, or a home. \"On every surface have I already sat!...I become"
"15268" 1998 "thin, I am almost equal to a shadow!\" At last, in despair, such men"
"15269" 1998 "do indeed cry out: \"Nothing is true; all is permitted,\" and then they"
"15270" 1998 "become mere wreckage. \"Too much hath become clear unto me: now nothing"
"15271" 1998 "mattereth to me any more. Nothing liveth any longer that I love,--how"
"15272" 1998 "should I still love myself! Have I still a goal? Where is MY home?\""
"15273" 1998 "Zarathustra realises the danger threatening such a man. \"Thy danger is"
"15274" 1998 "not small, thou free spirit and wanderer,\" he says. \"Thou hast had a bad"
"15275" 1998 "day. See that a still worse evening doth not overtake thee!\" The danger"
"15276" 1998 "Zarathustra refers to is precisely this, that even a prison may seem a"
"15277" 1998 "blessing to such a man. At least the bars keep him in a place of rest;"
"15278" 1998 "a place of confinement, at its worst, is real. \"Beware lest in the end"
"15279" 1998 "a narrow faith capture thee,\" says Zarathustra, \"for now everything that"
"15280" 1998 "is narrow and fixed seduceth and tempteth thee.\""
"15281" 1998 ""
"15282" 1998 "Chapter LXX. Noontide."
"15283" 1998 ""
"15284" 1998 "At the noon of life Nietzsche said he entered the world; with him"
"15285" 1998 "man came of age. We are now held responsible for our actions; our old"
"15286" 1998 "guardians, the gods and demi-gods of our youth, the superstitions and"
"15287" 1998 "fears of our childhood, withdraw; the field lies open before us; we"
"15288" 1998 "lived through our morning with but one master--chance--; let us see to"
"15289" 1998 "it that we MAKE our afternoon our own (see Note XLIX., Part III.)."
"15290" 1998 ""
"15291" 1998 "Chapter LXXI. The Greeting."
"15292" 1998 ""
"15293" 1998 "Here I think I may claim that my contention in regard to the purpose and"
"15294" 1998 "aim of the whole of Nietzsche's philosophy (as stated at the beginning"
"15295" 1998 "of my Notes on Part IV.) is completely upheld. He fought for \"all who"
"15296" 1998 "do not want to live, unless they learn again to HOPE--unless THEY learn"
"15297" 1998 "(from him) the GREAT hope!\" Zarathustra's address to his guests shows"
"15298" 1998 "clearly enough how he wished to help them: \"I DO NOT TREAT MY WARRIORS"
"15299" 1998 "INDULGENTLY,\" he says: \"how then could ye be fit for MY warfare?\" He"
"15300" 1998 "rebukes and spurns them, no word of love comes from his lips. Elsewhere"
"15301" 1998 "he says a man should be a hard bed to his friend, thus alone can he be"
"15302" 1998 "of use to him. Nietzsche would be a hard bed to higher men. He would"
"15303" 1998 "make them harder; for, in order to be a law unto himself, man must"
"15304" 1998 "possess the requisite hardness. \"I wait for higher ones, stronger ones,"
"15305" 1998 "more triumphant ones, merrier ones, for such as are built squarely in"
"15306" 1998 "body and soul.\" He says in par. 6 of \"Higher Man\":--"
"15307" 1998 ""
"15308" 1998 "\"Ye higher men, think ye that I am here to put right what ye have put"
"15309" 1998 "wrong? Or that I wished henceforth to make snugger couches for you"
"15310" 1998 "sufferers? Or show you restless, miswandering, misclimbing ones new and"
"15311" 1998 "easier footpaths?\""
"15312" 1998 ""
"15313" 1998 "\"Nay! Nay! Three times nay! Always more, always better ones of your type"
"15314" 1998 "shall succumb--for ye shall always have it worse and harder.\""
"15315" 1998 ""
"15316" 1998 "Chapter LXXII. The Supper."
"15317" 1998 ""
"15318" 1998 "In the first seven verses of this discourse, I cannot help seeing"
"15319" 1998 "a gentle allusion to Schopenhauer's habits as a bon-vivant. For a"
"15320" 1998 "pessimist, be it remembered, Schopenhauer led quite an extraordinary"
"15321" 1998 "life. He ate well, loved well, played the flute well, and I believe he"
"15322" 1998 "smoked the best cigars. What follows is clear enough."
"15323" 1998 ""
"15324" 1998 "Chapter LXXIII. The Higher Man. Par. 1."
"15325" 1998 ""
"15326" 1998 "Nietzsche admits, here, that at one time he had thought of appealing to"
"15327" 1998 "the people, to the crowd in the market-place, but that he had ultimately"
"15328" 1998 "to abandon the task. He bids higher men depart from the market-place."
"15329" 1998 ""
"15330" 1998 "Par. 3."
"15331" 1998 ""
"15332" 1998 "Here we are told quite plainly what class of men actually owe all their"
"15333" 1998 "impulses and desires to the instinct of self-preservation. The struggle"
"15334" 1998 "for existence is indeed the only spur in the case of such people."
"15335" 1998 "To them it matters not in what shape or condition man be preserved,"
"15336" 1998 "provided only he survive. The transcendental maxim that \"Life per se is"
"15337" 1998 "precious\" is the ruling maxim here."
"15338" 1998 ""
"15339" 1998 "Par. 4."
"15340" 1998 ""
"15341" 1998 "In the Note on Chapter LVII. (end) I speak of Nietzsche's elevation of"
"15342" 1998 "the virtue, Courage, to the highest place among the virtues. Here he"
"15343" 1998 "tells higher men the class of courage he expects from them."
"15344" 1998 ""
"15345" 1998 "Pars. 5, 6."
"15346" 1998 ""
"15347" 1998 "These have already been referred to in the Notes on Chapters LVII. (end)"
"15348" 1998 "and LXXI."
"15349" 1998 ""
"15350" 1998 "Par. 7."
"15351" 1998 ""
"15352" 1998 "I suggest that the last verse in this paragraph strongly confirms the"
"15353" 1998 "view that Nietzsche's teaching was always meant by him to be esoteric"
"15354" 1998 "and for higher man alone."
"15355" 1998 ""
"15356" 1998 "Par. 9."
"15357" 1998 ""
"15358" 1998 "In the last verse, here, another shaft of light is thrown upon the"
"15359" 1998 "Immaculate Perception or so-called \"pure objectivity\" of the scientific"
"15360" 1998 "mind. \"Freedom from fever is still far from being knowledge.\" Where a"
"15361" 1998 "man's emotions cease to accompany him in his investigations, he is"
"15362" 1998 "not necessarily nearer the truth. Says Spencer, in the Preface to his"
"15363" 1998 "Autobiography:--\"In the genesis of a system of thought, the emotional"
"15364" 1998 "nature is a large factor: perhaps as large a factor as the intellectual"
"15365" 1998 "nature\" (see pages 134, 141 of Vol. I., \"Thoughts out of Season\")."
"15366" 1998 ""
"15367" 1998 "Pars. 10, 11."
"15368" 1998 ""
"15369" 1998 "When we approach Nietzsche's philosophy we must be prepared to be"
"15370" 1998 "independent thinkers; in fact, the greatest virtue of his works is"
"15371" 1998 "perhaps the subtlety with which they impose the obligation upon one"
"15372" 1998 "of thinking alone, of scoring off one's own bat, and of shifting"
"15373" 1998 "intellectually for oneself."
"15374" 1998 ""
"15375" 1998 "Par. 13."
"15376" 1998 ""
"15377" 1998 "\"I am a railing alongside the torrent; whoever is able to grasp me, may"
"15378" 1998 "grasp me! Your crutch, however, I am not.\" These two paragraphs are an"
"15379" 1998 "exhortation to higher men to become independent."
"15380" 1998 ""
"15381" 1998 "Par. 15."
"15382" 1998 ""
"15383" 1998 "Here Nietzsche perhaps exaggerates the importance of heredity. As,"
"15384" 1998 "however, the question is by no means one on which we are all agreed,"
"15385" 1998 "what he says is not without value."
"15386" 1998 ""
"15387" 1998 "A very important principle in Nietzsche's philosophy is enunciated in"
"15388" 1998 "the first verse of this paragraph. \"The higher its type, always the"
"15389" 1998 "seldomer doth a thing succeed\" (see page 82 of \"Beyond Good and Evil\")."
"15390" 1998 "Those who, like some political economists, talk in a business-like way"
"15391" 1998 "about the terrific waste of human life and energy, deliberately overlook"
"15392" 1998 "the fact that the waste most to be deplored usually occurs among"
"15393" 1998 "higher individuals. Economy was never precisely one of nature's leading"
"15394" 1998 "principles. All this sentimental wailing over the larger proportion"
"15395" 1998 "of failures than successes in human life, does not seem to take into"
"15396" 1998 "account the fact that it is the rarest thing on earth for a highly"
"15397" 1998 "organised being to attain to the fullest development and activity of all"
"15398" 1998 "its functions, simply because it is so highly organised. The blind Will"
"15399" 1998 "to Power in nature therefore stands in urgent need of direction by man."
"15400" 1998 ""
"15401" 1998 "Pars. 16, 17, 18, 19, 20."
"15402" 1998 ""
"15403" 1998 "These paragraphs deal with Nietzsche's protest against the democratic"
"15404" 1998 "seriousness (Pobelernst) of modern times. \"All good things laugh,\" he"
"15405" 1998 "says, and his final command to the higher men is, \"LEARN, I pray you--to"
"15406" 1998 "laugh.\" All that is GOOD, in Nietzsche's sense, is cheerful. To be able"
"15407" 1998 "to crack a joke about one's deepest feelings is the greatest test of"
"15408" 1998 "their value. The man who does not laugh, like the man who does not make"
"15409" 1998 "faces, is already a buffoon at heart."
"15410" 1998 ""
"15411" 1998 "\"What hath hitherto been the greatest sin here on earth? Was it not the"
"15412" 1998 "word of him who said: 'Woe unto them that laugh now!' Did he himself"
"15413" 1998 "find no cause for laughter on the earth? Then he sought badly. A child"
"15414" 1998 "even findeth cause for it.\""
"15415" 1998 ""
"15416" 1998 "Chapter LXXIV. The Song of Melancholy."
"15417" 1998 ""
"15418" 1998 "After his address to the higher men, Zarathustra goes out into the"
"15419" 1998 "open to recover himself. Meanwhile the magician (Wagner), seizing the"
"15420" 1998 "opportunity in order to draw them all into his net once more, sings the"
"15421" 1998 "Song of Melancholy."
"15422" 1998 ""
"15423" 1998 "Chapter LXXV. Science."
"15424" 1998 ""
"15425" 1998 "The only one to resist the \"melancholy voluptuousness\" of his art, is"
"15426" 1998 "the spiritually conscientious one--the scientific specialist of whom we"
"15427" 1998 "read in the discourse entitled \"The Leech\". He takes the harp from the"
"15428" 1998 "magician and cries for air, while reproving the musician in the style"
"15429" 1998 "of \"The Case of Wagner\". When the magician retaliates by saying that the"
"15430" 1998 "spiritually conscientious one could have understood little of his song,"
"15431" 1998 "the latter replies: \"Thou praisest me in that thou separatest me from"
"15432" 1998 "thyself.\" The speech of the scientific man to his fellow higher men is"
"15433" 1998 "well worth studying. By means of it, Nietzsche pays a high tribute to"
"15434" 1998 "the honesty of the true specialist, while, in representing him as the"
"15435" 1998 "only one who can resist the demoniacal influence of the magician's"
"15436" 1998 "music, he elevates him at a stroke, above all those present. Zarathustra"
"15437" 1998 "and the spiritually conscientious one join issue at the end on the"
"15438" 1998 "question of the proper place of \"fear\" in man's history, and Nietzsche"
"15439" 1998 "avails himself of the opportunity in order to restate his views"
"15440" 1998 "concerning the relation of courage to humanity. It is precisely because"
"15441" 1998 "courage has played the most important part in our development that"
"15442" 1998 "he would not see it vanish from among our virtues to-day. \"...courage"
"15443" 1998 "seemeth to me the entire primitive history of man.\""
"15444" 1998 ""
